Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
7 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Reductio Against the Necessity of Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that no scholar uses the logicians' definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio argument against the claim that definitions are necessary for conceptualization and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If definitions were needed for knowledge, we would know nothing—yet we know much.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his attack on the logicians' theory of definition. Here he presents a devastating reductio: if sound definitions were really prerequisite for any conceptualization, then since no such definitions have ever been established, people would be utterly ignorant. Since that is absurd, the logicians' claim must be false. Pay close attention to how he uses examples from grammar, jurisprudence, and philosophy to show that even experts cannot produce a single uncontested definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians hold that a proper definition (hadd) is necessary for genuine conceptualization (tasawwur). Ibn Taymiyya targets this very claim.
2
List the failed definitions Ibn Taymiyya cites
He lists definitions of 'human', 'sun', 'noun' (ism), and 'analogy' (qiyas) – all from different sciences – none of which are accepted on the logicians' own criteria.
These are examples from grammar, usul al-fiqh, philosophy, and medicine.
3
Understand the reductio structure
Ibn Taymiyya argues: If conceptualization depended on definitions, and no correct definition exists (as shown), then no conceptualization would occur. Since conceptualization is necessary for assent (tasdiq), no knowledge would exist. But clearly knowledge does exist. Therefore, the premise that definitions are necessary is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Assess the charge of sophistry
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' position leads to 'the greatest sophistry' (safsata) because it denies the obvious reality of human knowledge.
Sophistry here means promoting a false or absurd conclusion through flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : الرابع : أنه إلى الساعة لا يُعلم للناس حد مستقيم على أصلهم. بل أظهر الأشياء لا يعلم حد «الإنسان» وحده بـ «الحيوان الناطق عليه الاعتراضات المشهورة. وكذلك حد على أصلهم «الشمس»، وأمثال ذلك حتى إن النحاةَ لَمّا دخل متأخروهم في الحدود ذكروا لـ «الاسم بضعة وعشرين حداً، وكلها معتَرَض عليها على أصلهم، وقيل: إنهم ذكروا لـ «الاسم» سبعين حداً لم يصح منها شيء، كما ذكر ذلك ابنُ الأنباري المتأخر (۱) . والأصوليون ذكروا لـ «القياس» بضعة وعشرين حدّا، وكلها معترض على أصلهم . وعامة الحدود المذكورة في كتب الفلاسفة والأطباء والنحاة، والأصوليين، والمتكلمة معترضة على أصلهم ؛ وإن قيل بسلامة بعضها كان قليلاً، بل منتفياً. فلو كان تصور الأشياء موقوفاً على الحدود لم يكن [٦] إلى الساعة قد تَصَوَّر الناسُ شيئاً من هذه الأمور ؛ والتصديق موقوف على التصور، فإذا لم يحصل تصور لم يَحْصُل تصديق ؛ فلا يكون عند بني آدم علم في عامة علومهم . وهذا من أعظم السَّفْسَطة .
The fourth aspect: That up to now, no correct definition according to their principle is known to people. Rather, the most apparent things are not known: the definition of 'human' alone by 'rational animal' has well-known objections. Similarly, according to their principle, the definition of 'sun', and the like, until when the later grammarians entered into definitions, they mentioned for 'noun' twenty-some definitions, all of them objected to according to their principle, and it is said that they mentioned for 'noun' seventy definitions, none of which were valid, as mentioned by the later Ibn al-Anbari. And the usuliyyun mentioned for 'analogy' twenty-some definitions, all of them objected to according to their principle. And most definitions mentioned in the books of philosophers, physicians, grammarians, usuliyyun, and mutakallimun are objected to according to their principle; even if it is said some are sound, it is few, rather non-existent. So if conceptualization of things were dependent on definitions, people would not have conceptualized anything of these matters up to now; and assent is dependent on conceptualization, so if conceptualization does not occur, assent does not occur; so the sons of Adam would have no knowledge in most of their sciences. And this is of the greatest sophistry.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a critical attack by Ibn Taymiyya on the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) are necessary for conceptualization (tasawwur). He presents the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rabi‘). He states that no correct definition according to the logicians' own principles has been established. Even the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal' faces famous objections. He gives examples from grammarians (e.g., definitions of 'ism' or noun), from jurists (definitions of qiyas/analogy), and from philosophers and physicians. All are flawed on the logicians' own grounds. If a few are sound, they are rare. Then he argues by reductio: if conceptualization depended on definitions, people would never have conceptualized anything, and hence would have no knowledge at all. This would be sophistry (safsata). The argument is that since people do have knowledge, definitions cannot be necessary.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Ibn Taymiyya refers to the logicians' definition that captures the essence of a thing via genus and differentia. [Note: In the wider tradition, hadd can also mean legal punishment; here it is logical definition.]
تصور (tasawwur): Conceptualization, the mental grasp of a term's meaning without judgment. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya critiques the standard notion that hadd is prerequisite for tasawwur.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what would follow if conceptualization were dependent on the logicians' definitions?
A) People would have more precise knowledge.
B) People would have no knowledge at all.
C) Only philosophers would have knowledge.
D) Definitions would be easier to formulate.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses a reductio to show that the necessity of definitions for knowledge is false – because its implication that we have no knowledge contradicts reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition of 'salah' (prayer) from a fiqh manual and compare it to the logicians' genus-differentia structure. Does the definition meet the logicians' criteria? Does its shortcoming affect the jurist's ability to discuss rulings on prayer?
?
Construct your own reductio: Assume that no valid definition of 'kalam' (speech) exists according to the logicians. Then argue that this does not prevent theologians from using the term. Use Ibn Taymiyya's method.

The Difference Between 'Adam al-'Aks and 'Adam al-Ta'thir
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logical concepts of 'adam al-'aks and 'adam al-ta'thir as invalidating causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'lack of converse' and 'lack of effect' and identify which one invalidates a cause according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Lack of converse alone does not invalidate a cause; only lack of effect does.

Ibn Taymiyya draws a critical distinction between two ways a cause might be challenged: the absence of its converse ('adam al-'aks) and the absence of its effect ('adam al-ta'thir). This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the former is insufficient to invalidate a cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts in the Arabic
Find the terms 'adam al-'aks and 'adam al-ta'thir in the text. Notice that Ibn Taymiyya introduces them together as two ways a cause is challenged.
They appear early in the passage.
2
Understand the logical principle of converse
For a cause (illa) to be valid, its presence should necessitate the judgment, and the judgment's absence should necessitate the cause's absence. 'Adam al-'aks means the judgment can occur without the cause, but that does not automatically invalidate the cause.
3
Check the condition for invalidation
Ibn Taymiyya states that invalidation requires 'adam al-ta'thir: the judgment occurs without the cause and no other cause substitutes. Find the Arabic sentence: 'فمجرد عدم الانعكاس لا يدل على فساد العلة إلا إذا وجد الحكم بدون العلة من غير أن تَخْلُفَها علة أخرى.'
This is the key criterion.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
In the passage, blood permissibility has multiple causes (apostasy, adultery, killing). The fact that one cause is absent does not invalidate the others because the judgment can arise from a different cause. This shows 'adam al-'aks is not fatal.
Each cause produces a distinct type of permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علة فيسمى «عدم عكس» و «عدم [١٠] تأثير»، ونفس الحكم المتعلق بهـا ينتفي لانتفائها، ولا يجب انتفاء نظيره إذا كان له علة أخرى. فمجرد عدم الانعكاس لا يدل على فساد العلة إلا إذا وجد الحكم بدون العلة من غير أن تَخْلُفَها علة أخرى. وهو في الحقيقة وجود نظير الشخص، وهو نوعه . فهنا تكون العلة عديمة التأثير، فتكون باطلة . وبهذا يظهر أن عدم التأثير مبطل لها، وعدم الانعكاس ليس مبطلاً لها . فإنها إذا انتفت انتفى الحكم المعلق بها، وإذا وجد الحكم بعلة أخرى، فإن كانت العلة مساوية لها من كل وجه فهى هي، وإن كانت مجانبة لها في بعض الـ . فلا بد أن يختلف [الحكم]، كما أن حلّ الدم كان اسم جنس، فالحل الحاصل بالرِدَّة نوع غير النوع الحاصل بالزّنا والحل الحاصل بالقتل. فإن حِلَّ الدم الحاصل [بالقتل] يجوز فيه الفداء والمعافاة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالرّدة [ يجوز] فيه الغُفْران بالتوبة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالزنا فيه حد الرجم بالحجارة وبحضور شهود وكثير نظائر هذا. فإلى ... في أحد الحِلَّين إن كان مماثلاً للآخر فالعلتان [واحدة]، وإن اختلف الحكم اختلفت [العلة]. وانتقاض الوضوء بالبول والغائط نوع واحد ليس هو من باب تغاير الحكم بعلتين . . . كالقتل بالحدود. قال المعترض : هذه الأوصاف لا تأثير لها في حِلّ الدّم، فإن الردّة و زنا المحصن ... وحراب الكافر الأصلي يبيح الدّم مع انتفاء هذه الأوصاف . فيقال له : فإن الحلّ وُجد لسبب آخر لا لعدم تأثير هذه الأوصاف، كما يستحق الابن الإرث بالنسب والنكاح والولاء ويثبتُ المِلْك بالمعاوضة والإرث والإيهاب والاغتنام. وتلك المباحات لو كان للمستدلّ ... فيجب القود قياساً على القتل لم يرد عليه هذ السؤال، لأن الواجب بالردّة والزنا ليس قودا . وأما عدم التأثير مبطل لما هو العلة، لأن تأثير الدّلالة الذي يجمع فيه لا يدلّ على العلة، فإن الدليل لا يجب انعكاسه، كما لو قال من يركب القياس في مسألة زكاة الصبي : ملبوس، فلا يجب له الزكاة كلباس الكبير . قيل له : لا تأثير لكونه لباساً، لا في الفرع ولا في الأصل، بل هذا الحكم ثابت عند أهل الشرع في جميع مال الصغير. وهذه كلمات جوامع في هذا الكليات التي يكثر فيها اضطراب الناس .
A cause is called 'lacking converse' and 'lacking effect', and the judgment attached to it is negated by its negation, but the negation of its analogue is not necessary if it has another cause. Mere lack of converse does not indicate corruption of the cause unless the judgment occurs without the cause and no other cause substitutes for it. This is in reality the existence of the analogue of the individual, which is its species. Here the cause is without effect, so it is invalid. By this it is shown that lack of effect invalidates it, and lack of converse does not invalidate it. For if the cause is negated, the judgment attached to it is negated; and if the judgment occurs with another cause, then if that cause is equivalent in every respect, it is the same; if it is different in some respect, then the judgment must differ. For example, the permissibility of blood is a generic term: the permissibility obtained by apostasy is a type different from that obtained by adultery and that obtained by killing. For the permissibility of blood obtained by killing may allow ransom and pardon, the permissibility obtained by apostasy allows forgiveness through repentance, and the permissibility obtained by adultery involves punishment of stoning with stones and witnesses, and many similar examples. So if one of two permissions is similar to the other, then the causes are one; if the judgment differs, the cause differs. The invalidation of ablution by urine and feces is one type, not a case of differing judgments with two causes... like killing by prescribed punishments. The objector said: These descriptions have no effect on the permissibility of blood, for apostasy and adultery of the married and brigandage of the original disbeliever permit blood despite the absence of these descriptions. So it is said to him: The permission exists due to another cause, not due to the lack of effect of these descriptions, just as a son inherits by lineage, marriage, and clientage, and ownership is established by exchange, inheritance, gift, and booty. And if the proponent had said... that retaliation is obligatory by analogy with killing, this question would not have been raised, because what is obligatory by apostasy and adultery is not retaliation. As for lack of effect, it invalidates the cause, because the effect of the evidence that includes it does not indicate the cause, for evidence is not necessarily convertible. For example, if someone uses analogy in the issue of the zakah of a minor's garment, saying: It is worn, so zakah is not obligatory for it, just as with the garment of an adult. It is said to him: The fact that it is worn has no effect, neither in the branch nor in the original; rather, this ruling is established by the people of the Shariah for all the property of a minor. These are comprehensive statements on these universals in which people often become confused.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logical doctrine that lack of converse ('adam al-'aks) invalidates a cause. Ibn Taymiyya begins by distinguishing between 'lack of converse' and 'lack of effect' ('adam al-ta'thir). He argues that the mere non-occurrence of the converse does not prove the cause's invalidity; rather, one must check whether the judgment occurs without the cause and with no other cause replacing it. Only then is the cause truly ineffective. The author supports this with examples from Islamic law: different causes for the permissibility of blood (e.g., apostasy, adultery, killing) produce different types of permission, each with distinct consequences. Similarly, inheritance and ownership can arise from multiple causes without invalidating any. The objector's claim that certain descriptions lack effect is countered by noting that the judgment exists due to another cause, not despite those descriptions. The passage concludes with an example from zakah on a minor's clothing, showing that the ruling depends on the cause (ownership, not the act of wearing). Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that these distinctions are often misunderstood, and the logical principle of 'adam al-'aks is insufficient to invalidate a cause.

Key Terms
علة
عدم عكس
عدم تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what condition must be met for 'adam al-ta'thir to invalidate a cause?
A) The judgment must be absent when the cause is absent, and no other cause substitutes for it.
B) The converse of the cause must be absent.
C) The cause's effect must be absent in all cases.
D) The cause must conflict with another known cause.

Key Takeaway
'Adam al-'aks' (lack of converse) is not a sufficient reason to invalidate a cause; only 'adam al-ta'thir' (lack of effect) is, because a judgment may have multiple independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a contemporary example: In Islamic finance, the prohibition of riba is attributed to the cause of 'increase without compensation'. If a transaction involves increase but is permitted due to another cause (e.g., sale), does that invalidate the cause? Using Ibn Taymiyya's framework, determine whether this is 'adam al-'aks or 'adam al-ta'thir.

Sophistry and Fallacies
advanced
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logical Principle of 'Adam al-'Aks
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logical concepts of 'adam al-'aks and 'adam al-ta'thir as invalidating causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against using 'adam al-'aks to invalidate causes, and apply it to legal examples.

Core Idea
A cause is not invalidated simply because its converse fails; the judgment may have another cause.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the standard logical principle that if the cause is absent but the judgment still appears, the cause is invalid. He argues that this only holds if no other cause exists. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
The standard logical claim is that 'adam al-'aks (lack of converse) invalidates a cause. Find where Ibn Taymiyya introduces this as a common challenge.
The passage begins with 'علة فيسمى «عدم عكس»'.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument
He distinguishes between the judgment being absent when the cause is absent (which is true) and the judgment being present when the cause is absent (which might be due to another cause). The key sentence: 'فمجرد عدم الانعكاس لا يدل على فساد العلة إلا إذا وجد الحكم بدون العلة من غير أن تَخْلُفَها علة أخرى.'
3
Analyze the legal examples
Blood permissibility by apostasy, adultery, and killing: each has distinct consequences (ransom, forgiveness, stoning). This shows different causes produce different types of permission.
The judgment is the same generic 'permissibility', but the specific rulings differ.
4
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector says descriptions like 'apostate' have no effect because blood is permissible from other causes. Ibn Taymiyya responds that the judgment exists due to another cause, not due to the ineffectiveness of the description.
5
Apply to the zakah example
A minor's garment is not subject to zakah. An objector might say 'being worn' has no effect because the minor's garment is exempt. Ibn Taymiyya replies that the ruling is based on ownership, not being worn. The cause for exemption is ownership of a minor's property, not the act of wearing.
This illustrates that the apparent lack of effect is not 'adam al-ta'thir because another cause exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علة فيسمى «عدم عكس» و «عدم [١٠] تأثير»، ونفس الحكم المتعلق بهـا ينتفي لانتفائها، ولا يجب انتفاء نظيره إذا كان له علة أخرى. فمجرد عدم الانعكاس لا يدل على فساد العلة إلا إذا وجد الحكم بدون العلة من غير أن تَخْلُفَها علة أخرى. وهو في الحقيقة وجود نظير الشخص، وهو نوعه . فهنا تكون العلة عديمة التأثير، فتكون باطلة . وبهذا يظهر أن عدم التأثير مبطل لها، وعدم الانعكاس ليس مبطلاً لها . فإنها إذا انتفت انتفى الحكم المعلق بها، وإذا وجد الحكم بعلة أخرى، فإن كانت العلة مساوية لها من كل وجه فهى هي، وإن كانت مجانبة لها في بعض الـ . فلا بد أن يختلف [الحكم]، كما أن حلّ الدم كان اسم جنس، فالحل الحاصل بالرِدَّة نوع غير النوع الحاصل بالزّنا والحل الحاصل بالقتل. فإن حِلَّ الدم الحاصل [بالقتل] يجوز فيه الفداء والمعافاة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالرّدة [ يجوز] فيه الغُفْران بالتوبة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالزنا فيه حد الرجم بالحجارة وبحضور شهود وكثير نظائر هذا. فإلى ... في أحد الحِلَّين إن كان مماثلاً للآخر فالعلتان [واحدة]، وإن اختلف الحكم اختلفت [العلة]. وانتقاض الوضوء بالبول والغائط نوع واحد ليس هو من باب تغاير الحكم بعلتين . . . كالقتل بالحدود. قال المعترض : هذه الأوصاف لا تأثير لها في حِلّ الدّم، فإن الردّة و زنا المحصن ... وحراب الكافر الأصلي يبيح الدّم مع انتفاء هذه الأوصاف . فيقال له : فإن الحلّ وُجد لسبب آخر لا لعدم تأثير هذه الأوصاف، كما يستحق الابن الإرث بالنسب والنكاح والولاء ويثبتُ المِلْك بالمعاوضة والإرث والإيهاب والاغتنام. وتلك المباحات لو كان للمستدلّ ... فيجب القود قياساً على القتل لم يرد عليه هذ السؤال، لأن الواجب بالردّة والزنا ليس قودا . وأما عدم التأثير مبطل لما هو العلة، لأن تأثير الدّلالة الذي يجمع فيه لا يدلّ على العلة، فإن الدليل لا يجب انعكاسه، كما لو قال من يركب القياس في مسألة زكاة الصبي : ملبوس، فلا يجب له الزكاة كلباس الكبير . قيل له : لا تأثير لكونه لباساً، لا في الفرع ولا في الأصل، بل هذا الحكم ثابت عند أهل الشرع في جميع مال الصغير. وهذه كلمات جوامع في هذا الكليات التي يكثر فيها اضطراب الناس .
A cause is called 'lacking converse' and 'lacking effect', and the judgment attached to it is negated by its negation, but the negation of its analogue is not necessary if it has another cause. Mere lack of converse does not indicate corruption of the cause unless the judgment occurs without the cause and no other cause substitutes for it. This is in reality the existence of the analogue of the individual, which is its species. Here the cause is without effect, so it is invalid. By this it is shown that lack of effect invalidates it, and lack of converse does not invalidate it. For if the cause is negated, the judgment attached to it is negated; and if the judgment occurs with another cause, then if that cause is equivalent in every respect, it is the same; if it is different in some respect, then the judgment must differ. For example, the permissibility of blood is a generic term: the permissibility obtained by apostasy is a type different from that obtained by adultery and that obtained by killing. For the permissibility of blood obtained by killing may allow ransom and pardon, the permissibility obtained by apostasy allows forgiveness through repentance, and the permissibility obtained by adultery involves punishment of stoning with stones and witnesses, and many similar examples. So if one of two permissions is similar to the other, then the causes are one; if the judgment differs, the cause differs. The invalidation of ablution by urine and feces is one type, not a case of differing judgments with two causes... like killing by prescribed punishments. The objector said: These descriptions have no effect on the permissibility of blood, for apostasy and adultery of the married and brigandage of the original disbeliever permit blood despite the absence of these descriptions. So it is said to him: The permission exists due to another cause, not due to the lack of effect of these descriptions, just as a son inherits by lineage, marriage, and clientage, and ownership is established by exchange, inheritance, gift, and booty. And if the proponent had said... that retaliation is obligatory by analogy with killing, this question would not have been raised, because what is obligatory by apostasy and adultery is not retaliation. As for lack of effect, it invalidates the cause, because the effect of the evidence that includes it does not indicate the cause, for evidence is not necessarily convertible. For example, if someone uses analogy in the issue of the zakah of a minor's garment, saying: It is worn, so zakah is not obligatory for it, just as with the garment of an adult. It is said to him: The fact that it is worn has no effect, neither in the branch nor in the original; rather, this ruling is established by the people of the Shariah for all the property of a minor. These are comprehensive statements on these universals in which people often become confused.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logical doctrine that lack of converse ('adam al-'aks) invalidates a cause. Ibn Taymiyya begins by distinguishing between 'lack of converse' and 'lack of effect' ('adam al-ta'thir). He argues that the mere non-occurrence of the converse does not prove the cause's invalidity; rather, one must check whether the judgment occurs without the cause and with no other cause replacing it. Only then is the cause truly ineffective. The author supports this with examples from Islamic law: different causes for the permissibility of blood (e.g., apostasy, adultery, killing) produce different types of permission, each with distinct consequences. Similarly, inheritance and ownership can arise from multiple causes without invalidating any. The objector's claim that certain descriptions lack effect is countered by noting that the judgment exists due to another cause, not despite those descriptions. The passage concludes with an example from zakah on a minor's clothing, showing that the ruling depends on the cause (ownership, not the act of wearing). Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that these distinctions are often misunderstood, and the logical principle of 'adam al-'aks is insufficient to invalidate a cause.

Key Terms
علة
عدم عكس
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Ibn Taymiyya's example of blood permissibility, why does the fact that apostasy permits blood not invalidate the cause of adultery permissibility?
A) Because apostasy and adultery are the same cause.
B) Because the two permissions are of different types and have distinct consequences.
C) Because the objector misunderstood the definition of blood.
D) Because adultery permissibility does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
A cause should only be invalidated if the judgment occurs without it and no other cause can account for it; the mere presence of an alternative cause does not negate the original cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the case of zakat on a minor's property. A minor's garment does not require zakat, but an adult's garment also does not require zakat. An objector might say: 'The cause of exemption is being worn, but it fails because the minor's garment is exempt even if not worn?' Using Ibn Taymiyya's method, explain why this objection fails.

The Origin of Logic in Greek Polytheism
Ibn Taymiyya's genealogy of Greek logic from geometry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace Ibn Taymiyya's historical account of how Greek logic originated from geometry and its connection to idolatrous planetary worship.

Core Idea
Logic’s origins in geometry are tied to polytheistic star-worship.

Ibn Taymiyya does not simply reject logic; he provides a historical account of its development among the Greeks. In this lesson, we examine his narrative linking geometry, planetary worship, and the formation of logical terminology. Understanding this genealogy helps us evaluate the epistemological weight of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of geometry among the Greeks
According to Ibn Taymiyya, the Greeks observed planets for their polytheistic rituals. They needed geometry to calculate planetary motions and build temples.
This shows geometry was not pursued for abstract knowledge but for idolatry.
2
Trace the connection between planetary observation and geometry
The celestial spheres are circular, so calculating their magnitudes and movements required knowledge of curved and straight lines—i.e., geometry.
3
Explain how logical 'forms' and 'definitions' were modeled on geometry
Ibn Taymiyya states that the Greeks made logic into 'shapes' (ashkāl) like geometric shapes, and called them 'definitions' (hudūd) analogous to geometric boundaries, to move from sensible to intelligible forms.
This reflects an attempt to replicate geometric certainty in reasoning.
4
Recognize the implicit critique
Ibn Taymiyya implies that this indirect method (sensible → intelligible) is due to weak intellects, because knowledge required a far-fetched path. In contrast, Allah facilitated direct knowledge for Muslims.
The critique is historical and epistemological, not a rejection of all logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الرابع : مبدأ وضع «المنطق» من «الهندسة»

أصل مشروع ، فتبقى فيه رياضة العقل وحفظ الشرع . لكن ليس هو علماً يُطلب لذاته، ولا تكمل به النفس .

وأولئك المشركون كانوا يعبدون الكواكب، ويبنون لها الهَياكِلَ، ويدعونها بأنواع الاستعانة على معرفة الدعوات، كما هو معروف من أخبارهم وما صُنّف على طريقهم من الكتب الموضوعة في الشرك، والسحر، ودعوة الكواكب، والعزائم والأقسام التي بها يعظم إبليس وجنوده. وكان الشيطان بسبب السحر والشرك يُغويهم بأشياء هي التي دعتهم إلى ذلك الشرك والسحر. فكانوا يرصدون الكواكب ليتعلموا مقاديرها ومقادير حركاتها، وما بين بعضها وبعض من الاتصالات، ليستعينوا بذلك على ما يرونه مناسباً لها .

الكواكب

الأفلاك

ولما كانت الأفلاك مستديرة، ولم يكن معرفةُ حِسابها إلا بمعرفة «الهندسة» معرفة وأحكام الخطوط المنحنية والمستقيمة، تكلموا في الهندسة» لذلك، ولعمارة الدنيا . وعمارة الدنيا فلهذا صاروا يتوسعون في ذلك . وإلا فلو لم يتعلق بذلك غرض إلا مجرد تصور الأعداد والمقادير : لم تكن هذه الغاية مما يوجب طلبها بالسعي المذكور.

وربما كانت هذه غايةٌ لبعض الناس الذين يتلذذون بذلك؛ فإن لذات النفوس أنواع. ومنهم من يلتذ بالشَّطْرَنج، والنَّرْد ،والقمار ، حتى يشغله ذلك عما هو أنفع له منه

فكان مبدأ وضع المنطق» من «الهندسة». فجعلوه أشكالاً كالأشكال الهندسية، وسموه حدوداً كحدود تلك الأشكال، لينتقلوا من الشكل المحسوس إلى الشكل المعقول . وهذا لضعف عقولهم وتعذر المعرفة عليهم إلا بالطريق البعيدة .

والله تعالى قد يسّر للمسلمين من العلم والبيان مع العمل الصالح والإيمان، ما برَّزُوا به على كل نوع من أنواع جنس الإنسان، والحمد لله رب العالمين.
Third Station - Fourth Aspect: The origin of the discipline of logic from geometry. A permissible foundation, so the exercise of the mind and the preservation of the Law remain in it. But it is not a science pursued for its own sake, nor does the soul attain perfection through it. Those polytheists used to worship the planets, build temples for them, and invoke them with various means of seeking help for knowledge of prayers, as is known from their reports and books composed on their path about polytheism, sorcery, planetary invocation, and spells and oaths by which Iblis and his hosts are magnified. Satan, through sorcery and polytheism, would mislead them with things that called them to that polytheism and sorcery. They would observe the planets to learn their magnitudes, the magnitudes of their movements, and the connections between them, to seek help in that for what they deemed appropriate for them. The planets, the spheres. And since the spheres were circular, and their calculation was only known through knowledge of geometry and the rulings of curved and straight lines, they spoke about geometry for that reason, and for the construction of the world. The construction of the world — thus they expanded in that. Otherwise, if there were no purpose attached to that except mere conception of numbers and magnitudes, this end would not necessitate seeking it with the mentioned effort. And perhaps this was an end for some people who take pleasure in that; for the pleasures of souls are of various types. Some take pleasure in chess, backgammon, and gambling, until it distracts them from what is more beneficial for them. Thus the origin of the discipline of logic was from geometry. They made it into forms like geometric forms, and called them definitions like the definitions of those forms, to move from the sensible form to the intelligible form. And this is due to the weakness of their intellects and the difficulty of knowledge for them except through a far-fetched path. And Allah the Exalted has facilitated for the Muslims knowledge and clear exposition with righteous action and faith, by which they excelled over every type of the human race, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the origins of Greek logic. He begins by stating that the subject of logic is a permissible foundation, serving mental exercise and preservation of the Law, but it is not a science sought for itself nor does it perfect the soul. He then provides a historical account: the polytheists worshiped planets and built temples for them, using magical invocations. They observed the planets' magnitudes and motions to aid their polytheistic practices. Since the celestial spheres are circular, calculating them required geometry, so they studied geometry for that purpose and for worldly construction. Had it been only for abstract numbers and magnitudes, it would not justify such effort. Some may take pleasure in such pursuits, like chess or gambling. The key point: the Greeks derived logic from geometry, making logical 'forms' analogous to geometric shapes and 'definitions' like geometric boundaries, in order to move from sensible to intelligible forms. Ibn Taymiyya criticizes this as a sign of weak intellects, because they required a long indirect path. In contrast, Allah facilitated for Muslims direct knowledge with clear exposition and righteous deeds.

Key Terms
المنطق
الهندسة
حدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why did the Greeks need geometry?
A) To calculate planetary movements for their polytheistic worship and for worldly construction.
B) To prove the existence of God.
C) To understand Islamic law.
D) For pure mathematical pleasure without any practical purpose.

Key Takeaway
The Greeks derived logic from geometry to serve their polytheistic astronomy and compensate for weak intellects, whereas Muslims were granted direct knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logic's Epistemic Status
Ibn Taymiyya's critical assessment of the origin and value of Greek logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and articulate Ibn Taymiyya's objections to Greek logic based on its origin and its epistemological limitations.

Core Idea
Logic is permissible but not praiseworthy; its Greek pedigree taints its universal validity.

Ibn Taymiyya makes a crucial distinction: logic is a permissible tool but not a science sought for itself. In this lesson, we unpack his critique that the Greek method is a sign of weak intellects, and contrast it with the Islamic path of direct knowledge through revelation and righteous action.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's statement that logic is 'a permissible foundation' (أصل مشروع)
Find this phrase at the beginning of the passage. Note that he does not forbid logic entirely; he allows it for mental exercise and preserving the Law.
This nuance is important: it is not a blanket rejection.
2
Identify his core criticism: the Greeks derived logic from geometry due to weak intellects
Ibn Taymiyya says they made logic into geometric forms and definitions 'to move from the sensible form to the intelligible form. This is due to the weakness of their intellects.'
The criticism is that they needed a sensible crutch for abstract reasoning.
3
Understand the alternative path for Muslims
Allah facilitated for Muslims knowledge and clear exposition with righteous action and faith, making them excel over others. This implies a direct, revelation-based epistemology.
4
Evaluate the strength of the critique
Does Ibn Taymiyya's historical narrative logically invalidate the use of logic? Consider if the origin of a method determines its validity. His critique is historical and epistemological, not a formal refutation of logical rules.
Later scholars debated this; some accepted logic as a neutral tool.

Sophistry and Fallacies

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Necessity of Syllogism for Knowing God
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that all theoretical knowledge requires demonstrative syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that demonstrative syllogism is neither necessary nor sufficient for knowledge of God, and identify his alternative Quranic method of qiyas al-awla.

Core Idea
The logicians' syllogism is not the only path to knowing God; the Quran's qiyas al-awla and God's signs suffice.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks the core claim of the logicians (especially Ibn Sina) that all theoretical knowledge, including knowledge of God, requires Aristotelian syllogism with a middle term. He argues that this claim is false and that the Quran uses a different, more effective reasoning method: qiyas al-awla (a fortiori argument). This lesson unpacks his critique and shows why he considers the logicians' claim to be both philosophically flawed and theologically dangerous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Locate the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: that theoretical sciences are not obtained except by means of demonstrative syllogism, and even God's knowledge requires a syllogism with a middle term.
The passage states their claim as: 'إن العلوم النظرية لا تحصل إلا بواسطة برهانهم' and that God's and prophets' knowledge requires syllogism.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's first objection: Quranic reasoning is qiyas al-awla
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Quran's demonstrative proofs are of the type 'qiyas al-awla' (argument from the more fitting), not categorical syllogism. This involves graded terms (mushtakka) rather than univocal terms.
Qiyas al-awla is a form of a fortiori argument, e.g., 'If God can do X, He can do something greater.'
3
Extract his second objection: God's signs entail His existence directly
Ibn Taymiyya says that the special attributes of the Lord are indicated by His 'ayat' (signs) which necessarily entail His existence. No syllogism—demonstrative or otherwise—can point to these.
This is a direct epistemic claim: the universe and revelation provide immediate evidence of God without needing a middle term.
4
Understand his overall verdict on the logicians' syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' syllogism fails to achieve the true goal (perfection of the soul in knowing God and existents). Their claim is a false negative universal that restricts knowledge to a narrow path.
He calls their syllogism 'a narrow path that yields nothing but a worthless desired object.'
5
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ibn Taymiyya equates the logicians' position with that of Pharaoh and those who dispute God's signs arrogantly. He cites Quranic verses (Ghafir 56, 35, 83-85) to condemn their attitude.
The verses warn against arguing God's signs without authority and the fate of those who reject messengers only to repent too late.

ولا بد من إثبات التفاضل، وهو مدلول المشككة التي هي قسيم «المتواطئة الخاصة». وذلك هو مدلول الأقيسة البرهانية القرآنية، وهي قياس الأولى». ولا بد من إثبات خاصة الرب التي بها يتميّز عما سواه، وذلك مدلول «آياته سبحانه التي يستلزم ثبوتها ثبوت نفسه، لا يدل على هذه «قياس»، لا «برهاني» ولا «غير برهاني». فتبين بذلك إن قياسهم البرهاني لا يحصِّلُ المطلوب الذي به تكمل النفس في معرفة «الموجودات» ومعرفة خالقها، يذكر ذلك ابن سينا وأتباعه . وهم في إثبات ذلك خير ممن نفى عِلمه وعِلمَ أنبيائه من سلفهم الذين هم من أجهل الناس برب العالمين، وبأنبيائه، وبكتبه. فابن سينا لَمَّا تميز عن أولئك بمزيد عقل وعلم سلك طريقهم المنطقي في تقرير ذلك. وصاروا سالكوا هذه الطريق، وإن كانوا أعلم من سلفهم وأكمل، فهم أضلُّ من اليهود والنصارى وأجهلُ ، إذ كان أولئك حَصَل لهم من الإيمان بـ «واجب الوجود» وصفاته ما لم يحصل لهؤلاء الضلال، بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَاهُمْ إِن فِي صُدُورِهِمْ إِلَّا كِبْرُ مَاهُم بِبَلِغِيهِ ﴾ [سورة غافر : ٥٦]. وقال : ﴿ الَّذِينَ يُجَدِلُونَ فِي ءَايَتِ اللَّهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَاهُمْ كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِندَ اللَّهِ وَعِندَ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كَذَلِكَ يَطْبَعُ اللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ قَلْبِ مُتَكَبِّرٍ جَبَّارٍ ﴾ [سورة غافر : 35] وقال: ﴿ فَلَمَّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُم بِالْبَيِّنَتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِندَهُم مِّنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَاقَ بِهِم مَّا كَانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ فَلَمَّا رَأَوْا بَأْسَنَا قَالُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ وَحْدَهُ وَكَفَرْنَا بِمَا كُنَّا بِهِ مُشْرِكِينَ فَلَمْ يَكُ يَنفَعُهُمْ إِيمَانُهُمْ لَمَّا رَأَوْا بَأْسَنَا سُنَّتَ اللَّهِ الَّتِي قَدْ خَلَتْ فِي عِبَادِهِ وَخَسِرَ هُنَالِكَ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ [سورة غافر : 83-85] .
And there is no escape from proving superiority, and it is what is indicated by the graded (mushtakka) which is the counterpart of 'special univocal' (mutawati'a al-khassa). That is what is indicated by the demonstrative syllogisms of the Qur'an, and they are the argument from the more fitting (qiyas al-awla). And there is no escape from proving the attribute of the Lord by which He is distinguished from other than Him, and that is what is indicated by 'His verses (ayat) that entail His own existence, no syllogism indicates this, neither 'demonstrative' nor 'non-demonstrative'. So it became clear by that that their demonstrative syllogism does not achieve the desired goal by which the soul is perfected in knowing 'existents' and knowing their Creator, let alone it being said: 'Theoretical matters are not known except through it.' And this is a broad topic, but the intention in this place is to alert to the falsity of their negative proposition, which is their statement: 'Theoretical sciences are not obtained except by means of their demonstration.' Then their general negation which they made narrow what was broad was not enough for them, and they confined sciences to a narrow path that yields nothing but a worthless desired object, until they claimed that God's knowledge and the knowledge of His prophets and saints is only obtained by means of 'syllogism' containing the middle term, as Ibn Sina and his followers mention. And they in proving that are better than those who denied His knowledge and the knowledge of His prophets among their predecessors who were the most ignorant of people about the Lord of the worlds, and about His prophets, and about His books. So when Ibn Sina distinguished himself from those with increased reason and knowledge, he followed their logical path in establishing that. And they became followers of this path, and even though they were more knowledgeable and more perfect than their predecessors, they are more misguided than the Jews and Christians and more ignorant, since those had obtained faith in the 'Necessary Existent' and His attributes what these misguided ones did not obtain, because of what is in their breasts of arrogance and fantasy. And they are followers of Pharaoh and his like; and for this you find them belittling or hostile to Moses and those with him from the people of religions and laws. God Almighty said: 'Indeed, those who dispute concerning the signs of God without any authority having come to them, there is in their breasts only arrogance, which they will never attain' [Surah Ghafir: 56]. And He said: 'Those who dispute concerning the signs of God without any authority having come to them, it is greatly hated in the sight of God and in the sight of those who believe. Thus does God seal every heart of a proud tyrant.' [Surah Ghafir: 35] And He said: 'And when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and they were surrounded by what they used to mock. And when they saw Our punishment, they said: We have believed in God alone and disbelieved in what we used to associate with Him. But their faith did not benefit them when they saw Our punishment, the established practice of God which has passed among His servants, and there the disbelievers lost out.' [Surah Ghafir: 83-85]
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians, particularly Ibn Sina and his followers, regarding the necessity of demonstrative syllogism for attaining knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya opens by establishing that there is a need to prove superiority (tafadul), which is denoted by the concept of 'graded' (mushtakka) terms—those that apply to things in varying degrees (like existence or goodness)—as opposed to 'univocal' (mutawati'a) terms that apply equally. He asserts that this graded meaning is what the Quranic demonstrative proofs, specifically the 'argument from the more fitting' (qiyas al-awla, akin to a fortiori reasoning), convey. Then he argues that the special attribute of the Lord distinguishing Him from other things is indicated by His signs (ayat) in the universe and revelation, which necessarily entail His existence. Such signs, he says, are not proven by any syllogism, demonstrative or otherwise. Hence, the logicians' demonstrative syllogism fails to achieve the true goal of perfecting the soul in knowing existents and their Creator, even more so their claim that no theoretical knowledge can be obtained except through their syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya then critiques their sweeping negation that restricts all theoretical sciences to their narrow method. They go further to claim that even God's knowledge and the knowledge of prophets and saints is obtained via syllogism with a middle term, a view attributed to Ibn Sina. Ibn Taymiyya contrasts these logicians with their predecessors who outright denied God's knowledge, saying Ibn Sina et al. are slightly better but more misguided than Jews and Christians, because the latter had some faith in the Necessary Existent and His attributes. The logicians' arrogance leads them to belittle Moses and the people of religion, as Ibn Taymiyya substantiates with Quranic verses condemning those who dispute God's signs arrogantly. The passage concludes with verses about the disbelievers who rejected the messengers only to repent when punishment came, to no avail.

Key Terms
المشككة
المتواطئة الخاصة
قياس الأولى
الحد الأوسط
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Ibn Taymiyya, what type of reasoning does the Quran use to demonstrate God's attributes?
A) Categorical syllogism with a middle term
B) Qiyas al-awla (argument from the more fitting)
C) Inductive enumeration
D) Analogical reasoning (tamthil)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' claim that knowledge of God requires demonstrative syllogism is false; the Quran uses qiyas al-awla, and God's signs themselves suffice for knowledge of Him.

Critique of Greek Logicians' Narrowness and Forced Definitions
Criticizes Greek logicians for narrowness, forced expressions, and poor definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Greek logicians' forced expressions and definitions.

Core Idea
Greek logicians' narrowness forces them into weak expressions and useless definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against the intellectual narrowness of the Greek logicians. He argues that when intellects are narrow, expressions become forced and definitions become useless. This lesson will help you dissect his critique and see how it applies to avoiding sophistry in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
The target is the Greek logicians' method itself, not logic per se. Note that Ibn Taymiyya blames their narrowness for poor expressions and definitions.
2
Extract the two main examples
First example: al-Kindi's phrase 'loss of the absence of existence' — a redundant and convoluted expression. Second example: defining the sun as 'a star that rises by day' — defining an obvious thing by something less obvious.
These examples illustrate forcedness (takalluf) and weakness (rakka).
3
Analyze why these are failures
The phrase is unnecessarily long and adds nothing. The definition of the sun is circular or useless: anyone who knows 'sun' knows it already; anyone who doesn't either needs translation or suffers blindness (in which case the definition is unhelpful).
This shows the definition does not achieve its purpose of clarifying.
4
Relate to the broader charge of sophistry
Ibn Taymiyya claims such forcedness leads to sophistry (safsata). The logicians' method, when applied, produces confusion rather than clarity, which is contrary to the aim of logic.
Sophistry arises from misuse of language and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استطراد - الركة والعِيُّ - وإذا اتسعت العقولُ وتصوراتها اتسعت عباراتها، وإذا ضاقت العقول والتصورات بقي صاحبها كأنه محبوس العقل واللسان، كما يصيب أهل المنطق اليوناني - تجدهم من أضيق الناس علماً وبياناً، وأعجزهم تصوراً وتعبيراً. ولهذا من كان منهم ذكياً إذا تصرّف في العلوم، وسَلَك مسلك أهل المنطق، طَوَّلَ وضيق، وتكلّف وتعسف ـ وغايتُه بيان البين وإيضاح الواضح ـ من العِيّ . وقد يوقعه ذلك في أنواع من السَّفْسَطة التي عافى الله منها من لم يسلك طريقهم . مثال الركة مثل ما ذكروه عن يعقوب بن إسحاق الكندي الفيلسوف أنه قال في بعض مناظراته : هذا من باب فقد عدم الوجود . ومثل هذه العبارات الطويلة الركيكة كثير في كلامهم، حتى في كلام أفضل متأخريهم، مع أنه أفضلهم وأحسنهم بياناً . وكذلك تكلفاتهم في حدودهم، مثل حدّهم لـ «الإنسان»، و «الشمس» بأنها كوكب يطلع نهاراً». وهل مَنْ يحدُّ «الشمس» مثل هذا الحد ونحوه إلا مَنْ هو مِن أجهل الناس؟ وهل عند الناس شيء أظهر من الشمس حتى تُحدّ الشمس به؟ ومن لم يعرف «الشمس» فإما أن يجهل اللفظ، فيترجم له، وليس هذا من الحد الذي ذكروه؛ وإما أن لا يكون رآها لعماه، فهذا لا يرى النهار، ولا الكواكب بطريق الأولى، مع أنه لا بد أن يسمع من الناس ما يعرف ذلك دون طريقهم.
Digression: Weakness and speech impediment. When intellects and their concepts expand, their expressions expand; and when intellects and concepts shrink, their possessor remains as if imprisoned in mind and tongue, as happens to the people of Greek logic. You find them among the narrowest of people in knowledge and expression, and the most incapable in conceptualization and articulation. So, if one of them is intelligent and engages in the sciences following the method of the logicians, he becomes long-winded and narrow, constrained and forced, and his goal is to explain the obvious and clarify the clear – due to speech impediment. And this may lead him into types of sophistry from which Allah has protected those who do not follow their way. An example of weakness is what they reported about Ya'qub ibn Ishaq al-Kindi, the philosopher, that he said in some of his debates: 'This is from the category of loss of the absence of existence.' And such long, weak expressions are common in their speech, even in the speech of the best of their later ones, even though he is the best and most eloquent among them. Likewise, their forcedness in their definitions, such as their definition of 'man' and 'sun' as 'a star that rises by day.' Is one who defines the 'sun' with such a definition and the like anything but the most ignorant of people? Is there anything more apparent to people than the sun that the sun should be defined by it? And whoever does not know 'sun' either does not know the term – so it is translated for him, and this is not the kind of definition they mentioned – or he has not seen it due to blindness, in which case he does not see the daytime or the stars by first priority, and yet he must have heard from people what makes him know it without their method.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique of the Greek logicians. The author begins by stating that intellectual breadth leads to expressive breadth, and narrowness leads to constraint. He accuses Greek logicians of being the narrowest in knowledge and expression. Their forcedness leads to sophistry. He provides two examples: al-Kindi's convoluted phrase 'loss of the absence of existence' (which is redundant), and their definition of the sun as 'a star that rises by day.' The author questions the utility of defining such an obvious thing; anyone who doesn't know the sun either doesn't know the Arabic term (translation suffices) or is blind (they can learn from others). Thus, their definitions are forced and unnecessary.

Key Terms
الركة (al-rakka)
العِيُّ (al-'ayy)
السَّفْسَطة (al-safsata)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya consider the definition of the sun as 'a star that rises by day' a failure?
A) Because it is too long and complicated.
B) Because the sun is more obvious than what it is defined by, making the definition useless.
C) Because it uses an astronomical term that is controversial.
D) Because it was invented by Greek logicians and not found in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique warns against forced expressions and definitions that obscure rather than clarify, a lesson directly applicable to avoiding sophistry in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a forced expression in a contemporary Islamic lecture (e.g., 'the absence of existence of benefit') and explain why it is weak.
?
Given the definition of 'wudu' as 'the use of water on specific body parts in a specific manner', evaluate whether it is a proper definition or could be a forced definition like the sun example.

Ibn Taymiyya's Linguistic Critique of Logicians
Ibn Taymiyya points out logicians' awkward language, gives al-Kindi as example, and describes deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique of logicians and explain how he uses al-Kindi's awkward phrasing as evidence for their artificiality.

Core Idea
Logicians' awkward language exposes the artificiality of their claimed certainty.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' claim to provide certain knowledge is refuted by their own awkward and stilted language. He contrasts them with the natural eloquence of the 'bulagha'' (eloquent ones) who establish clear proofs. As a concrete example, he points to al-Kindi, the so-called 'Philosopher of Islam,' whose unnatural phrase 'for lack of existence' reveals how logicians deform speech. This critique is not merely stylistic—it challenges the epistemological foundation of Greek logic by showing that its practitioners cannot even speak clearly. This lesson will help you recognize how linguistic critique serves as a powerful tool in Islamic intellectual debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contrasted Groups
Find where Ibn Taymiyya contrasts two groups: the eloquent people (bulagha') who establish certain proofs with clear phrases, and the logicians who constrict the path of knowledge. Note that the criterion is clarity of expression.
2
Locate the Specific Charge
Ibn Taymiyya accuses logicians of 'rakka wa 'iyy' (awkwardness and stammering). This is a linguistic and rhetorical criticism, not a logical one. It implies that their speech is unnatural and forced.
The terms are not technical logical terms; they are from the science of balagha (eloquence).
3
Examine the Example of al-Kindi
Read the phrase Ibn Taymiyya quotes from al-Kindi: 'li-`adam wujūd kadha' (for lack of existence of such-and-such). Why is this phrasing awkward? Consider that a natural Arabic speaker would say 'because such-and-such does not exist' without the artificial nominalization.
4
Analyze the Attribution of Eloquence
Ibn Taymiyya claims that if a logician like Ibn Sina shows eloquence, it comes from his learning from Muslims, not from his logical training. This undermines the idea that logic produces clarity.
5
Consider the Deception of the Ignorant
Ibn Taymiyya describes how the ignorant are impressed by logical terminology and later realize their error. He cites Ibn Sina's repentance as proof that even leading logicians eventually reject their own method.
The margin note about Ibn Sina's repentance is a historical claim Ibn Taymiyya uses to strengthen his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا لا يوجد في كلام البلغاء أهل البيان الذين يُقيمون البراهين والحُجَجَ اليقينية بأبين العبارات مِنْ استعمال المقدمتين في كلامهم ما يوجَدُ في كلام أهل المنطق. بل من سلك طريقهم كان من المضيقين لطريق العلم عقولاً والسنة، ومعانيهم من جنس ألفاظهم، تجد فيها من الرّكة والعِيّ ما لا يرضاه عاقل. وكان يعقوب بن إسحاق الكندي فيلسوفُ الإسلام في وقته ـ أعنى الفيلسوف العيب على من أثبت فلاسفة الذي في الإسلام وإلا فليس الفلاسفة من المسلمين كما قالوا لبعض أعيان القضاة الذين كانوا في زماننا ابن سينا من فلاسفة الإسلام، فقال: ليس للإسلام فلاسفة للإسلام كان يعقوب يقول في أثناء كلامه لعدم فقد وجود كذا، وأنواع هذه الإضافات. ومن وجد في بعض كلامه فصاحة أو بلاغة كما يوجد في بعض كلام ابن سينا وغيره فلما استفاده من المسلمين - من عقولهم وألسنتهم وإلا فلو مشى على طريقة سلفه وأعرض عما تعلمه من المسلمين لكان عقله ولسانه يشبه عقولهم وألسنتهم. التباس أمر المنطق على طائفة لم يتصوروا حقائقه وهم أكثر ما ينفقون على من لا يفهم ما يقولونه ويعظمهم بالجهل والوهم أو يفهم بعض ما يقولونه، أو أكثره، أو كله مع عدم تصوّره في تلك الحال لحقيقة ما جاء به الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وما يُعرف بالعقول السليمة، وما قاله سائر العقلاء، مناقضاً لما قالوه وهو إنما وصل إلى منتهى أمرهم بعد كلفة ومشقة، واقترن بها حسنُ ظَنّ، فتورّط من ضلالهم فيما لا يعلمه إلا الله. ثم إن تداركه الله بعد ذلك - كما أصاب كثيراً من الفضلاء الذين أحسنوا بهم الظنَّ ابتداء، ثم انكشف لهم من ضلالهم ما أوجب رجوعهم عنهم، وتبرأهم منهم، بل ورَدَّهم عليهم - وإلا بقي في الضلال [نص هامش: قد ذكر ابن خلكان أن ابن سينا تاب قبيل موته وأعتق مماليكه، وأقبل على تلاوة القرآن]
And for this reason, you do not find in the speech of eloquent people, the people of clear expression who establish proofs and certain arguments with the clearest phrases, the use of the two premises in their speech as is found in the speech of the logicians. Rather, whoever follows their path becomes among those who constrict the path of knowledge, both in intellects and tongues, and their meanings are of the same kind as their words; you find in them awkwardness and stammering that no rational person would be pleased with. And Ya'qub ibn Ishaq al-Kindi, the philosopher of Islam in his time—I mean the philosopher who was faulted for affirming the philosophers who were in Islam, otherwise the philosophers are not from among the Muslims, as they said to some prominent judges in our time about Ibn Sina being among the philosophers of Islam, and he said: 'Islam has no philosophers'—Ya'qub used to say in the midst of his speech 'for lack of existence of such-and-such' and various such additions. And whoever finds in some of his speech eloquence or rhetoric, as is found in some speech of Ibn Sina and others, that is only because he benefited from the Muslims—from their intellects and tongues. Otherwise, if he had followed the way of his predecessors and turned away from what he learned from the Muslims, his intellect and tongue would resemble theirs. The confusion of the matter of logic upon a group who did not conceive its realities... and they mostly spend on those who do not understand what they say and magnify them by ignorance and illusion, or understand some of what they say, or most of it, or all of it, while not conceiving at that time the reality of what the Messenger of Allah brought... until they are caught in their misguidance. Then if Allah grants them recovery afterwards, otherwise they remain in misguidance. [Margin note: Ibn Khallikan mentioned that Ibn Sina repented before his death, freed his slaves, and turned to reciting the Quran.]
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the linguistic style and intellectual confusion of the logicians. Ibn Taymiyya first contrasts the clear speech of eloquent people (bulagha') with the awkwardness (rakka and 'iyy) of logicians. He then gives a historical example: al-Kindi, known as the philosopher of Islam, used awkward phrases like 'for lack of existence of such-and-such'. Any eloquence found in logicians like Ibn Sina is attributed to their learning from Muslims, not from their logical method. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya discusses how the ignorant are deceived by logical terminology, and how some later realized the misguidance and repented, as in the case of Ibn Sina's repentance. The commentary follows the author's order.

Key Terms
الركة والعي
فيلسوف الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To show that even the best logicians use awkward language.
B) To prove that al-Kindi was not a true Muslim.
C) To demonstrate the eloquence of logicians.
D) To argue that al-Kindi's logic was perfect.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique reveals that logicians' artificial language undermines their claim to clarity, while the natural eloquence of Islamic discourse remains the superior path to certain knowledge.

?
Find a contemporary claim that uses complex terminology to sound authoritative (e.g., in academic or political discourse). Write a short critique identifying the awkwardness in its language, following Ibn Taymiyya's method of contrasting it with natural, clear expression.
?

The Logicians' Regress Objection to Sabr and Taqsim
Ibn Taymiyya presents the logicians' regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logicians' regress objection against sabr and taqsim and identify why they claim it yields only conjecture.

Core Idea
Logicians argue sabr and taqsim are uncertain because the ruling might be due to the essence, otherwise an infinite regress follows.

In this lesson, we examine the logicians' objection that the methods of sabr (examination) and taqsim (division) can only produce probable knowledge (zann), not certainty. Their key argument is that the ruling in the original case could be due to the essence of that case itself, and if not, an infinite regress of attributes would be required. Understanding this objection is essential before seeing Ibn Taymiyya's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Restate the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that rotation and division only yield conjecture. Locate their statement in the passage: 'لا يفيدان إلا الظن'.
2
Extract the regress objection
The logicians argue that sabr and taqsim are not certain because 'يجوز أن يكون الحكم ثابتاً في الأصل لذات الأصل لا لخارج، وإلا لزم التسلسل'. This means the ruling could be due to the essence of the original itself, not an external attribute; otherwise a regress would follow.
The phrase 'وإلا لزم التسلسل' indicates a conditional: if the ruling is not due to the essence, then regress.
3
Identify the underlying assumption
The logicians assume that if the ruling is due to an external attribute, then that attribute itself would require a cause, leading to an infinite chain. They conclude that certainty is impossible because the ruling might be due to the essence.
4
Recognize the epistemic consequence
Because we cannot rule out that the ruling is due to the essence, the logicians claim that sabr and taqsim can never yield certainty—only probable knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - إبطال قولهم : (الدوران) و التقسيم» لا يفيدان إلا الظن
فيه قياس الشمول .
جواب اعتراضهم على السبر والتقسيم بقولهم (ص ٢٤٧، س ٢٠) : «وهو أيضاً غير يقيني لجواز أن يكون الحكم ثابتاً
في الأصل لذات الأصل لا لخارج، وإلا لزم التسلسل .
قولهم: يجوز
وأما قوله يجوز أن يكون الحكم ثابتاً في الأصل لذات الأصل، وإلا لزم أن يكون
الحكم لذات
التسلسل، فنقول : لا يلزم التسلسل إلا إذا كان لا يثبت إلا لخارج (۱) . أما إذا كان تارة الأصل
يثبت لخارج (۱) وتارة لغير خارج (١) لم يلزم (۲) التسلسل . وحينئذ فلا يمتنع أن تعلم أن الحكم في هذا الأصل المعيَّن لم يثبت لما يختص به بل لوصف مشترك بينه وبين غيره.
The third station: Refuting their statement: 'Rotation and division only yield conjecture.' It contains the comprehensive syllogism. Answer to their objection to sabr and taqsim by their statement (p. 247, line 20): 'It is also non-certain because the ruling could be established in the original due to the essence of the original itself, not due to an external [attribute]—otherwise a regress would follow.' Their statement: 'It is possible'... And as for his statement: 'It is possible that the ruling is established in the original due to the essence of the original, otherwise it would be that the ruling is due to the essence of the original causing a regress'—we say: A regress only occurs if [the ruling] is established only for the external [attribute]. If it is sometimes established for the external and sometimes for something other than external, regress does not occur. At that point, it is not impossible to know that the ruling in this specific original [case] is established not because of what is specific to it but because of a description common between it and others.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the methods of sabr (examination) and taqsim (division) only yield probable knowledge (zann). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the third station (maqam) which aims to invalidate the logicians' statement that rotation (al-dawaran) and division (al-taqsim) only produce conjecture. He notes that this station contains the comprehensive syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). Then he presents the logicians' objection: they argue that sabr and taqsim are not certain because it is possible that the ruling in the original case is due to the essence of that case itself, not due to an external attribute, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Ibn Taymiyya responds by explaining that a regress only occurs if the ruling is established solely for the external attribute. However, if the ruling can be established sometimes for the external attribute and sometimes for something other than the external, then no regress occurs. Therefore, it is not impossible to know that the ruling in a particular original case is established not because of what is specific to it, but because of a description common between it and other cases. This refutes the logicians' claim that such knowledge is only conjectural.

Key Terms
الدوران
التقسيم
السبر
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, why are sabr and taqsim not certain?
A) Because the ruling might be due to an external attribute, leading to regress.
B) Because the ruling might be due to the essence of the original, and if not, regress follows.
C) Because the method is only based on conjecture from the beginning.
D) Because the ruling can never be known with certainty due to human limitation.

Key Takeaway
The logicians claim that the possibility of the ruling being due to the essence, combined with the threat of regress, undermines the certainty of sabr and taqsim.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation: Circumscribing the Regress
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the regress objection and identify the condition under which regress does not occur.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that regress only occurs if the ruling is exclusively for an external attribute; otherwise certainty is attainable.

Ibn Taymiyya responds to the logicians' regress objection by pointing out a critical flaw: the regress only follows if the ruling is always established for an external attribute. But if the ruling can be established for an external attribute in some cases and for something else (like the essence) in other cases, then no regress occurs. This allows us to know with certainty that in a given case, the ruling is due to a common description.

Guided Path
1
Restate the logicians' objection
Recall that the logicians argued sabr and taqsim are uncertain because the ruling might be due to the essence, and if not, regress follows.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's key qualification
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'لا يلزم التسلسل إلا إذا كان لا يثبت إلا لخارج' – regress only occurs if [the ruling] is established only for an external attribute. This is the crucial condition.
The phrase 'لا يثبت إلا لخارج' means the ruling is exclusively for an external attribute.
3
Contrast with the alternative scenario
Ibn Taymiyya then presents the alternative: if the ruling can be established for an external attribute sometimes and for something other than an external attribute (like the essence) at other times, then no regress follows: 'إذا كان تارة يثبت لخارج وتارة لغير خارج لم يلزم التسلسل'.
4
Apply to the original case
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that it is not impossible to know that the ruling in a particular original case is established not because of what is unique to it, but because of a description common to it and other cases. This refutes the claim that such knowledge is only conjectural.

في الأصل لذات الأصل لا لخارج، وإلا لزم التسلسل .

Ibn Taymiyya on Sophistry as Disease and the Limits of Qiyas
Ibn Taymiyya describes skeptical sophistry as a disease and argues qiyas only gives universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's two critiques of Greek logic in this passage and explain why he treats sophistry as a psychological ailment and qiyas as limited to universals.

Core Idea
Skeptical sophistry is a disease, not a rational flaw; syllogism gives only universal knowledge, easily obtained otherwise.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his radical critique of Aristotelian logic. Here he tackles two key points: the nature of skeptical sophistry (safsata) and the epistemic limitation of syllogism (qiyas). He reframes sophistry as a psychological disorder, not a logical error, and argues that qiyas only yields universals, which are more easily known through other means. This challenges the very foundation of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two targets
Find the two distinct claims Ibn Taymiyya is making: one about sophistry (سفسطة) and one about syllogism (قياس). Locate where each begins in the Arabic text.
The first part discusses sophistry; the second part starts with 'وأيضاً' and critiques qiyas.
2
Analyze his characterization of sophistry
How does Ibn Taymiyya describe skeptical sophistry? He calls it 'من جنس المرض والوساوس' (a kind of illness and whisperings). What does this imply about its nature? Is it a rational error or a psychological condition?
He emphasizes individual variation and cure through personal means like supplication.
3
Examine his argument against qiyas
Ibn Taymiyya argues that qiyas only produces knowledge of universals (كليات), not particulars. He further claims that those universals can be known more easily by other means. Identify the reasons he gives.
He says knowledge of specific things (المعيَّنات) may be more evident than knowledge of universals.
4
Evaluate the strength of his critique
Does Ibn Taymiyya's critique itself rely on logical reasoning? Is his dismissal of sophistry as mere illness a rigorous argument? Consider whether his alternative (personal reflection, supplication) is a sufficient replacement for formal logic.
This evaluation is part of using the critique_dissector tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما قد يعرض لبعضهم في بعض الأحوال من سَفْسَطة تشكّكه في المعلومات السفسطة المشككة فتلك من جنس المرض والوساوس. وهذه إنما يمكن بيان أنواعها العامة، وأما ما يختص به كلُّ شخص فلا ضابط له حتى يذكر في كلام بل هذا يزول بأسباب تختص بصاحبه كدعائه لنفسه ومخاطبة شخص معين له بما يناسب حاله، ونظره هو فيما يخص حاله، ونحو ذلك . وأيضاً فما يذكرونه من «القياس لا يفيد إلا العلم بأمور كلية، لا يفيد العلم بشيء معين من الموجودات، ثم تلك الأمور الكلية يمكن العلم بكل واحد منها بما أيسر من قياسهم، فلا تعلم كلية بـ قياسهم إلا والعلم بجزئياتها ممكن بدون قياسهم الشمولي، وربما كان أيسر فإن العلم بالمعيَّنات قد يكون أبين من العلم بالكليات. وهذا مبسوط في موضعه .
And as for what may afflict some of them in some situations of sophistry (safsata) that makes them skeptical about known matters—that skeptical sophistry is of the kind of illness and whisperings (waswasa). This can only be explained in its general types; as for what is specific to each person, there is no rule for it to be mentioned in discourse. Rather, this goes away by causes specific to its owner, such as his supplication for himself, or addressing a specific person with what suits his state, or his own reflection on his state, and the like. Also, what they mention about 'syllogism (qiyas) does not produce knowledge except of universal matters; it does not produce knowledge of any specific thing from existents. Then those universal matters can be known, each one of them, by something easier than their syllogism. So no universal is known by their syllogism except that knowledge of its particulars is possible without their universal syllogism, and perhaps it is easier. For knowledge of specific things may be more evident than knowledge of universals. This is elaborated in its place.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on two points: the nature of skeptical sophistry and the limitation of syllogism (qiyas). He begins by characterizing skeptical sophistry as a kind of illness or whisperings (waswasa) that afflicts some people in certain situations. He notes that while general types of sophistry can be described, specific cases vary by individual and cannot be codified; they are cured by personal means such as supplication or targeted counsel. Then he turns to a critique of the logicians' claim for qiyas: he argues that qiyas only yields knowledge of universals, not particular existents. Moreover, those universals can be known more easily by other means, and knowledge of particulars is often more evident than knowledge of universals. Thus, qiyas is not the privileged method for acquiring knowledge. This aligns with his overall rejection of the primacy of Greek syllogistic logic.

Key Terms
سفسطة
قياس
كليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reduces skeptical sophistry to a personal ailment and restricts syllogism to universal knowledge, both moves aimed at undermining the epistemic authority of Greek logic.

Sophistical Doubt as a Psychological Disease
Author defines sophistical doubt as a psychological disease with individual cures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya redefines sophistry as a psychological condition and contrast it with the logicians' view.

Core Idea
Sophistry is not just a logical error but a disease requiring personal cures.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya shifts the discussion of sophistry from logical fallacies to psychological malady. He argues that while general types can be described, each person's doubt is unique and requires individual remedies. This challenges the view that logic can fully address sophistical doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
Read the beginning: 'sophistry that causes doubt in known matters'. Note that it is a state (hal) that occurs to some people.
2
Recognize the metaphor
Ibn Taymiyya likens it to 'disease and obsessive thoughts' (marad wa wasawis). This shifts it from a cognitive error to a psychological affliction.
Waswasa is a term used in Islamic spirituality for intrusive doubts.
3
Distinguish general vs. specific
He says general types (anwa' 'amma) can be explained, but individual specifics (ma yakhtassu bi kull shakhs) have no regulation (la dabita lahu).
This is a key epistemological boundary.
4
Extract the cures
The remedies are personal: supplication (du'a'), direct counsel from a specific person, and personal reflection.
These are not universal logical rules.
5
Contrast with logicians’ approach
Logicians treat sophistry as a category of fallacies to be refuted by syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya insists that such universal methods are insufficient for individual cases.
This is a critique of the pretensions of formal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما قد يعرض لبعضهم في بعض الأحوال من سفسطة تشككه في المعلومات، فتلك من جنس المرض والوساوس. وهذه إنما يمكن بيان أنواعها العامة، وأما ما يختص به كل شخص فلا ضابط له حتى يذكر في كلام بل هذا يزول بأسباب تختص بصاحبه كدعائه لنفسه ومخاطبة شخص معين له بما يناسب حاله، ونظره هو فيما يخص حاله، ونحو ذلك.
And as for what may occur to some of them in some states of sophistry that causes doubt in known matters, that is of the kind of disease and obsessive thoughts. These can only be explained in terms of their general types; but as for what is specific to each person, there is no regulation for it to be mentioned in speech, rather it is removed by causes specific to its owner, such as his supplication for himself and the address of a particular person to him with what suits his state, and his own reflection on what concerns his state, and the like.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines sophistical doubt (safsata) not as a logical problem but as a psychological disease. Ibn Taymiyya argues that while general types of sophistry can be described, the individual manifestations are unique and cannot be systematized. The cure is personal: supplication, direct counsel from a knowledgeable person, or personal reflection. This contrasts with the logicians' assumption that sophistry can be fully treated by logical rules. The author emphasizes that doubts are akin to mental illnesses (waswasa) and require individual remedies.

Key Terms
سفسطة
مرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A social phenomenon

Key Takeaway
Sophistical doubt is not merely a logical error; it is a psychological condition that requires individualized spiritual and cognitive remedies.

General Types vs. Individual Specifics in Epistemology
Author distinguishes general explanations from individual cures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between general types and individual specifics to evaluate claims about universal methods in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
What can be known generally may not cure individual doubt.

Ibn Taymiyya concedes that general types of sophistry can be explained, but insists that individual manifestations are beyond regulation. This highlights a key epistemological limitation: universal methods cannot fully address personal epistemic crises. We explore how this principle operates in fields like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find the words 'anwa' 'amma' (general types) and 'ma yakhtassu bi kull shakhs' (what is specific to each person).
2
Note what is claimed about each
General types can be explained (yumkin bayanuha). Specifics have no regulation (la dabita lahu) and cannot be captured in speech (yudhkar fi kalam).
This is a strong claim about the limits of systematic knowledge.
3
Understand the implication
If individual doubts cannot be regulated, then universal logical rules cannot guarantee the removal of doubt for every person. Each case requires its own remedy.
4
Apply to a real Islamic science example
Consider a student in kalam who is unconvinced by a general proof for God's existence. According to Ibn Taymiyya, no additional universal syllogism will necessarily help; rather, a personal approach may be needed.
This is analogous to the fiqh principle that general legal maxims have exceptions.
5
Evaluate the value of general types
Despite the limits, Ibn Taymiyya does not dismiss general knowledge entirely; he says it can be explained. The point is that it is not sufficient for all cases.
