Mental Conceivability Does Not Prove External Existence
Critique of Ibn Sina's argument from universal concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental possibility and external possibility and critique arguments that conflate them.

Core Idea
Mental conceivability does not entail external existence.

Ibn Taymiyya targets Ibn Sina's argument that because we can conceive a universal concept like 'human', it must be possible for such an intelligible to exist outside the mind. This lesson unpacks why this argument fails and how the mind can conceive impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Extract Ibn Sina's argument
Ibn Sina argues: we conceive the universal 'human' that encompasses individuals; therefore, such a universal must be possible in external reality. Locate this in the passage.
The argument confuses mental existence with external existence.
2
Identify the flaw
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'The universal does not exist as universal except in the mind.' The conceivability only proves mental possibility, not external. Also, the mind can conceive what is impossible externally, like the conjunction of opposites.
This shows that conceivability is not a reliable guide to external possibility.
3
Provide a counterexample
Think of something impossible: a square circle. The mind can conceive it, but it cannot exist. Thus, conceivability is not sufficient for external possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - فساد إثبات الإمكان الخارجي بمجرد عدم العلم بالامتناع والمقصود هنا أن الإمكان الخارجي» يُعرف بالوجود، لا بمجرد عدم العلم بالامتناع، كما يقوله طائفةٌ منهم: هذا لم يلزم منه ممتنع . فإن هذه القضية الشرطية غير معلومة، فإن كونه لا يلزم منه محذور ليس معلوماً بالبديهة، ولا أقام عليه دليلاً نظرياً. الآمدي - إذا أرادوا إثبات إمكان أمر قالوا : «لو قدرنا /٣٢٢ بفساد إثباته وأبعد من إثباته الإمكان الخارجي بـ الإمكان الذهني» ما يسلكه طائفة من المتفلسفة ،والمتكلّمة كابن سينا والرازي ،وغيرهما، في إثبات الإمكان الخارجي بمجرد إمكان تصوره في الذهن . كما أن ابن سينا وأتباعه لما أرادوا إثبات موجود في الخارج معقول ـ لا يكون محسوساً بحال - استدلوا على ذلك بتصوّر «الإنسان الكلّي المطلق المتناول للأفراد الموجودة في الخارج. وهذا إنما يفيد إمكان وجود هذه المعقولات في الذهن، فإن الكلي لا يوجد كليّاً إلا في الذهن، وهذا ليس مورد النزاع، وإنما النزاع في إمكان وجود مثل هذا المعقول في الخارج. وليس كلُّ ما تصوَّرَه الذهن يكون موجوداً في الخارج كما يتصور الذهنُ. فإن الذهن يتصور ما يمتنع وجوده في الخارج، كما يتصور الجمع بين النقيضين والصدين . وقد تبعه الرازي على الاستدلال بهذا واستدل هو وغيره على إمكان ذلك بأن قال : يمكن أن يقال : الموجود إما أن يكون مُجانباً لغيره وإما أن يكون مبائناً لغيره وإما أن لا يكون ـ لا مجانباً ولا مبائنا فظنوا أنه بإمكان هذا التقسيم العقلي يُسْتَدَلُّ على إمكان وجود كل من الأقسام في الخارج، وهذا غلط . فإن هذا التقسيم كقول القائل : الموجود إما أن يكون واجباً وإما أن يكون ممكناً، وإما أن لا يكون واجباً ولا ممكناً. وإما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون محدثاً، وإما أن لا يكون قديماً ولا محدثاً . وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه وإما أن يكون قائماً بغيره وإما أن لا يكون قائماً بنفسه ولا بغيره. وأمثال ذلك من التقسيمات التي يقدّرها الذهن ومعلوم أن هذا لا يدل
The Fourth Station — The corruption of proving external possibility by mere lack of knowledge of impossibility. What is intended here is that 'external possibility' is known by existence, not by mere lack of knowledge of impossibility, as a group among them say: 'No impossibility follows from this.' For this conditional proposition is not known — for its being such that no absurdity follows is not known by intuition, nor has he established a theoretical proof for it. Al-Amidi — when they want to prove the possibility of something they say: 'If we suppose...' The corruption of proving external possibility by mental possibility is worse than proving it by mental possibility, which is the method of a group of philosophers and theologians like Ibn Sina and al-Razi and others in proving external possibility by the mere possibility of its conception in the mind. Just as Ibn Sina and his followers when they wanted to prove the existence in the external world of an intelligible being that is not sensible at all — they argued for that by conceiving 'the universal absolute human being encompassing the individuals existing in the external world'. This only yields the possibility of the existence of these intelligibles in the mind, for the universal does not exist as universal except in the mind, and this is not the point of dispute; rather the dispute is about the possibility of the existence of such an intelligible in the external world. And not everything the mind conceives exists in the external world as the mind conceives it. For the mind conceives what is impossible in the external world, as it conceives the conjunction of opposites and the two contraries. And al-Razi followed him in this argument, and he and others argued for its possibility by saying: 'It can be said: The existent is either adjacent to another or separate from another or neither adjacent nor separate.' They thought that by this intellectual division one could argue for the possibility of each division's existence externally, but this is an error. For this division is like someone saying: The existent is either necessary or possible or neither necessary nor possible; or it is either eternal or originated or neither eternal nor originated; or it is either self-subsistent or subsistent by another or neither self-subsistent nor subsistent by another. And similar divisions that the mind imagines — and it is known that this does not prove...
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logical method used by Ibn Sina, al-Razi, and others to prove 'external possibility' (al-imkan al-khariji). Ibn Taymiyya argues that external possibility cannot be established merely by the absence of known impossibility (as al-Amidi thought) or by the fact that something can be conceived in the mind. He uses the example of the universal concept 'human' — its conceivability in the mind only proves mental possibility, not external existence. The mind can conceive impossibilities like the conjunction of opposites. Furthermore, al-Razi's appeal to an intellectual division (taqsim 'aqli) of the existent into adjacent/separate/neither is fallacious because such divisions are mental constructs that do not guarantee actual external instantiation. Ibn Taymiyya compares it to similar divisions (necessary/possible/neither, etc.) that are purely mental and prove nothing about external reality. The passage ends with the claim that such divisions do not prove external possibility — a clear rejection of this proof method.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخارجي
الإمكان الذهني
التقسيم العقلي

A) The universal concept 'human'
B) The conjunction of opposites and two contraries
C) The necessary existent
D) The division of existent into adjacent and separate

Key Takeaway
Mental conceivability is not proof of external possibility; the mind can conceive the impossible.

advanced
10 min
Intellectual Division Does Not Prove External Possibility
Refutation of al-Razi's argument from intellectual division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an intellectual division (taqsim 'aqli) is being used as a proof of external possibility and refute it using Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Core Idea
An intellectual division is a mental construct; it does not guarantee external instantiation of each division.

Al-Razi and others used a division of the existent into adjacent, separate, or neither to argue for the existence of a 'neither' entity. Ibn Taymiyya exposes this as a fallacy by showing analogous divisions that are clearly uninformative. Learn to detect such false moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division and its purpose
Al-Razi divides the existent into: (1) adjacent to another, (2) separate from another, (3) neither adjacent nor separate. The aim is to prove that the third category is externally possible.
They think the logical division itself implies that each category has an external instance.
2
Note the fallacy type
Ibn Taymiyya calls it an error (ghalat). The division is a mental operation; it does not prove that each division is realized externally.
This is similar to the fallacy of assuming that a complete division guarantees existence.
3
Compare with Ibn Taymiyya's counterexample
He gives analogous divisions: necessary/possible/neither; eternal/originated/neither; self-subsistent/subsistent by another/neither. These are similarly uninformative. The fact that the mind can create a division does not mean the world accommodates all options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - فساد إثبات الإمكان الخارجي بمجرد عدم العلم بالامتناع والمقصود هنا أن الإمكان الخارجي» يُعرف بالوجود، لا بمجرد عدم العلم بالامتناع، كما يقوله طائفةٌ منهم: هذا لم يلزم منه ممتنع . فإن هذه القضية الشرطية غير معلومة، فإن كونه لا يلزم منه محذور ليس معلوماً بالبديهة، ولا أقام عليه دليلاً نظرياً. الآمدي - إذا أرادوا إثبات إمكان أمر قالوا : «لو قدرنا /٣٢٢ بفساد إثباته وأبعد من إثباته الإمكان الخارجي بـ الإمكان الذهني» ما يسلكه طائفة من المتفلسفة ،والمتكلّمة كابن سينا والرازي ،وغيرهما، في إثبات الإمكان الخارجي بمجرد إمكان تصوره في الذهن . كما أن ابن سينا وأتباعه لما أرادوا إثبات موجود في الخارج معقول ـ لا يكون محسوساً بحال - استدلوا على ذلك بتصوّر «الإنسان الكلّي المطلق المتناول للأفراد الموجودة في الخارج. وهذا إنما يفيد إمكان وجود هذه المعقولات في الذهن، فإن الكلي لا يوجد كليّاً إلا في الذهن، وهذا ليس مورد النزاع، وإنما النزاع في إمكان وجود مثل هذا المعقول في الخارج. وليس كلُّ ما تصوَّرَه الذهن يكون موجوداً في الخارج كما يتصور الذهنُ. فإن الذهن يتصور ما يمتنع وجوده في الخارج، كما يتصور الجمع بين النقيضين والصدين . وقد تبعه الرازي على الاستدلال بهذا واستدل هو وغيره على إمكان ذلك بأن قال : يمكن أن يقال : الموجود إما أن يكون مُجانباً لغيره وإما أن يكون مبائناً لغيره وإما أن لا يكون ـ لا مجانباً ولا مبائنا فظنوا أنه بإمكان هذا التقسيم العقلي يُسْتَدَلُّ على إمكان وجود كل من الأقسام في الخارج، وهذا غلط . فإن هذا التقسيم كقول القائل : الموجود إما أن يكون واجباً وإما أن يكون ممكناً، وإما أن لا يكون واجباً ولا ممكناً. وإما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون محدثاً، وإما أن لا يكون قديماً ولا محدثاً . وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه وإما أن يكون قائماً بغيره وإما أن لا يكون قائماً بنفسه ولا بغيره. وأمثال ذلك من التقسيمات التي يقدّرها الذهن ومعلوم أن هذا لا يدل

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Divine Science as Mental Abstraction
Ibn Taymiyya critiques divine science as mental construct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' universal existence and Necessary Being are mental constructs with no external reality.

Core Idea
What you think is divine science may be only in your head.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the very foundation of the philosophers' metaphysics. He argues that their 'highest science' is not about Allah but about an abstraction that exists only in the mind. Let's trace how he dismantles their framework step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of philosophy
Ibn Taymiyya refers to natural, mathematical, and divine science. Notice which one, according to him, deals with external reality.
Natural science is the only one with external referents.
2
Examine the status of divine science
The philosophers claim its subject is 'universal existence.' Ibn Taymiyya asserts this has no external existence.
3
Analyze 'absolute universal existence'
How does Ibn Taymiyya characterize this concept? He says it is an estimation in the mind, not a real thing.
It is divided into substance/accident, cause/effect, old/originated – all mental categories.
4
Contrast with the true highest being
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the philosophers' 'highest science' with Allah, the truly Most High, citing Qur'an 87:1-5.
The real divine science is knowledge of Allah, not of mental abstractions.
5
Evaluate the Necessary Being of Ibn Sina
Whether defined as completely abstract or restricted to negative attributes, Ibn Taymiyya argues it exists only in the mind.
He concludes that it is impossible for such a being to exist externally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تأمل الخبير بالحقائق كلامهم في هذه الأنواع لم يجد عندهم بمعلومات موجودة في الخارج إلا القسم الذي يسمونه الطبيعي» وما يتبعه من الرياضي». وأما الرياضي المجرَّد في الذهن فهو الحكم بمقادير ذهنية لا وجود لها في الخارج. والذي سموه علم ما بعد الطبيعة»، وهو ما جرَّدوه عن المادة في الذهن والخارج، إذا تُدبّر لم يوجد فيه علم بمعلوم موجود في الخارج، وإنما تصوروا أموراً مقدرة في أذهانهم لا حقيقة لها في الخارج. ولهذا كان منتهى نظرهم وآخر فلسفتهم ، وحكمتهم هو «الوجود المطلق الكلي» أو المشروط بسَلْب الأمور الوجودية. جميع موضوع العلم فموضوع العلم الإلهي الناظر في الوجود» ولواحقه هو «الوجود الكلي» المنقسم إلى جوهر وعَرَض وعلة ،ومعلول، وقديم وحادث وهذا لا وجود له في الخارج، وإنما يوجد في الذهن وهو العلم الأعلى عندهم. فهذا العلم الأعلى مقدر في الذهن. والعليّ الأعلى الموجود في الخارج هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو، الذي أنزل قوله: ﴿ سَبِّح اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى الَّذِي خَلَقَ فَسَوَّى وَالَّذِى قَدَّرَ فَهَدَى وَالَّذِي أَخْرَجَ الْمَرْعَى فَجَعَلَهُ غناء أحوى ) [سورة الأعلى : ١ - ٥] . وأما واجب الوجود الذي يثبتونه، فإما أن يجعلوه وجوداً مجرداً عن كل قيد واجب الوجود ممتنع الوجود ثُبوتي وعَدَمي، وإما أن يقولوا : بل هو مقيد بالأمور السلبية دون الثبوتية، كقول ابن سينا وأمثاله. وهذا إنما يوجد في الذهن لا في الخارج. فإنه يمتنع ثبوت موجود خارجي لا ...
And if an expert in realities examines their speech about these types, he finds that they have no information about existing things externally except the section they call 'natural' and what follows it from the mathematical. As for the mathematical abstracted in the mind, it is a judgment about mental quantities that have no external existence. And what they called 'science of what is after nature', which they abstracted from matter in the mind and externally, upon reflection, there is no knowledge of any known thing existing externally in it; rather they imagined matters that are estimated in their minds with no reality externally. And for this reason, the end of their view and the finality of their philosophy and wisdom is 'absolute universal existence' or existence conditioned by the negation of existential things. The entire subject of science, so the subject of divine science that examines existence and its accidents is 'universal existence' divided into substance and accident, cause and effect, old and originated, and this has no external existence, rather it exists only in the mind, and it is the highest science according to them. So this highest science is estimated in the mind. And the highest highest existing externally is Allah, whom there is no god but He, who sent down His saying: 'Exalt the name of your Lord, the Most High, who created and proportioned, and who destined and guided, and who brought out the pasture and made it black stubble.' [Surah al-A'la: 1-5]. As for the Necessary Being that they affirm, either they make it an existence abstracted from every positive and negative restriction, or they say: rather it is restricted by negative attributes without positive ones, like the saying of Ibn Sina and his likes. And this exists only in the mind, not externally. For it is impossible to have an external existent that is not ...
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the philosophers' divine science. He argues that their 'universal existence' and 'Necessary Being' are mental constructs without external reality. First, he notes that an expert examination of their three types reveals that only natural science deals with external existents. The mathematical is mental, and the divine science is purely imaginary. Their highest science, the study of 'absolute universal existence,' does not correspond to anything external. The true highest being external is Allah. As for the Necessary Being they posit, it is either completely abstract or restricted to negative attributes, both of which only exist in the mind. Thus, their metaphysics is empty of real external knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Empty Universals
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the emptiness of universals claimed by logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' concept of universals, and distinguish between genuine universals with external instances and mere mental constructs.

Core Idea
Universals without external instances are empty mental fictions, not genuine knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that they reason with universal concepts. He shows that their 'universals' often have no external reality, making them mere mental constructs. In this lesson, we dissect his critique and see how he uses the demand for examples to expose the emptiness of such claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Read the opening: logicians claim to speak about universal intellectual matters. Note their retreat to 'more general' concepts when pressed.
Ibn Taymiyya presents this as a rhetorical move to avoid scrutiny.
2
The demand for an example
Ibn Taymiyya demands a concrete example. The logicians cannot provide one, revealing that their universal is merely mental.
An example would be a particular, which should exist if the universal is real.
3
Categorize the types of universals
He lists four possibilities: impossible (partner of God), non-existent (winged goat), existent but no sharing (a species confined to one), and existent with possibility of sharing but hasn't occurred. All fail to provide a genuine universal.
Only the last has some claim, but it is still dependent on external conditions.
4
Linguistic analysis of 'al-shams' and 'al-ilah'
Ibn Taymiyya argues that in actual usage, these terms are particular, referring to the known sun and Allah. The logicians treat them as common nouns, but they are defined by the definite article for a specific entity.
He cites Quran 21:33 to show that 'al-shams' is used for the known sun, not a universal.
5
Conclude on the method of reasoning
He asserts that actual reasoning about universals is analogical (qiyas al-tamthil), not the inclusive syllogism. The logicians' claim depends on false universals.
This aligns with his broader empiricist and particularist epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أنهم كثيراً ما يدّعون في المطالب البرهانية من الأمور العقلية ما عجزهم عن يكونوا قد قدروه في أذهانهم، ويقولون: نحن نتكلم في الأمور الكلية والعقليات الكلية المحضة . وإذا ذُكر لهم شيء قالوا: نوعه في شخصه، لا لمجرّد تصور معناه ؛ وهذا مشهور بينهم . وإنما يصح هذا إذا كان لفظ «الإله» ولفظ «الشمس» اسم جنس بحيث لا يُقصد به الشمس المعيّنة ولا الإله المعين المعروف فإن الكلي» عندهم مثل «اسم الجنس في اصطلاح النحاة، وهو ما عُلّق على الشيء وعلى كل ما أشبهه. والناس لا يقصدون بلفظ «الشمس» إلا الشمس المعيّنة، واللام فيها لتعريف العهد، لا للجنس، كما قال تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ كُلٌّ فِي فَلَكٍ يَسْبَحُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأنبياء : ٣٣]. فمسمى «الشمس» و «القمر هنا جزئي لا كلي بخلاف لفظ «الكوكب» و النجم ونحو ذلك، فإنه كلي وكذلك اسم «الإله» عند المسلمين إنما يريدون به إلههم»، وهو «الله» لا إله إلا هو . وعلى هذا فليس هذا ولا هذا كليّاً مشتركاً، بل نفس تصور معناه يمنع من وقوع الشركة فيه . فهو معيَّن مختص، وهو الذي يسمونه «الجزئي»، ليس مطلقاً مشتركاً، وهو الذي يسمونه «الكلي». وكان الخُسْرَوْشَاهي من أعيانهم وأعيان أصحاب الرازي. وكان يقول: «ما عثرنا إلا على هذه الكليات وكان قد وقع في حَيْرة وشكّ ، حتى كان يقول : والله ! ما أدري ما أعتقد والله ما أدري ما أعتقد والله ! ما أدري ما أعتقدُ . والمقصود أن الذي يدَّعونه من الكليات هو إذا كان علماً فهو مما يُعرف بـ «قياس التمثيل»، لا يقف على القياس المنطقي الشمولي أصلاً. بل ما يدعون توقفه
What is intended here is that they often claim in demonstrative demands from intellectual matters that they are unable to be what they have estimated in their minds, and they say: 'We speak about universal matters and pure universal intellectual matters.' And if something is mentioned to them, they say: 'We speak about what is more general than that,' and about 'the reality insofar as it is reality,' and similar expressions. So they are demanded to verify what they mentioned in external existence, and it is said: 'Explain this, what is it?' Then their ignorance becomes apparent, and that what they say is a matter estimated in minds, with no reality in external entities. This is like when it is said to them: 'Mention an example of that,' and the example is a particular matter. So if they fail to give an example and say: 'We speak about universal matters,' know that they speak without knowledge and about matters they do not know have a known instance in external existence, rather about matters that have no known instance in external existence, and about matters that may be impossible to have an instance in external existence. Otherwise, knowledge of existing things, if it is universal, would have instances established in external existence. And the term 'universal' (kulli) they mean by it 'what does not prevent its conception from the occurrence of sharing in it.' Then it may be that the meaning of the term is impossible in external existence, like the partner of the Creator. And it may be non-existent, only estimated by the mind, as is estimated a 'winged goat', and this is Aristotle's example. And it may exist in external existence but does not admit sharing, and it may be possible for sharing to occur in it (a subtle discussion in [literal] terms) but it did not occur. And they give as an example of this the name 'the god' (al-ilah) and 'the sun' (al-shams), and they make the referent of this universal: its conception does not prevent sharing in it, but sharing is prevented only for an external reason, so its type is confined to its individual, not merely because of the conception of its meaning. This is well-known among them. And this is only valid if the word 'al-ilah' and the word 'al-shams' are common nouns such that one does not intend by them the specific sun nor the specific known god. For 'universal' according to them is like 'common noun' in the terminology of grammarians, which is attached to a thing and to everything similar to it. But people do not intend by the word 'al-shams' except the specific sun, and the definite article in it is for the definition of the known (al-'ahd), not for the genus, as Allah the Exalted said: 'And He it is Who created the night and the day, and the sun and the moon; each in an orbit they float' [al-Anbiya: 33]. So the referent of 'al-shams' and 'al-qamar' here is particular, not universal, unlike the word 'al-kawkab' (planet) and 'al-najm' (star) and the like, for it is universal. And likewise, the name 'al-ilah' for Muslims, they intend by it their god, which is 'Allah', there is no god but Him. And on this basis, neither this nor that is a common universal; rather, the very conception of its meaning prevents sharing in it. So it is specific and exclusive, which they call 'particular' (juz'i), not absolute and common, which they call 'universal' (kulli). And al-Khusrawshahi was one of their prominent figures and prominent companions of al-Razi. He used to say: 'We have only come across these universals,' and he fell into confusion and doubt, until he would say: 'By Allah, I do not know what I believe! By Allah, I do not know what I believe! By Allah, I do not know what I believe!' And what is intended is that what they claim of universals, if it is knowledge, it is known through 'analogical reasoning' (qiyas al-tamthil), not at all depending on the inclusive logical syllogism. Rather, what they claim depends on...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' concept of universals (al-kulliyat). He begins by noting that logicians often claim to discuss universal intellectual matters but fail to provide concrete examples. When asked for an example, they retreat to saying they speak about 'what is more general' or 'reality as such.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that this reveals their ignorance: their universals are merely mental constructs with no external reality. He points out that if universal knowledge were true, there would be actual instances in external existence. He then explains the logicians' definition of 'universal' as 'what does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it,' and categorizes possible types: some universals are impossible in externality (e.g., a partner to God), some are non-existent (e.g., a winged goat, Aristotle's example), some exist but do not admit sharing (e.g., a species confined to one individual), and some could admit sharing but it hasn't occurred. He critiques their examples of 'al-ilah' and 'al-shams' as universals, showing that in actual language use, these terms are particular, referring to the specific sun and Allah. He cites the Quranic verse to demonstrate that 'al-shams' is used as a known particular, not a universal. He argues that the very conception of these terms entails exclusivity, making them particulars, not universals. The passage then recounts the story of al-Khusrawshahi, a prominent follower of al-Razi, who confessed confusion about the universals and doubted his own beliefs. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that what logicians claim about universals, if it is knowledge, actually relies on analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), not on the inclusive logical syllogism they pretend. All examples used are from the Islamic tradition (Quran, theology) as required.

Key Terms
الكلي
الجزئي
قياس التمثيل
القياس المنطقي الشمولي

Checkpoint

A) That the universal is too abstract to exemplify
B) That they are following the correct method of reasoning
C) That their universals are mental constructs with no external reality
D) That the example must be universal, not particular

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demands that universals must have particular external instances to be genuine knowledge; otherwise they are mere mental constructs.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
6 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Complete Skepticism
Ibn Taymiyya defines sophistry and refutes the myth of complete skeptics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's definition of sophistry and reconstruct his argument against the existence of a school that denies all reality.

Core Idea
Sophistry is denial of truth, not a school of total skeptics.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya clarifies the meaning of sophistry (safsata) and dismantles the myth of a group that denies all realities. He shows that such a position is not only false but inconceivable for any living being. By understanding his critique, you will learn how to identify and reframe extreme skeptical claims using practical necessity as a logical tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of sophistry
Find where Ibn Taymiyya explicitly defines safsata as 'jahd al-haqq' (denial of truth). Note that he presents it as an attitude occurring in many souls, not a coherent school.
This is the core definition for the lesson.
2
Understand the origin of the term
Recognize that safsata is an Arabicized word from Greek 'sophistia', meaning 'colored wisdom'. This helps contextualize the term's history.
3
Recognize the target doctrine
Identify the claim that Ibn Taymiyya refutes: that there was a man named Sufasta and his followers who denied all realities and sciences.
This is the false story he attacks.
4
Analyze the refutation argument
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's argument: It is impossible for any human or animal to live while denying all realities because life requires basic distinctions (hunger, thirst, social interaction, etc.). This is an argument from practical necessity.
He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the claim were true, life would be impossible.
5
Evaluate the strength of the critique
Consider why Ibn Taymiyya considers the story false. The grounds are empirical and based on the necessities of life. This type of critique is foundational in refuting radical skepticism.
Think about whether this argument works against more limited forms of skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن السفسطة أمر يعرض لكثير من النفوس، وهي جَحْد الحق . وهي لفظة معرَّبة من اليونانية، أصلها «سُوفِسْطيا» ، أي حكمة مُمَوَّهة». فلما عُرّبت قيل : «سَفْسَطة» . وأما ما يذكره طائفة من أهل الكلام وناقلي المقالات أن في الناس رجل يقال له : سوفسطا»، وأنه وأصحابه ينكرون جميع الحقائق والعلوم، فهذا باطل لا حقيقة له. ولا يتصور أن يعيش أحد من بني آدم بل ولا من البهائم مع جحد جميع الحقائق والشعور بها. فإن الإنسان مدني بالطبع، فلا بد أن يعرف بعض الناس بعضاً. ويعرف الإنسان جوعه وشبعه، وعطشه ورِيَّه، ولذته وألمه، ويميز بين ما يأكله وما لا يأكله، وما يلبسه وما لا يلبسه، وبين مسكنه ومسكن جاره وبين الليل والنهار وغير ذلك من الأمور التي هي ضرورية في الحياة .
For sophistry (safsata) is a matter that occurs to many souls, and it is the denial of truth. It is an Arabicized word from the Greek, originally 'sūfisṭiā', i.e., 'colored wisdom'. When it was Arabicized, it was said: 'safsata'. As for what a group of speculative theologians and transmitters of doctrines mention—that there is a man called 'Sufasta' and that he and his followers deny all realities and sciences—this is false and has no reality. It is inconceivable that any human, nay even any animal, could live while denying all realities and the consciousness of them. For man is social by nature, so he must know some people from others. And a person knows his hunger and satiety, his thirst and quenching, his pleasure and pain, and distinguishes between what he eats and what he does not, between what he wears and what he does not, between his dwelling and his neighbor's dwelling, between night and day, and other matters that are necessary for life.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage defines sophistry (safsata) and refutes the claim of a school of sophists who deny all truth. Ibn Taymiyya first gives the linguistic definition: safsata is an Arabicization of the Greek 'sophistia', meaning 'colored wisdom', and it denotes the denial of truth. He then attacks the common story that there existed a man named Sufasta and his followers who denied all realities. This, he says, is impossible because no human—not even an animal—could live while rejecting all truths and sensory experience. Humans are social by nature and need to distinguish basic facts: hunger, thirst, pain, etc. Life itself requires acknowledging these realities. Therefore, the idea of complete denial is absurd.

Key Terms
سفسطة
جحد الحق
معربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A Greek philosophical school that denied all realities.
B) The use of fallacious arguments.

D) A type of logical fallacy in syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
Sophistry is the denial of truth, and the claim of a school that denies all reality is impossible because life itself requires recognizing basic truths.

Refining the Understanding of the Samaniyya's Position
Ibn Taymiyya corrects the claim that the Samaniyya deny all knowledge except sensory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Samaniyya's actual denial of imperceptible existence and the mistaken claim that they deny all non-sensory knowledge.

Core Idea
The Samaniyya didn't deny knowledge of the unseen—they denied the existence of what cannot be sensed.

In theological debates, some critics attribute extreme skepticism to the Indian Samaniyya sect, claiming they reject any knowledge beyond the senses. Ibn Taymiyya corrects this error: their denial was ontological, not epistemological. Understanding this nuance prevents misinterpretation of their position in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Find what is attributed to the Samaniyya: they deny all sciences except sensory ones, even mutawatir reports.
This is the claim Ibn Taymiyya refutes.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's correction
He says they only denied the existence (wujud) of things that cannot be sensed, not knowledge of them.
Key distinction: ontological vs. epistemological.
3
Examine the evidence cited
Ibn Taymiyya references Imam Ahmad's account of a debate with them to support his correction.
He uses historical authority.
4
Distinguish ontological from epistemological denial
The Samaniyya are empiricists about existence, not about knowledge. They accept knowledge of imperceptibles via reports, etc.
This nuance prevents straw-man refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك ما يذكرونه أن في السُّمَنِيَّة قوم ينكرون من العلوم ما سوی الحسيات حتى ينكروا «المتواترات» غَلَط على القوم . فإنهم أنكروا وجود ما لا يمكن الإحساس به ، من العلوم ما لم ينكروا وجودَ ما لا يُحِسُّونه هم. وقد ذكر الإمام أحمد مناظرتهم
And similarly, what they mention that among the Samaniyya (al-Sumaniyya) there are people who deny from the sciences what is other than sensory things, even to the point of denying 'mutawatirat' (mass-transmitted reports), is a mistake about these people. For they denied the existence of what cannot be perceived by the senses, but they did not deny knowledge of what they themselves do not sense. And Imam Ahmad has mentioned their disputation.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage refutes a common misrepresentation of the Samaniyya sect. Some claimed that the Samaniyya deny all knowledge except sensory perception, including mutawatir reports. Ibn Taymiyya corrects this: they only denied the existence of things that cannot be sensed at all—i.e., they were empiricists about existence, not about knowledge. They did not deny that they could know things they do not personally sense (e.g., through reports), but rather denied that imperceptible objects exist. He supports this by referencing Imam Ahmad's account of a debate with them.

Key Terms
سمنية
متواترات
حسيات

Checkpoint

A) All knowledge except sensory experience.
B) The existence of what cannot be perceived by the senses.
C) The validity of logical deduction.
D) The reality of the external world.

Key Takeaway
Accurate refutation requires knowing what an opponent actually claims, not just what is attributed to them.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
8 min
Sophistry as Partial Disease and the Dialectical Cure
Ibn Taymiyya argues sophistry afflicts only some people and proposes a dialectical cure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of universal sophistry and describe the dialectical method he proposes for curing it.

Core Idea
Sophistry is not total ignorance — it is a partial disease cured by Socratic questioning.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the notion that sophistry (safsata) can afflict someone to the point of denying all evident truths. He compares it to bodily diseases: no one is sick with every disease. Instead, sophistry affects only certain areas of knowledge. He then outlines a therapeutic approach using borrowed premises and examples, a method used by early Muslim scholars and ancient Greeks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Ibn Taymiyya targets the view that sophistry is universal—that someone could deny all sense experience and reports. He argues this never happens; people agree on basics like lands, rivers, and reports of unseen things.
2
Examine the disease analogy
He compares sophistry to bodily diseases: just as no one is sick with every disease, no one is ignorant of everything. Sophistry is partial and affects only some people in some matters.
This empirical observation grounds his critique.
3
Understand the proposed cure
Instead of using theoretical proofs, he recommends a step-by-step questioning method. The questioner borrows premises from the sophist, getting him to admit to intermediate truths that lead to the desired conclusion.
This is the method of early Muslim scholars and ancient Greeks.
4
Recognize the use of examples
Ibn Taymiyya illustrates with an arithmetic error: a person who denies a calculation can be led through intermediate steps ("you took this and this, so it became such"). Similarly, examples and analogies can reveal truth even in sensory matters.
5
Distinguish the questioner's role
In this dialectical method, the arguer (mustadill) is the questioner, not the objector. He asks "What do you say about such and such?" and builds premises until the sophist cannot deny the conclusion.
This reverses the usual jadal roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يُتصوّر أن أحدهم ينكر ما كان قبل مولده، فلا ينكر وجود البلاد والأنهار والجبال والدور التي لم يرها؛ ولا ينكر وجود كل إنسان أو بهيمة لم يرها . فإن هذا ليس عليه أحد من بني آدم، بل بنو آدم كلُّهم متفقون على أن ما شاهدوه عَلِموه بالمشاهدة، وما غاب عنهم علموه بالأخبار. فلا يتصور أن كل طائفة من الطوائف تتفق على جحد جنس الأخبار . ولكن قد تعرض السَّفْسَطة لبعض الطوائف ولبعض الأشخاص في بعض المعارف، فإن أمراض القلوب كأمراض الأجسام. فكما أنها ليس في الوجود أمة ولا شخص يمرض بكل مرض، فليس فيهم من هو جاهل بكل شيء، وفاسد الاعتقاد في كل شيء، وفاسدُ القصد في كل شيء. بل قد يوجد فيهم من هو مريض ببعض الأمراض، بل قد يوجد بعضُ الطوائف يكثر فيهم بعض الأمراض. وهؤلاء المرضى لا ينتفعون بالأغذية الفطرية، بل يحتاجون إلى علاج وأدوية تُناسب مزاجهم . وكذلك من كان به سفسطة ومرضت فطرتُه في بعض المعارف لا يستعمل معه الأدلة النظرية ، بل يستعمل معه نوع من العلاج والأدوية. فقد تكون الحدود والأدلة التي تُخوجه إلى النظر والفكر إذا تصوّرها مقدمة مقدمة مما يزيل سفسطته وتُخوِجه إلى الاعتراف بالحق. وهذا بمنزلة من يغلط في الحساب والحساب لا يحتمل وجهين . وقد يكون غلطه ظاهراً وهو لا يعرفه، أو لا يعترف به . فيُسلك معه طريق طويل يعرف بها الحق ويقال له أخذت كذا، وأخذت كذا فصار كذا؛ وأخذت كذا، وأخذت كذا، فصار كذا . وكذلك المناظر . قد تُضرب له الأمثالُ، فإن المثال يكشف الحال حتى في المعلومات بالحس والبديهة. وقد تُسْتَسْلَفُ معه المقدّماتُ، وإلا فقد يجحد إذا عَرَف أنه يلزمه الاعتراف بما ينكره. وهي طريقة المتقدمين من نظار المسلمين وقدماء اليونان المناظرة. يكون المستدل هو السائلُ، لا المعترضُ. فيستسلف المقدمات، ويقول: ما تقول في كذا وفي كذا؟ أو يقول : لِيُبين كذا وكذا مقدمة مقدمة، فإذا اعترف بتلك المقدمات بين ما تستلزمه من النتائج المطلوبة . فيجب الفرق بين ما تقف معرفة الحق عليه وتحتاج إليه وبين ما يُعرف الحق بدونه، ولكن قد يُزال به بعض الأمراض ويقطع به بعضُ المعاندين، والله سبحانة أعلم .
So it is not conceivable that any one of them denies what existed before his birth; he does not deny the existence of lands, rivers, mountains, and houses he has not seen; nor does he deny the existence of any human or animal he has not seen. For no son of Adam does this; rather, all sons of Adam agree that they know what they have witnessed through observation, and what is absent from them they know through reports. So it is not conceivable that every group of groups agrees to reject the entire class of reports. But sophistry may affect some groups and some persons in some areas of knowledge, for diseases of the hearts are like diseases of bodies. Just as there does not exist a nation or a person afflicted with every disease, so among them there is no one who is ignorant of everything, corrupt in belief about everything, and corrupt in intention about everything. Rather, among them one may find the one who is sick with some diseases, and among some groups some diseases may be prevalent. Those sick people do not benefit from natural nourishments, but need treatment and medicines suited to their temperament. Likewise, the one with sophistry whose innate disposition is diseased in some areas of knowledge, theoretical proofs should not be used with him; rather, a type of treatment and medicine should be used with him. The definitions and proofs that lead him to reflection and thought, if he conceives them premise by premise, may remove his sophistry and lead him to acknowledge the truth. This is like one who errs in arithmetic, and arithmetic does not admit two interpretations. His error may be apparent while he does not know it, or he does not admit it. So a long path is taken with him to know the truth, and it is said to him: you took this and this, so it became such; and you took this and this, so it became such. And likewise the disputant. Examples may be struck for him, for the example reveals the situation even in matters known by sense and intuition. And premises may be borrowed from him, otherwise he might deny when he knows that it necessitates admitting what he denies. This is the method of the early Muslim scholars and ancient Greek disputants: the arguer is the questioner, not the objector. He borrows premises and says: What do you say about such and such? Or he says: Let him explain such and such, premise by premise. When he acknowledges those premises, he demonstrates what results they entail of the required conclusions. So one must distinguish between that on which knowledge of truth depends and is needed, and that by which truth is known without it, but it may remove some diseases and cut off some obstinate people. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' assumption that sophistry is universal and can only be cured by logical demonstration. Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing that it is not conceivable for any rational person to deny all reports or all absent things, because even idol worshippers accept the existence of unseen places through testimony. Thus, no group agrees to reject all reports. However, sophistry (safsata) does affect some people in some matters, analogous to diseases of the body. Just as no one is diseased in every way, no one is ignorant of everything. Those affected need a tailored treatment, not pure theoretical proofs. The cure involves a step-by-step questioning method (istisalaf al-muqaddimat) where the questioner leads the sophist through accepted premises to force an admission. This is the method of early Muslims and ancient Greeks, where the arguer is the questioner, not the objector. Examples and analogies are useful even for sense-perceptible matters. The key is to distinguish between the essential path to truth and auxiliary treatments that remove obstinacy.

Key Terms
السفسطة
المقدمات
المستدل
المعترض
الحدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Use theoretical proofs and syllogisms
B) Use step-by-step questioning and examples

D) Use force to make them admit truth

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines sophistry as a partial, curable condition and prescribes a dialectical method of borrowed premises and examples, not abstract syllogisms.

Why Philosophers Disagree More Than Ahl al-Hadith: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the unity of Ahl al-Hadith with the excessive disagreement of the philosophers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that disagreement is a sign of distance from prophetic guidance, and apply this criterion to evaluate competing claims to truth in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Disagreement is a symptom of distance from revelation, not a sign of intellectual independence.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin argues that the level of disagreement among groups is inversely proportional to their adherence to the Prophet's teachings. He contrasts the unity of Ahl al-Hadith with the rampant disagreements of the philosophers, challenging the notion that rational methods alone guarantee consensus. You will learn to recognize this empirical argument and consider its implications for evaluating religious claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic evidence
Note the three verses Ibn Taymiyya cites commanding unity and forbidding division. What is the divine mandate?
2
Trace the correlation
How does Ibn Taymiyya link closeness to the Prophet with reduced disagreement? Identify the groups he ranks.
3
Examine the critique of philosophers
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the disagreements of the philosophers? Why is this significant in his broader attack on logic?
4
Assess the argument's structure
Is Ibn Taymiyya offering a logical proof or an empirical observation? How does he use the Quran to support his claim?
5
Apply the criterion
How would this principle (less disagreement indicates more truth) be used to evaluate other groups?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف

وقد أمر الله بالجماعة والائتلاف ونهى عن الفرقة والاختلاف فقال تعالى: ﴿وَاعْتَصِمُوا بِحَبْلِ اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا وَلَا تَفَرَّقُوا﴾ [سورة آل عمران: ١٠٣] وقال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ فَرَّقُوا دِينَهُمْ وَكَانُوا شِيَعًا لَسْتَ مِنْهُمْ فِي شَيْءٍ﴾ [سورة الأنعام: ١٥٩] وقال: ﴿وَلَا تَكُونُوا كَالَّذِينَ تَفَرَّقُوا وَاخْتَلَفُوا مِنْ بَعْدِ مَا جَاءَهُمُ الْبَيِّنَاتُ﴾ [سورة آل عمران: ١٠٥] وقد أخبر أن أهل الرحمة لا يختلفون فقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَزَالُونَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ إِلَّا مَن رَّحِمَ رَبُّكَ﴾ [سورة هود: ١١٨-١١٩]

ولهذا يوجد أتبع الناس للرسول أقلهم اختلافاً كأهل الحديث والسنة فإنهم أقل الطوائف اختلافاً ثم من كان إليهم أقرب من جميع الطوائف المنتسبة إلى الحديث والسنة كانوا أقل اختلافاً فأما من بعد عن السنة كالمعتزلة والرافضة فتجدهم أكثر الطوائف اختلافاً

كثرة اختلاف الفلاسفة

وأما اختلاف الفلاسفة فلا يحصره أحد وقد ذكر أبو الحسن الأشعري في كتاب مقالات غير الإسلاميين عنهم من المقالات ما لم يذكر الفارابي وابن
Disagreement. And God commanded unity and harmony and forbade division and disagreement. He said: {And hold fast to the rope of God all together and do not separate} [Al Imran: 103]. And He said: {Indeed, those who divided their religion and became sects, you are not with them in anything} [Al-An'am: 159]. And He said: {And do not be like those who separated and differed after the clear proofs had come to them} [Al Imran: 105]. And He informed that the people of mercy do not differ. He said: {And they will not cease to differ except those upon whom your Lord has mercy} [Hud: 118-119]. For this reason, the most following of the Messenger are the least in disagreement, like the people of hadith and sunna, for they are the least of the sects in disagreement. Then those who are closer to them among all the sects attributed to hadith and sunna are less in disagreement. As for those who are far from the sunna, like the Mu'tazila and Rafida, you find them the most of the sects in disagreement. The abundance of the disagreement of the philosophers. And as for the disagreement of the philosophers, no one can enumerate it. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari mentioned in his book 'Articles of the Non-Muslims' about them articles that al-Farabi and Ibn
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a critique of disagreement, particularly aimed at the philosophers. Ibn Taymiyya begins by citing Quranic verses commanding unity and forbidding division. He then observes a correlation: the most faithful followers of the Prophet, specifically Ahl al-Hadith, are the least divided. Those closer to them are also relatively united, while those farther from the sunna, like the Mu'tazila and Rafida, are more divided. Finally, he turns to the philosophers, stating that their disagreements are so vast they cannot be counted, and he references al-Ash'ari's work that contains more differing opinions of the philosophers than even al-Farabi and Ibn Sina mentioned. This serves to undermine the claim that logic, developed by philosophers, is a reliable tool for achieving truth and unity.

الاختلاف
أهل الحديث
المعتزلة
الرافضة
الفلاسفة

Checkpoint

A) The Mu'tazila
B) The philosophers
C) Ahl al-Hadith
D) The Rafida

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses the observable fact of disagreement among philosophers to undermine their claim to epistemic authority, arguing that unity is a mark of truth and derives from adherence to revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify a Quranic verse that commands unity (other than those cited) and explain how Ibn Taymiyya might use it to support his argument.

Why Syllogism Fails for Allah
Critique of applying syllogism to Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against applying the logical syllogism to Allah and evaluate its internal validity.

Core Idea
The syllogism yields only universals, not the unique reality of Allah.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assumption that the categorical syllogism can provide certain knowledge about any subject, including the divine. In this passage, he argues that when the subject is unique and has no equal, the syllogism fails to deliver specific knowledge. Let's dissect his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targeted syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya refers to 'the syllogism whose individuals are equal and the branch resembles its origin.' This is the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) used by logicians. Note that he is not rejecting all forms of reasoning, only this specific type when applied to Allah.
The term 'qiyas' here is the Aristotelian syllogism, not analogy in usul al-fiqh.
2
Locate the key premise
The foundational premise is: 'Allah has no equal (la mithla lahu).' This means there is nothing that resembles Allah in His essence or attributes. The syllogism requires a middle term that applies equally to the subject and other things, but Allah's uniqueness breaks this requirement.
This is a theological axiom derived from the Quran and Sunnah.
3
Understand why the syllogism produces only a universal
Ibn Taymiyya states that applying the syllogism to Allah yields 'nothing but a universal matter shared between Him and others.' Because the syllogism works by finding a common attribute, it cannot capture what is unique to Allah. The conclusion is always a general concept (e.g., 'exists,' 'powerful'), not Allah's specific reality.
For example, 'Allah is powerful' is a universal truth shared with some created beings; it does not distinguish His power.
4
Grasp the conclusion: specification requires other knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya argues that even if the universal's species is restricted to a single individual (i.e., the concept applies only to Allah), the syllogism still does not give specific knowledge of that individual. To know Allah's essence, one needs additional knowledge beyond the syllogism—revelation or direct recognition.
This refutes the claim that syllogism alone can provide complete knowledge of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «القياس» الذي تستوي أفراده ويماثل الفرعُ فيه أصله، فهذا يمتنع استعماله في حق الله تعالى فإن الله لا مِثْلَ له سبحانه وتعالى . وإذا استعمل فيه مثل هذا القياس في حق لم يفد إلا أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، لا يدل على ما يختص به الرب سبحانه، إلا أن يُضَم إليه علم آخر. فإن هذا الكلي الذي هو مدلول «القياس» قد انحصر نوعه في شخصه، وهذا أيضاً لا يفيد التعيين، بل لا بد في التعيين من علم آخر .
As for the syllogism (qiyas) whose individuals are equal and in which the branch resembles its origin, its use in the case of Allah is impossible, for Allah has no equal, glorified and exalted is He. And if such a syllogism is used in His case, it yields nothing but a universal matter shared between Him and others, not indicating what is specific to the Lord, unless another knowledge is attached to it. For this universal, which is the signification of the syllogism, its species is restricted to its individual, and this also does not benefit specification; rather, specification requires another knowledge.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage delivers a sharp critique of applying the logical syllogism to matters concerning Allah. Ibn Taymiyya states that the syllogism (qiyas) in which the individuals are equal and the branch resembles its origin (the standard categorical syllogism) cannot be used for Allah because Allah has no equal or analogy. The problem is that such a syllogism only produces a universal concept that is shared between Allah and other beings, which fails to capture what is unique to Allah. Even if the universal's species is restricted to a single individual (i.e., the concept applies only to Allah), the syllogism still does not give specific knowledge; it only indicates a commonality. Therefore, any specific knowledge of Allah requires additional information beyond the syllogism. This argument targets the logicians' claim that syllogisms can yield certain knowledge about all subjects, including the divine.

Key Terms
القياس
الكلي

C) Because it is too complex for religious matters
D) Because it is prohibited by the Quran

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the categorical syllogism, by design, produces only universal concepts, making it incapable of conveying the unique and specific reality of Allah.

Does it give knowledge of the specific grade of weakness (e.g., munkar or shadh)? Why or why not? Compare to Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Ibn Sina's One-Cause Principle
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Ibn Sina's argument for the One Cause by tracing its origin to Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the origins and logical structure of Ibn Sina's principle that from the One only one proceeds, and explain Ibn Taymiyya's historical and philosophical critique.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's 'One cause only one effect' is not Peripatetic but borrowed from Mu'tazili theology.

Ibn Sina argued that if two effects proceeded from the One, the One would be composed. Ibn Taymiyya traces this argument to the Mu'tazila, not to Aristotle. Understanding this historical pedigree reveals the theological stakes of the debate: does God's simplicity preclude multiple acts? This lesson unpacks the critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target argument
Ibn Sina's argument that if two effects proceed from one cause, the cause would have composition, violating unity. Locate this in the Arabic.
2
Extract the historical attribution
Ibn Taymiyya says this argument was taken from the Mu'tazila. Identify the phrase 'أخذوها المعتزلة'.
This is a key move: delegitimizing by foreign origin.
3
Distinguish philosophical schools
Note the contrast between Peripatetics (مشائون) and earlier great philosophers (أساطين). The latter affirmed attributes.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show the argument is not from the ancient philosophical consensus.
4
Assess the critique's force
Does attributing the argument to Mu'tazila invalidate it? Consider whether origin affects truth.
Ibn Taymiyya is making an historical argument, not a logical refutation of the argument itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما احتجاج ابن سينا وأمثاله بأنه لو صدر اثنان لكان مصدر هذا غير مصدر حجة التركيب هذا، ولزم التركيب المنافي للوحدة، فهذه الحجة تبع الحجة التركيب. وهذه أخذوها المعتزلة من أهل الكلام من المعتزلة ونحوهم، ليست هذه من كلام أئمة الفلاسفة كأرسطو وأتباعه، لا سيما أكثر أساطين الفلاسفة. فإنهم كانوا يقولون بإثبات الصفات لله تعالى، بل وبقيام الأمور الاختيارية، كما ذكرنا كلامهم في غير هذا الموضع وهو اختيار أبي البركات صاحب «المعتبر». ومن قال إنه خالف أكثر الفلاسفة فمراده المشائين. وأما الأساطين قبل هؤلاء فكلام كثير منهم يدل على هذا الأصل، كما هو مذكور في موضعه.
As for the argument of Ibn Sina and his likes that if two things were to proceed, the source of this would not be the source of that—this is the argument of composition, and it would necessitate composition contradictory to unity—this argument follows the argument of composition. And this they took from the Mu'tazila among the theologians from the Mu'tazila and their ilk; it is not from the speech of the leading philosophers like Aristotle and his followers, especially not from the majority of the great philosophers. For they used to affirm the attributes of Allah, and even the occurrence of voluntary matters, as we mentioned their speech elsewhere, and it is the choice of Abu al-Barakat, author of 'al-Mu'tabar'. And whoever says that he contradicted the majority of philosophers means the Peripatetics. As for the great philosophers before these, much of their speech indicates this principle, as is mentioned in its place.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of Ibn Sina's argument for the principle 'from the One only one proceeds.' Ibn Taymiyya identifies the argument as based on the notion that if two effects proceeded from one source, there would be composition in the source, negating its unity. He asserts that this argument was borrowed from the Mu'tazili theologians, not from the founding philosophers like Aristotle. He then notes that the great philosophers (asateen) actually affirmed that God has attributes and that voluntary actions can proceed from Him, citing Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi's view. Those who think Abu al-Barakat contradicted the majority are only referring to the Peripatetics; the earlier philosophers held a different view. Ibn Taymiyya thus delegitimizes Ibn Sina's argument by showing its foreign origin and its inconsistency with the broader philosophical tradition.

Key Terms
التركيب (al-tarkīb): Composition; the idea that a cause with multiple effects would be composed of parts, violating unity.
الأساطين (al-asātīn): The great or founding philosophers, older than Aristotle, who held different views about divine attributes.
المشائون (al-mashshā'ūn): The Peripatetics (Aristotelians) who adhered to the principle that from one only one proceeds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Aristotle and his followers.
B) The ancient philosophers (asateen).
C) The Mu'tazila theologians.
D) Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya delegitimizes Ibn Sina's principle by showing it derives from Mu'tazili kalam, not from the ancient philosophical tradition.

First Reason: Divine Balances Precede Greek Logic
First chronological argument against equating mizan with Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's chronological argument that the revealed mizan cannot be Greek logic and apply it to evaluate claims about the origins of Islamic reasoning methods.

Core Idea
Greek logic was invented long after the prophets; it cannot be the revealed balance.

Ibn Taymiyya opens his refutation with a simple but powerful argument from historical priority. He points out that God sent down balances (mawāzīn) with His books from the time of Noah, Abraham, and Moses — long before Aristotle ever wrote the Organon. If the revealed mizan were identical to Greek logic, how could earlier nations have used it? This lesson walks you through the first reason and shows how chronological precedence is used as a critical tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine under attack
Ibn Taymiyya is attacking the claim that the 'mizan' (balance) mentioned in the Quran (e.g., 55:7-9, 42:17) is the same as Aristotelian logic. Find the statement of this claim in the passage.
The passage begins by stating 'the falsity of the claim that the revealed balance is the logic of the Greeks'.
2
Extract the chronological data
Ibn Taymiyya provides a timeline: God revealed balances with earlier scriptures before the Greeks existed, specifically mentioning Noah, Abraham, and Moses. Aristotelian logic was invented by Aristotle 300 years before Christ. Note the gap.
This is a historical rather than logical argument.
3
Formulate the impossibility
If the divine mizan were identical to Greek logic, earlier nations would have had to use a system that did not yet exist. That is impossible. So the two cannot be the same.
This is a reductio ad absurdum based on chronology.
4
Evaluate the strength of the argument
Consider: does this argument refute the claim definitively? What assumptions does it rely on? For instance, it assumes that earlier nations possessed the same rational balance, which Ibn Taymiyya elsewhere affirms.
Ibn Taymiyya is not rejecting rationality, only the exclusive identification with Greek logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان الزعم أن الميزان المنزل هو منطق اليونان. لا يجوز لعاقل أن يظن أن الميزان العقلي الذي أنزله الله هو منطق اليونان لوجوه: أحدها: إن الله أنزل الموازين مع كتبه قبل أن يخلق اليونان من عهد نوح وإبراهيم وموسى وغيرهم، وهذا المنطق اليوناني وضعه أرسطو قبل المسيح بثلاث مئة سنة. فكيف كانت الأمم المتقدمة تزن به؟ الثاني: إن أمتنا أهل الإسلام ما زالوا يزنون بالموازين العقلية ولم يسمع سلفنا بذكر هذا المنطق اليوناني، وإنما ظهر في الإسلام لما عرّبت الكتب الرومية في دولة المأمون أو قريباً منها. الثالث: إنه ما زال نظار المسلمين بعد أن عُرّب وعرفوه يعيبونه ويذمونه، ولا يلتفتون إليه ولا إلى أهله في موازينهم العقلية والشرعية. ولا يقول القائل ليس فيه مما انفردوا به إلا اصطلاحات لفظية، وإلا فالمعاني العقلية مشتركة بين الأمم. فإنه ليس الأمر كذلك، بل فيه معان كثيرة فاسدة.
The falsity of the claim that the revealed balance is the logic of the Greeks. No intelligent person may think that the rational balance which God revealed is the logic of the Greeks, for several reasons: First, God revealed balances with His books before He created the Greeks, from the time of Noah, Abraham, Moses, and others, while this Greek logic was invented by Aristotle three hundred years before Christ. So how could the earlier nations have weighed with it? Second, our nation, the people of Islam, have always weighed with rational balances, and our predecessors never heard of this Greek logic; it only appeared in Islam when the Roman books were translated in the time of al-Ma'mun or close to it. Third, the scholars of Islam ever since it was translated and they became acquainted with it have criticized and condemned it, and they do not pay attention to it or its proponents in their rational and legal balances. And let no one say that the only unique thing in it is verbal terminology, and otherwise the rational meanings are common among all nations. For that is not the case; rather, it contains many corrupt meanings.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the claim that the revealed 'balance' (mizan) mentioned in the Quran is identical to Aristotelian logic. He provides three clear reasons. First, the divine balances were revealed with the Scriptures long before the Greeks existed, e.g., from Noah, Abraham, and Moses. Aristotelian logic was invented by Aristotle only about 300 years before Christ, so it could not have been used by earlier prophets and nations who also used sound reasoning. Second, the Muslim community (early generations) always used rational balances without needing Greek logic; it only entered Islamic civilization after the translation movement under Caliph al-Ma'mun. Thus, it was not part of the original Islamic intellectual apparatus. Third, after it was translated, Muslim scholars (nuzzar) criticized and condemned it, refusing to rely on it for their rational and legal reasoning. Then Ibn Taymiyya anticipates an objection: someone might say that Greek logic contains only new terminology, not new meanings, since rational concepts are universal. He rejects this, asserting that Greek logic actually contains many corrupt concepts (ma'ani fāsida). This underscores that the problem is not merely linguistic but substantive.

Key Terms
الميزان
منطق اليونان
أرسطو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why could the earlier nations not have used Greek logic as their balance?
A) They were illiterate.
B) Greek logic was invented after their time.
C) They used only revelation.
D) Greek logic was unknown to them until modern times.

Key Takeaway
The chronological priority of divine revelation over Greek invention proves that the revealed mizan cannot be identical to Aristotelian logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar chronological argument against the claim that the method of qiyas (analogy) in usul al-fiqh is borrowed from Greek logic. Use the fact that the Companions used qiyas before encountering Greek thought.

Second Reason: Early Muslims Never Used Greek Logic
Second argument based on early Muslim practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second historical argument of Ibn Taymiyya based on the practice of early Muslims and use it to assess the originality of Islamic reasoning methods.

Core Idea
The early Muslim community reasoned correctly without ever hearing of Greek logic.

Ibn Taymiyya's second reason is empirical: the early generations of Muslims (salaf) engaged in sophisticated rational and legal reasoning (e.g., in fiqh, tafsir, and kalam) without any knowledge of Aristotelian logic. The system only entered the Islamic world after the translation movement under Caliph al-Ma'mun. This lesson explores the implications of this historical fact for the claim of identity between the revealed mizan and Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
State the second reason concisely
Ibn Taymiyya says that the Muslim community (umma) has always used rational balances (mawāzīn 'aqliyya) and the predecessors (salaf) never heard of Greek logic. It only appeared after translations in the time of al-Ma'mun.
This is an argument from precedent: what was absent in the best generations cannot be essential.
2
Contrast the two periods
Note the contrast: Islamic reasoning flourished for centuries before Greek logic was introduced. The salaf produced valid inferences and legal rulings without it.
This implies that Greek logic is not necessary for correct reasoning.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The argument relies on the principle that if the revealed mizan were Greek logic, the earliest and most knowledgeable Muslims would have known and used it. Their ignorance of it shows the two are distinct.
Similar to the first reason but focuses on Islamic history rather than pre-Islamic prophets.
4
Consider the objection Ibn Taymiyya addresses later
Ibn Taymiyya anticipates that someone might say Greek logic only contains new terminology, not new meanings, and that rational meanings are universal. He will reject this in the third reason.
This lesson focuses only on the historical absence argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان الزعم أن الميزان المنزل هو منطق اليونان. لا يجوز لعاقل أن يظن أن الميزان العقلي الذي أنزله الله هو منطق اليونان لوجوه: أحدها: إن الله أنزل الموازين مع كتبه قبل أن يخلق اليونان من عهد نوح وإبراهيم وموسى وغيرهم، وهذا المنطق اليوناني وضعه أرسطو قبل المسيح بثلاث مئة سنة. فكيف كانت الأمم المتقدمة تزن به؟ الثاني: إن أمتنا أهل الإسلام ما زالوا يزنون بالموازين العقلية ولم يسمع سلفنا بذكر هذا المنطق اليوناني، وإنما ظهر في الإسلام لما عرّبت الكتب الرومية في دولة المأمون أو قريباً منها. الثالث: إنه ما زال نظار المسلمين بعد أن عُرّب وعرفوه يعيبونه ويذمونه، ولا يلتفتون إليه ولا إلى أهله في موازينهم العقلية والشرعية. ولا يقول القائل ليس فيه مما انفردوا به إلا اصطلاحات لفظية، وإلا فالمعاني العقلية مشتركة بين الأمم. فإنه ليس الأمر كذلك، بل فيه معان كثيرة فاسدة.
The falsity of the claim that the revealed balance is the logic of the Greeks. No intelligent person may think that the rational balance which God revealed is the logic of the Greeks, for several reasons: First, God revealed balances with His books before He created the Greeks, from the time of Noah, Abraham, Moses, and others, while this Greek logic was invented by Aristotle three hundred years before Christ. So how could the earlier nations have weighed with it? Second, our nation, the people of Islam, have always weighed with rational balances, and our predecessors never heard of this Greek logic; it only appeared in Islam when the Roman books were translated in the time of al-Ma'mun or close to it. Third, the scholars of Islam ever since it was translated and they became acquainted with it have criticized and condemned it, and they do not pay attention to it or its proponents in their rational and legal balances. And let no one say that the only unique thing in it is verbal terminology, and otherwise the rational meanings are common among all nations. For that is not the case; rather, it contains many corrupt meanings.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the claim that the revealed 'balance' (mizan) mentioned in the Quran is identical to Aristotelian logic. He provides three clear reasons. First, the divine balances were revealed with the Scriptures long before the Greeks existed, e.g., from Noah, Abraham, and Moses. Aristotelian logic was invented by Aristotle only about 300 years before Christ, so it could not have been used by earlier prophets and nations who also used sound reasoning. Second, the Muslim community (early generations) always used rational balances without needing Greek logic; it only entered Islamic civilization after the translation movement under Caliph al-Ma'mun. Thus, it was not part of the original Islamic intellectual apparatus. Third, after it was translated, Muslim scholars (nuzzar) criticized and condemned it, refusing to rely on it for their rational and legal reasoning. Then Ibn Taymiyya anticipates an objection: someone might say that Greek logic contains only new terminology, not new meanings, since rational concepts are universal. He rejects this, asserting that Greek logic actually contains many corrupt concepts (ma'ani fāsida). This underscores that the problem is not merely linguistic but substantive.

السلف
الموازين العقلية
المأمون

When did Greek logic appear in the Islamic world according to Ibn Taymiyya?
A) From the time of the Prophet
B) After the translation movement under al-Ma'mun
C) During the Umayyad dynasty
D) In the 4th century AH

Key Takeaway
The historical absence of Greek logic among the salaf proves that the revealed balance is a different, indigenous method of reasoning.

Third Reason: Muslim Scholars Unanimously Rejected Greek Logic
Third argument: scholarly rejection and response to counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument from scholarly consensus and evaluate his rebuttal to the 'mere terminology' objection.

Core Idea
Muslim scholars have consistently criticized Greek logic, rejecting both its terminology and its content.

Ibn Taymiyya's third reason is based on the reception of Greek logic by Muslim scholars (nuzzār). He claims that after translation, the scholars of Islam criticised and condemned it, refusing to rely on it in their rational and legal balances. He then anticipates and refutes a potential objection: that the only novelty in Greek logic is verbal terminology, while the underlying rational meanings are universal. Ibn Taymiyya firmly denies this, asserting that Greek logic contains many corrupt meanings (ma'āni fāsida). This lesson dissects the structure of this argument and its substantive critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about scholarly criticism
Ibn Taymiyya says the nuzzār (speculative theologians) since the translation have consistently criticized and condemned Greek logic, not paying attention to it in their own reasoning.
This is an appeal to authority: the consensus of qualified scholars.
2
Anticipate the objection
Ibn Taymiyya raises a possible counter: someone might argue that the only unique thing in Greek logic is its terminology, while the rational concepts are common to all humans.
This objection tries to minimize the difference between the mizan and Greek logic.
3
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the objection outright, saying 'it is not the case'; he asserts that Greek logic contains many corrupt meanings beyond mere terminology.
This moves the critique from terminological to substantive.
4
Evaluate the force of the argument
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya offers evidence for the 'corrupt meanings' here. He does not elaborate in this passage, but elsewhere in his works he details the faults (e.g., category mistakes, flawed syllogistic rules).
The argument relies on the reader's familiarity with his broader critique.

For that is not the case; rather, it contains many corrupt meanings.
