Distinguishing Efficient Cause from Condition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that efficient cause cannot be simultaneous with effect, using hand/ring, sound/movement, sun/shine examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between an efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'ila) and a condition (al-shart), and use it to critique claims of simultaneous causation.

Core Idea
An efficient cause must precede its effect; simultaneity indicates only a condition, not a cause.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the common philosophical assumption that cause and effect can coexist in time. Through concrete examples, he shows that what appears to be simultaneous causation is actually a condition-effect relationship. This lesson dissects his argument and its implications for Islamic theology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The passage targets the idea that an efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'ila) can be simultaneous with its effect. Find where Ibn Taymiyya states this clearly.
Look at the end of the Arabic: 'فبطل أن يكون في الوجود سبب يقارن مسببه في الزمان'
2
Analyze the hand-ring example
Notice that the movement of the hand and ring are not two separate movements; they are one movement due to their physical connection. No efficient cause exists between them.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show that apparent 'cause' is actually just participation in a single action.
3
Extract the cause-condition distinction
Contrast the efficient cause (must precede) with the condition (can be simultaneous). The hand's movement is a condition for the ring's movement, not its cause.
The Arabic distinguishes 'علة فاعلة' from 'شرط'.
4
Apply the distinction to other examples
Sound follows movement but movement is not its efficient cause; the sun's ray appears with the sun but the sun is only a condition. Confirm that these fit the condition model.
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'والشمس شرط في الشعاع والحركة شرط في الصوت'
5
Conclude the principle
Summarize: no efficient cause can be simultaneous with its effect; only conditions can. The term 'cause' ('illa) is ambiguous and must be clarified.
This refutes the philosophers' claim that the First Cause can coexist with its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حركة اليد والخاتم متلازمة. ليس هنا حركتان إحداهما سبب الأخرى، فضلاً عن أن تكون فاعلة لها. ومن قال في مثل هذا : «حركتُ يده فتحرك خاتمه فإنما هو كقوله فتحركت «اليد». فإنما يريد بذلك أن الحركة شملت الجميع، ولم يرد بذلك أن حركة اليد كانت سبباً للحركة الأولى. ولا نعرف عاقلاً يقول هذا ويقصد هذا. وإن قدر أنه وجد فليس قوله حجة على سائر العقلاء. ومن رفع يد ميت أو نائم وفيها خاتم لم تكن حركةُ بعض ذلك سبباً لبعض، بل الجميع موجود في زمان واحد لفاعل واحد وسبب واحد . فبطل أن يكون في الوجود سبب يقارن مسببه في الزمان، بل لا يكون إلا قبله. لا يقارن السبب مسببه فكيف بالفاعل المستقل؟ وإنما الذي يقارن الشيء في الزمان شرطه . وتقدم الواحد على الاثنين في الزمان هو من هذا الباب، لا من باب تقدم الشرط على المشروط . وحركة اليد وحركة الخاتم مع اليد هي من باب المشروط مع الشرط، ليست من باب المفعول مع الفاعل . ولكن لفظ «العلة» فيه إجمال . وكذلك الصوت مع الحركة فإن الصوت يحدث عقب الحركة، وغايته أن يكون معها كالجزء الثاني من الحركة مع الأول والحركة المتصلة، والزمان المتصل، ليس بعضه مع بعض في الزمان فغاية الصوت مع الحركة أن تكون كذلك. وكذلك الشعاع مع ظهور الشمس، مع أن الشمس ليست فاعلة للشعاع، بل الشعاع يحدث في الأرض إذا قابل الشمس ما ينعكس الشعاعُ عليه. وكذلك الحركة ليست هي الفاعلة للصوت ولكن الشمس شرط في الشعاع والحركة شرط في الصوت . وأما فاعل يُبدع مفعوله ويكون مقارناً له في الزمان فهذا لا يوجد قط . الفرق بين العلة التي هي شرط شيء والعلة التي هي شرط العلة الفاعلة شيء، والشرط قد يقارن المشروط في زمانه بخلاف الفاعل فإنه لا بد أن يتقدم فعله على المعين .
The movement of the hand and the ring are concomitant. There are not two movements, one being the cause of the other, let alone being its efficient cause. Whoever says in such a case, 'I moved my hand so his ring moved' is just like saying 'so the hand moved.' He only means that the movement encompassed both, not that the movement of the hand was the cause of the first movement. We do not know any rational person who says this intending that meaning. If it is supposed that such a person exists, his statement is not a proof against other rational people. And if one raises the hand of a dead or sleeping person, and there is a ring on it, the movement of one part is not the cause of the other; rather, both exist in one time due to one agent and one cause. Thus it is false that there exists in existence a cause that is simultaneous with its effect in time; rather, it can only be before it. The cause is not simultaneous with its effect, so how about the independent agent? That which accompanies something in time is only its condition. The priority of one over two in time falls under this category, not under the category of the priority of the condition over the conditioned. The movement of the hand and the movement of the ring with the hand fall under the category of the conditioned with the condition, not under the category of the effect with the agent. But the word 'cause' is ambiguous. Similarly, sound and movement: sound occurs after movement, and at most it is with it like the second part of movement with the first, and continuous movement and continuous time; not all of it is with a part in time, so the utmost of sound with movement is like that. Similarly, the ray with the appearance of the sun, although the sun is not the efficient cause of the ray; rather, the ray occurs on earth when it faces what reflects the ray. And movement is not the efficient cause of sound, but the sun is a condition for the ray and movement is a condition for sound. As for an agent that creates its effect and is simultaneous with it in time, this does not exist at all. The difference between the cause that is a condition for something and the cause that is an efficient cause: the condition may be simultaneous with the conditioned in its time, unlike the agent, for its action must precede the determinate.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya against the notion that an efficient cause can be simultaneous with its effect. He begins with the example of moving a hand with a ring: the movement of hand and ring are not two separate movements where one causes the other; rather, they are a single movement encompassing both due to their connection. If someone says 'I moved my hand so his ring moved,' they mean the movement covered both, not that hand movement caused ring movement. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that no rational person intends a causal relationship here; if such a person existed, their statement would not be evidence. He then illustrates with a dead or sleeping person's hand: raising it moves the ring, but one part is not cause of another; both occur simultaneously from a single agent and cause. This leads to the conclusion: a cause cannot be simultaneous with its effect; it must precede it. What accompanies something in time is its condition, not its cause. The priority of one thing over two in time (like the hand's movement over the ring's? Actually, he says the priority of one over two in time falls under the category of condition, not priority of condition over conditioned? The text says 'تقدم الواحد على الاثنين في الزمان هو من هذا الباب' meaning the priority of one over two in time is from the same category, i.e., of condition, not of cause). Then he clarifies the difference: the cause that is a condition may be simultaneous with the conditioned, but the efficient cause must precede its effect. He provides further examples: sound follows movement, but movement is not its efficient cause, only a condition; similarly, the sun's ray is not efficiently caused by the sun but is conditioned by it. The key point: a creator that simultaneously produces its effect does not exist. The term 'cause' ('illa) is ambiguous, conflating efficient cause and condition. Ibn Taymiyya thus refutes the idea of simultaneous causation, insisting on temporal priority for true causes.

Key Terms
العلة الفاعلة
الشرط
السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hand movement is the efficient cause of the ring movement.
B) The ring movement is the efficient cause of the hand movement.
C) They are two separate movements, one causing the other.
D) They are a single movement that encompasses both due to connection, not a causal relation.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critical insight is that true efficient causation requires temporal priority; simultaneity indicates only a condition, which fundamentally reshapes causal reasoning in Islamic thought.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Syllogisms as Crooked Scales
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes universal syllogisms as ambiguous scales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological objection to universal syllogisms and contrast divine revelation as the true balance.

Core Idea
Universal syllogisms are ambiguous scales; the only just balance is revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya levels a devastating epistemological critique against the logicians' reliance on universal syllogisms. He argues that when detached from innate rational knowledge of particulars, these syllogisms become ambiguous, mixing truth and falsehood. Using the Qur'anic imagery of cheating in weights and measures, he likens such syllogisms to crooked scales. In contrast, he presents the divine balance (al-mizan) that God sent with the prophets as the only just standard for knowledge and justice. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of criticism
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the logicians for turning away from 'innate rational knowledge of particulars' (al-ma'rifa al-fitriyya al-'aqliyya) toward 'universal syllogisms' (aqyisa kulliya). Understand that he is not rejecting all syllogisms, but the exclusive reliance on them.
2
Analyze the problem with universal syllogisms
He claims that when logicians formulate these syllogisms with abstract expressions, they become 'ambiguous' (mujmala) and 'encompass truth and falsehood'. This means they lack precision and can justify both valid and invalid conclusions, leading to misguidance.
Contrast this with the logicians' claim that universal syllogisms yield certainty.
3
Examine the analogy of the crooked scales
Ibn Taymiyya compares these syllogisms to 'crooked scales' (mawazin 'aila) used by those who cheat in trade (al-mutaffifin, referencing Surah 83). The scales are inherited without knowing if they are just, causing harm to intellects and religions, not just wealth.
This analogy emphasizes that the harm is epistemic and spiritual, not merely practical.
4
Contrast with the divine balance
He then cites Qur'an 42:17 and 57:25, where God sent down 'the Book and the Balance' (al-kitab wa al-mizan). This divine balance represents revealed truth and justice, the only reliable standard for weighing knowledge and actions.
The divine balance is not a syllogistic method but the revealed guidance itself.
5
Assess the epistemological conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya's critique implies that true knowledge must be grounded in innate human nature (fitra) and divine revelation, not in abstract syllogisms. Universal syllogisms, when used as a primary tool, are epistemically dangerous.
Consider how this aligns with his broader project of critiquing Greek-influenced logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في مواضعها، وإنما المقصود هنا أنهم إذا عدلوا عن المعرفة الفطرية العقلية للمعينات إلى أقيسة كلية وضعوا ألفاظها وصارت مجملةً تتناول حقاً وباطلاً : حصل بها من الضلال ما هو ضد المقصود من الموازين، وصارت هذه الموازين عائلة لا عادلة وكانوا فيها من المطففين الذين إذا اكتالوا على الناس يستوفون وإذا كالوهم أو وزنوهم يخسرون. وأين البخس في الأموال من البخس في العقول والأديان؟ مع أن أكثرهم لا يقصدون البخس، بل هم بمنزله من ورث موازين من أبيه يزن بها تارة له وتارة عليه، ولا يعرف أهي عادلة أم عائلة؟ والميزان الذي أنزله الله مع الكتاب حيث قال الله تعالى: الذي أنزل الكتاب بالحق والميزان وقال: لقد أرسلنا رسلنا بالبينات وأنزلنا معهم الكتاب والميزان ليقوم الناس بالقسط
These matters are expounded in their proper places. The intention here is only that when they turned away from the innate rational knowledge of particulars to universal syllogisms, they formulated their expressions and they became ambiguous, encompassing truth and falsehood. Thereby, they obtained from misguidance what is contrary to the purpose of the balances. These balances became crooked (ʿāila) not just (ʿādila), and they were among those who give short measure, 'who, when they take measure from people, take full measure, but when they give measure or weight to them, they give less' (Qur'an 83:2-3). And where is the fraud in wealth compared to the fraud in intellects and religions? Even though most of them do not intend fraud, rather they are like one who inherits scales from his father and weighs sometimes for himself and sometimes against himself, not knowing whether they are just or crooked. The balance that God sent down with the Book, where God Almighty said: 'He who sent down the Book with truth and the Balance' (Qur'an 42:17) and said: 'We have sent Our messengers with clear proofs and sent down with them the Book and the Balance so that people may uphold justice' (Qur'an 57:25).
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' method. He states that abandoning innate rational knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-fitriyya al-'aqliyya) of particulars in favor of universal syllogisms (aqyisa kulliya) leads to ambiguous formulations that mix truth and falsehood. Consequently, these syllogisms become 'crooked scales' (mawazin 'aila) instead of just ones (adila), likening the logicians to those who cheat in weights and measures (al-mutaffifin) from Surah al-Mutaffifin. The harm is greater because it affects intellects and religions, not just wealth. Most logicians do not intend fraud; they are like someone inherited scales from his father, not knowing if they are just. Ibn Taymiyya then contrasts these human-made scales with the divine balance (al-mizan) that God sent down along with the Book (al-kitab) as mentioned in Qur'an 42:17 and 57:25. The true balance is the revealed standard that enables people to uphold justice. This passage highlights Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological critique: reliable knowledge comes from innate human nature (fitra) and divine revelation, not from artificial, ambiguous syllogisms.

Key Terms
المعرفة الفطرية العقلية
أقيسة كلية
الميزان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the consequence of substituting innate rational knowledge with universal syllogisms?
A) It yields certain and precise knowledge.
B) It eliminates all error in reasoning.
C) It leads to ambiguous formulations that mix truth and falsehood, resulting in misguidance.
D) It is the only valid method for acquiring knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Universal syllogisms, when detached from innate knowledge and revelation, become ambiguous scales that cannot reliably distinguish truth from falsehood; the true balance is the divine revelation sent with the prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary argument that uses a universal syllogism (e.g., 'All intoxicants are harmful; all forms of gambling are intoxicating; therefore gambling is harmful.') and evaluate it from Ibn Taymiyya's perspective: Is the major premise ambiguous? Does it rely on unstated assumptions? Contrast it with a revealed ruling on gambling from the Qur'an.
?
Discuss an example from Islamic theology where a universal syllogism was used to prove a doctrine (e.g., the Ash'ari proof for the existence of God using the syllogism of temporality). Assess Ibn Taymiyya's critique: how might this syllogism be considered a 'crooked scale'? What would he propose instead?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Astronomical Certainty
Ibn Taymiyya argues astronomy is based on experience and transmitted reports, not certain demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological grounds on which Ibn Taymiyya critiques astronomy and evaluate whether astronomical claims meet the criteria for certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Astronomy, the crown of rational sciences, rests on fallible observations and solitary reports, not demonstrative proof.

The philosophers present astronomy as a rational science yielding certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this by dissecting its empirical and transmitted basis. In this passage, he systematically shows that astronomical inferences rely on fallible observations and non-mutawatir reports, undermining the claim to certainty. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate when a science truly provides demonstrative knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What philosophical claim about astronomy is Ibn Taymiyya attacking? He targets the idea that astronomy is a demonstrative rational science providing certain knowledge, and that it serves as a foundation for metaphysical doctrines like souls of spheres and intellects.
2
Follow the dissection of astronomical evidence
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if astronomy is a science, it falls under experiential matters (mujarrabat), where no demonstration can be brought against an opponent. Most people only know it through transmission, and tawatur (mass transmission) is rare; it is often passed from a few physicians or mathematicians.
3
Analyze the role of observation and inference
He gives specific examples: the senses see only motions of particular bodies (sun, moon, planets). Claims about spheres and their order are inferred from phenomena like eclipses—e.g., the moon eclipses others so it is below them; Saturn never eclipses so it is above. These inferences depend on observations (rasad) that are prone to error and not mutawatir; often they are based on solitary reports.
Notice the example of disagreement about precession: ancients vs. al-Ma'mun observatory differ on the rate, showing observational fallibility.
4
Examine the critique of metaphysical extrapolations
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes how philosophers move from astronomical observations to metaphysical claims: from the circular motion of the sphere they infer a soul moving it by will; from nine spheres they infer nine souls and ten intellects, culminating in emanation from the First Cause. He points out that this grand philosophical system is built on an unstable empirical foundation.
5
Evaluate the overall force of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya does not reject astronomy itself but its claim to demonstrative certainty. He uses the criteria of tawatur and fallibility of observation to show that astronomy cannot provide yaqīn (certainty). The critique is epistemologically rigorous, applying standards from hadith criticism to a scientific domain.

كون علم الهيئة من المجربات إن كان علماً. فإن هذه لا يقوم فيها برهان على المنازع. فليس عند القوم برهان على المنازع. وأيضاً فإن كون هذه الأجسام الطبيعية جُرّبت، وكونَ الحركات الفلكية جربت لا يعرفه أكثر الناس إلا بالنقل والتواتر في هذا قليل. وإنما الغاية أن تنتقل التجربة في ذلك عن بعض الأطباء، أو بعض أهل الحساب. وعلم الهيئة الذي هو أعظم علومهم الرياضية العقلية، الذي جَعَل أرسطو أهله هم تعرف أحكام أئمة الفلاسفة وعلمهم أصل الفلسفة فإنه منه تعرف عدد الأفلاك وحركاتها. وإلا علم الهيئة فالحس لا يرى إلا حركة الأجسام المعينة، فيرى الشمس متحركة، والقمر متحركاً، والكواكب متحركة . هذا الذي يعلم بالحس. وأما كون هذه مركوزة في أفلاك متحركة فهذا إنما يعلمونه من علم الهيئة والعلم بأن بعض الأفلاك فوق بعض علموه بكسوف الأسفل الأعلى، فلما رأوا القمر يكسف سائرها استدلوا بذلك على أنه تحت الجميع . ولما رأوا زُحَلَ لا يكسف شيئاً والجميع تكسفه استدلوا بذلك على أنه فوق الجميع . وكذلك كون الثوابت في الثامن ادعوا علمه بأنها لا تكسف شيئاً بل قد يكسفها غيرها . وأما أفلاكها فاستدلوا عليها بالحركات . والعلم بقدر حركاتها وبكسوف بعضها لبعض ونحو ذلك مداره على الأرصاد. وغايته أن بعض الناس رأى هذا فأخبر به غيره وليس هذا خبراً متواتراً، بل مداره على غالبه خبر واحد. ثم إن الأرصاد يقع فيها من الغلط ما هو معروف. ولهذا اختلفت الأرصاد أرصاد المتقدمين والمتأخرين في حركة الثوابت - هل تقطع درجة فلكية في كل مئة سنة، أو في ستة وستين وثلثي سنة كما زعمه أهلُ الرصد المأموني. ولما أخبرهم أهل الهيئة بحركة الفلك استدلوا بذلك على أن لها نَفْساً تحرّكه بالإرادة، لأن حركته دورية ليست طبيعية. واستدلوا على عدد النفوس التسعة بالأفلاك التسعة وعلى العقول العشرة بالأفلاك التسعة مع العقل العاشرة الذي هو أول صادر عن واجب الوجود. وجعلوا الفلك يتحرك للتشبه بالعلة الأولى ليخرج ما فيه من الأيون والأوضاع. وغاية الفلسفة التشبه بالعلة الأولى بحسب الإمكان. فهذا هو العلم الإلهي الذي هو عندهم الفلسفة.
That the science of astronomy is among the experiential matters if it is a science. For no demonstration can be established against the opponent in these [matters]. So the people [philosophers] have no demonstration against the opponent. Also, that these natural bodies have been experimented upon, and that the celestial motions have been experimented upon, most people do not know except by transmission and there is little tawatur in this. The utmost is that experience is transmitted from some physicians or some arithmeticians. And the science of astronomy, which is the greatest of their rational mathematical sciences, which Aristotle made its people the ones who know the judgments of the leading philosophers and their knowledge is the origin of philosophy — for from it the number of spheres and their motions are known. Otherwise, the science of astronomy: the senses see only the motion of particular bodies; they see the sun moving, the moon moving, the planets moving. This is what is known by sense. As for these being embedded in moving spheres, they only know this from the science of astronomy. And the knowledge that some spheres are above others, they learned by the eclipse of the lower by the higher. When they saw the moon eclipsing the others, they inferred that it is beneath all. And when they saw Saturn does not eclipse anything while all eclipse it, they inferred that it is above all. Similarly, the fixed stars being in the eighth [sphere], they claimed knowledge of it because they do not eclipse anything but others may eclipse them. As for their spheres, they inferred them from motions. Knowledge of the amount of their motions and their eclipses of each other and so on depends on observations. Its utmost is that some people saw this and informed others, and this is not a mutawatir report; rather most of it is based on a solitary report. Moreover, observations contain well-known errors. For this reason, the observations of the ancients and later ones differ concerning the motion of the fixed stars: whether they traverse a celestial degree every hundred years, or every sixty-six and two-thirds years as claimed by the al-Ma'mun observatory people. And when the astronomers informed them of the motion of the sphere, they inferred from that that it has a soul that moves it by will, because its motion is circular, not natural. They inferred the nine souls from the nine spheres, and the ten intellects from the nine spheres plus the tenth intellect which is the first emanation from the Necessary Existent. They made the sphere move in order to imitate the First Cause to bring out the material forms and positions that are in it. The goal of philosophy is to imitate the First Cause as much as possible. This is the divine science which according to them is philosophy.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the epistemological basis of astronomy (ilm al-hay'a), which the philosophers consider the pinnacle of rational sciences. Ibn Taymiyya argues that astronomy is not based on certain demonstration but on experiential knowledge and transmitted reports. He begins by stating that if astronomy is a science, it falls under experiential (mujarrabat) matters, where no demonstrative proof can be brought against an opponent. Most people only know of astronomical claims through transmission, and tawatur (mass transmission) is rare; it is often passed from a few physicians or mathematicians. He then details that sense perception only observes the motion of particular bodies (sun, moon, planets), not the underlying spheres. Claims about spheres and their order are inferred from phenomena like eclipses: the moon is inferred to be lower because it eclipses others; Saturn is higher because it never eclipses. Fixed stars are placed in the eighth sphere based on similar reasoning. These inferences rely on observations (rasad) which are prone to error and not mutawatir — often based on solitary reports. He gives an example of disagreement among observations regarding the precession of the equinoxes. He then criticizes the metaphysical inferences drawn from astronomy: the Philosophers infer a soul for the sphere because its motion is circular and voluntary; they infer nine souls from nine spheres, ten intellects, and the emanation of the universe. The ultimate goal of philosophy, the imitation of the First Cause, is thus built on an unstable empirical foundation. Ibn Taymiyya's point is that the grand metaphysical system is rooted in fallible, non-demonstrative knowledge, undermining its claim to certainty.

فلك
رصد
تواتر

Mutakallimun vs Philosophers: Whose Arguments are Truly Demonstrative?
Ibn Taymiyya defends mutakallimun, accusing philosophers of using sophistical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' classification of arguments and evaluate his claim that the mutakallimun's premises are more demonstrative than Aristotle's.

Core Idea
Philosophers' claim to demonstrative proof is undermined by their reliance on sophistical premises in theology.

In this passage from his refutation of the logicians, Ibn Taymiyya confronts a common accusation: that Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) only produce dialectical arguments (jadal), while philosophers possess true demonstration (burhan). He turns the tables, asserting that the mutakallimun actually use more certain demonstrative premises, while philosophers like Aristotle build their theology on corrupt sophistical foundations. This lesson unpacks Ibn Taymiyya's counter-critique and its implications for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' classification of qiyas
Note that philosophers divide qiyas (syllogism) into five types: demonstrative (burhani), rhetorical (khatabi), dialectical (jadali), poetic (shi'ri), and sophistical (sufistai). They place the mutakallimun's arguments under 'dialectical'.
2
Identify the philosophers' accusation
Philosophers like Ibn Sina and Ibn Rushd claim that the mutakallimun are 'people of jadal' (dialectic), not demonstration, and that their premises are dialectical, not certain.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's counter-accusation
Ibn Taymiyya concedes some mutakallimun are false but argues that when fairly compared in ilahiyyat (divine matters), the mutakallimun use certain demonstrative premises far more than Aristotle, who builds on 'sophistical premises of extreme corruption.'
4
Evaluate the evidence provided
Ibn Taymiyya points to Aristotle's works (Lambda and Theology) as evidence but does not detail them here. Consider whether his claim is supported by the examples he hints at.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And these mutakallimun (theologians) from the people of the religions whom the Ahl al-Sunna from the people of the religions declare innovators, their condemnation like the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila and what branches from them of all sects of kalam, for the philosophers say: the innovators are that these are people of dialectic (jadal), not possessors of demonstration (burhan), and they make themselves 'possessors of burhan' falsely. And they call these 'people of jadal', and consider their proofs from 'dialectical syllogisms' since they divided qiyas into five types: demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical, as we will discuss if God wills. And for this you find Ibn Sina, Ibn Rushd, and others of the philosophers making these 'people of jadal' and that their premises which they argue with are 'dialectical', not demonstrative, and they make themselves the people of burhan. And we do not dispute that much of what the mutakallimun speak is false, but if one speaks with fairness in their speech on divine matters and justice and looks at the speech of their first teacher Aristotle and his like in ilahiyyat and the speech of those who are based on the Muslims from the evil sects of the mutakallimun, like the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila for example, there is found between what these philosophers say and what these say of the sciences on which rational demonstration is based: differences that show the extreme ignorance of those in relation to these beyond what can be captured. And this speech of Aristotle exists and the speech of these exists. For these mutakallimun speak with certain demonstrative premises much more than those by far. And Aristotle mostly builds divine matters on sophistical premises of extreme corruption. And were this not the place to mention it, I would have mentioned his speech in the article 'Lambda' which is the last of his sciences in its wording, and likewise his speech in 'Theology'.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a critical defense of the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) against the philosophers' accusation that they are merely dialecticians. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that the philosophers classify qiyas into five types—demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical—and they place the theologians' arguments under dialectical, claiming their own are demonstrative. He cites Ibn Sina and Ibn Rushd as examples. While Ibn Taymiyya concedes that some mutakallimun's speech is false, he argues that when fairly compared, especially in divine matters, the mutakallimun use certain demonstrative premises far more than Aristotle, who relies on extremely corrupt sophistical premises. He contrasts the theologians' arguments, which are based on sound rational demonstration, with Aristotle's, which are built on sophistry. Thus, the philosophers' claim to be the sole possessors of demonstration is hypocritical.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Essential/Accidental Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the distinction between essential and accidental attributes is epistemological, not ontological, and apply it to a theological debate about divine attributes.

Core Idea
The essential-accidental distinction is about our knowledge, not reality itself.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a core tenet of Aristotelian logic: the division of attributes into essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi/gharadhi). Logicians claim that essential attributes constitute the definition of a thing and are known without a middle term, while accidental attributes require a syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this distinction is not grounded in the nature of things, but rather in the limitations of human knowledge. He then uses this insight to critique Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's further claim that the mental middle term is an external middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction being attacked
Understand the logicians' claim: essential attributes (like 'animal' for human) are part of the essence and known directly; accidental attributes (like 'laughing') require proof.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's reduction to epistemology
Note his examples: evenness is as much a part of the number 2 as numberness is; the difference is only what is clear to us.
3
Examine the analogy with perception
He compares it to seeing: we don't say unseen things are not there, only that we don't see them. Similarly, unknown attributes are not non-essential.
4
Extend the critique to al-Razi's middle term
Ibn Taymiyya accuses al-Razi of misconstruing the 'middle term' as an external entity, whereas it is just the proof (dalil).
5
Assess the force of the critique
Does Ibn Taymiyya successfully dissolve the logical distinction? What does this imply for defining things in theology?

And we have explained in our discussion on the corruption of their statement regarding the distinction between essential (dhati) and concomitant (lazim) that the distinction does not pertain to an attribute of the described in reality. Rather, the fact that 'evenness and oddness' are concomitant for the described even and odd in reality is like the concomitance of 'numberness' for number, and the concomitance of 'color-ness' for whiteness and blackness, and the concomitance of 'body-ness' for animal. But some concomitants are clear to some people that they are concomitant for all individuals, and some are hidden in their existence for some individuals. And this is a distinction that pertains to the human being, just as among things there are those we see and those we do not see, and likewise among things there are those we know and those we do not know. And that does not require that what he knows of attributes are parts of the essence (mahiyya) from which it is composed—if it were possible for the essence to be composed of attributes—and what he does not know are not parts of the essence. So how [can that be] when the composition of the essence existing externally is from attributes subsisting in it? And the claim that those attributes are constituent parts of it and are prior to it is in utmost corruption. The falseness of making [by] al-Razi and his likes—when they shared with the logicians in this error—claimed that the mental middle term is an external middle term. This is false, and I do not know the ancient logicians saying it, nor have I seen it in the words of Ibn Sina and his likes. Rather, what I have seen in his words is that 'the middle term' is the proof. So when al-Razi claimed what he claimed, it entailed for him a contradiction additional to the contradiction of the logicians. Al-Razi said: The proof that what is concomitant without a middle term is evident is that the essence, when it is such that it necessitates that concomitant. So when we conceive the essence, we must conceive from it that it, as it is, necessitates that concomitant; otherwise we would not have conceived the essence as it is. And when we conceive
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes. He argues that the distinction is not based on the actual nature of the described object, but on human knowledge. He uses the example of evenness and oddness, which the logicians claim are accidental because they require a middle term to be known for all numbers, while color-ness for black is essential. Ibn Taymiyya counters that all such attributes are equally necessary concomitants in reality; the difference is only in our awareness. He likens this to seeing and not seeing: some things are visible to us, some are not, but that does not mean the unseen things have a different ontological status. He then extends this critique to al-Razi's claim that the mental middle term (wasat dhihni) is an external middle term. Ibn Taymiyya insists that the middle term in logic is simply the proof (dalil) and not some separate entity. He accuses al-Razi of adding a further contradiction by making this claim. The passage ends with al-Razi's attempted proof that an immediate consequent is known directly from the essence, but Ibn Taymiyya's text cuts off; the implication is that al-Razi's proof fails because it assumes what it needs to prove.

Key Terms
الذاتي
اللازم
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the real basis for the distinction between essential and accidental attributes?
A) Their actual role in the essence
B) The clarity of their necessity to human knowers
C) Their frequency of occurrence in the world
D) Whether they are part of the definition

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya dissolves the ontological distinction between essential and accidental by showing it reduces to a difference in human awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a typical Kalam example: for a human, 'animality' is considered essential and 'laughing' accidental. Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, explain why this distinction is epistemological rather than ontological.

Al-Razi claimed that the mental middle term is an external middle term. Summarize Ibn Taymiyya's objection and evaluate whether al-Razi's position leads to a contradiction.

Sophistry and Fallacies

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation: No Middle Term for Necessary Concomitants
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the need for a middle term in necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that necessary concomitants follow the essence directly and require no middle term in reality.

Core Idea
All necessary concomitants follow the essence itself, not through a middle term.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that some properties require a middle term to link them to their subject. He argues that the division into 'concomitant for what it is' and 'concomitant for what it is not' is untenable. By examining the relationship between God's attributes, he shows that all are mutually concomitant and none serves as a middle term for another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logician's claim
The logician divides concomitance into two types: one that follows for what the thing is (no middle term) and one that follows for what it is not (requires middle term).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-claim
He asserts that no concomitant requires a middle term in reality; all follow the essence directly.
3
Analyze the divine attributes example
See how life, knowledge, power, etc., are all mutually concomitant (متلازمة) and none is a middle term for another. They all follow the divine essence.
4
Understand the implication
If some attribute were a middle term, it would imply that an attribute depends on another attribute for its existence, contradicting the mutual concomitance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : لا يفتقر شيء من اللوازم إلى وسط في نفس الأمر. فإن كونها ملزومة حقيقة معقولة، فقول القائل: «لما هي هي يقتضي أن اللزوم قسمان لزوم لما هي هي، ولزوم لغير ما هي هي.
فإن قيل : نعم عندي اللزوم نوعان فاللازم البين الذي لا يفتقر إلى وسط هو اللازم لما هي هي، واللازم الذي يفتقر إلى وسط هو اللازم لأجل الوسط، لا لما هي. قيل له الكلام في هذا الفرق فأنت تريد أن تقيم البرهان على أن ما كان لزومه بغير وسط كان بيناً لا يفتقر إلى دليل بل كان من الأوليات فمجرد تصور طرفي القضية كاف في العلم به والمنازع يقول لك : قد يكون اللازم بغير وسط كالعلة مع معلولها لا يُعلم إلا بدليل ووسط، فيكون وسطاً في العلم والذهن، لا وسطاً في الخارج. وأنت قد ادعيت أن كل ما كان مفتقراً إلى وسط في الذهن فإنه يفتقر إلى وسط في الوجود، وأن ما لا يفتقر إلى وسط في الوجود لا يفتقر إلى وسط في الذهن. وعلى هذا ادعيت إقامة البرهان.
ونحن نقول : مجرد التصور التام لما ادعيته يوجب العلم الضروري بفساده. لكن لا يفتقر ذلك إلى إقامة براهين. وهو أنك صادَرْتَ على المطلوب، فجعلت المطلوب مقدّمة في إثبات نفسه. فإنك لم يمكنك الاستدلال حتى أخذت مسلَّماً أن اللوازم تنقسم إلى البين الذي يلزم لما هي هي، وإلى اللازم بوسط الذي يفتقر إلى وسط في نفس الأمر. والمنازع لك يقول: لا نسلم افتقار شيء من اللوازم إلى وسط في نفس الأمر، بل جميع اللوازم يلزم الملزوم نفسه وإن كان بعض الملزومات شرطاً في البعض كما أن العلم مشروط بالحياة، والإرادة مشروطة بالعلم. فليس الشرط وسطاً ملزوماً للازم الثاني كما ادعيته، بل الذات مستلزمة للجميع، ومتى تحققت تحقق الجميع. ولا يتحقق شيء منها إلا مع الذات، وبعضها لا يتحقق إلا مع بعض بل قد تكون متلازمة كصفات الله تعالى، لا يتحقق شيء منها إلا مع الأخرى. فحياته لازمة لعلمه وقدرته ومشيئته، وكذلك قدرته ملازمة لعلمه وحياته. فهي كلها متلازمة. وهي أيضاً لازمة للذات، والذات لازمة لها وليس شيء من الصفات هو وسطاً للأخرى كما ادعيته من أن بعض اللوزام لازم للذات، وبعضها لازم اللازم، وبعضها لازم لازم اللازم بل جميعها لازمة للذات وهي أيضاً متلازمة.
وقد ذكرناه من أن بعض الصفات قد تكون شرطاً هو من جنس الصفات. وأما على المطلوب يكون خطأً جعله برهانياً. والذي ذكرناه من أن بعض الصفات قد تكون شرطاً هو من جنس الصفات. وأما على المطلوب خطأً جعله برهانياً، كالصفات اللازمة للموصوف مطلقاً، سواءً كان هو الخالق أو كان الملزوم مخلوقاً، كصفات الإنسان اللازمة كلها متلازمة فحيوانية الإنسان، وناطقيته، وضاحكيته متلازمة - لا يوجد واحد منها دون الآخر. وإن وُجدت حيوانية في غيره فتلك حيوانية غير حيوانيته. والفصول والخواص كلها متلازمة كالناطقية والضاحكية. وإذا كانت متلازمة وبعضها شرطاً في بعض امتنع أن يكون الشرط هو الوسط الذي به يثبت المشروط كما ادعوه لأن ذلك مستلزم أن يكون كل صفة لازمة وسطاً للأخرى، وأن يكون ليس شيء من الصفات اللازمة يلزم بنفسه، فيلزم الجمع بين النقيضين. فعلم أن كون إحدى الصفتين شرطاً في الأخرى لا يقتضي كونها وسطاً في الثبوت كما ادعوه. وإذا كان المنازع يمنع انقسام اللوازم إلا ما لا يلزم بنفسه وبوسط، وبرهانك مبني على أخذ هذا التقسيم مسلماً، كنت مُصادِراً على المطلوب، مثبتاً للشي بنفسه. فإن قلت : أنا أقيم هذا البرهان على من يسلم لي من المنطقيين أن من اللوازم ما يلزم بوسط وبغير وسط، قيل لك: فهذا أيضاً خطأ من وجوه. أحدها: أن القياس يكون حينئذٍ جَدَلياً، لابُرهانياً، وأنت جعلته بُرهانياً. الثاني: إن المنطق الذي هو ميزان العلوم عندكم لا يجوز أن تُجعل المقدمات المبينة له جَدَلية. فإنه حينئذ لا يكون علماً، بل يكون شيئاً اتفق بعض الناس على دعواه ونحن نعلم أن كثيراً من الناس يدعون صحة المنطق ولكن أي فائدة في هذا؟ الثالث أن يقال: ولو سلم لك هذا التقسيم فالبرهان خطأ، كما سيأتي بيانه. وخطأه دليل على فساد أصل التقسيم. وإذا كان أصل التقسيم خطأ كان البرهان باطلاً، وكان التقسيم باطلاً. وهو المطلوب.
The fourth station - the ninth aspect: Nothing among the necessary concomitants requires a middle term in reality. For their being concomitant is an intelligible reality, so the saying 'for what it is' necessitates that concomitance is two types: concomitance for what it is, and concomitance for what it is not. If it is said: Yes, according to me concomitance is two types: the evident concomitant that does not require a middle term is the concomitant for what it is, and the concomitant that requires a middle term is the concomitant for the sake of the middle term, not for what it is. It is said to him: The discussion is about this distinction, for you want to establish a proof that what has its concomitance without a middle term was evident, not requiring evidence, but rather is among primary principles, so mere conception of the two extremes of the proposition suffices for knowledge of it. But the disputant says to you: There may be a concomitant without a middle term, like the cause with its effect, not known except through evidence and a middle term, so it is a middle term in knowledge and the mind, not a middle term in existence. And you claim that everything that requires a middle term in the mind requires a middle term in existence, and that what does not require a middle term in existence does not require a middle term in the mind. On this basis you claim to have established a proof. And we say: Mere complete conception of what you claim necessitates necessary knowledge of its falsity. But that does not require establishing proofs. And that is that you have begged the question, so you made the desired conclusion a premise in proving itself. For you could not infer until you assumed as granted that the necessary concomitants are divided into the evident that follows for what it is, and the one with a middle term that requires a middle term in reality. And the disputant says to you: We do not accept that anything of the necessary concomitants requires a middle term in reality, but rather all necessary concomitants follow the thing itself, even if some of the things that are concomitant are conditions for others, just as knowledge is conditioned by life, and will is conditioned by knowledge. So the condition is not a middle term that is a consequence for the second concomitant as you claimed, but rather the essence is necessary for all, and when it is realized, all are realized. And nothing of them is realized except with the essence, and some are not realized except with others, but rather they may be mutually concomitant like the attributes of Allah, may He be exalted, none of them is realized except with the others. So His life is necessary for His knowledge, power, and will, and likewise His power is concomitant with His knowledge and life. So they are all mutually concomitant. And they are also necessary for the essence, and the essence is necessary for them, and no attribute is a middle term for another as you claimed—that some consequences are necessary for the essence, some are consequences of a consequence, and some are consequences of a consequence of a consequence—but rather all of them are necessary for the essence, and they are also mutually concomitant. And what we mentioned—that some attributes may be a condition—is of the same kind as attributes. As for being a proof, it is an error to consider it demonstrative. And what we mentioned—that some attributes may be a condition—is of the same kind as attributes. As for being a proof, it is an error to consider it demonstrative, like the attributes necessary for the described absolutely, whether He is the Creator or the thing that is concomitant is created, like the necessary attributes of a human are all mutually concomitant: humanity, rationality, and risibility are mutually concomitant—none of them exists without the other. And if humanity exists in another, that humanity is not his humanity. And the differentiae and properties are all mutually concomitant like rationality and risibility. And if they are mutually concomitant and some are conditions for others, it is impossible for the condition to be the middle term by which the conditioned is established as they claimed, because that would necessitate that every necessary attribute is a middle term for another, and that no attribute is necessary by itself, thus necessitating the combination of two contradictories. So it is known that one of the two attributes being a condition for the other does not require it to be a middle term in establishment as they claimed. And if the disputant denies the division of necessary concomitants except into what is necessary by itself and what is by a middle term, and your proof is based on taking this division as granted, you have begged the question, establishing the thing by itself. If you say: I establish this proof against those among the logicians who accept from me that some necessary concomitants follow by a middle term and some without a middle term, it is said to you: This is also erroneous from several aspects. One: that the syllogism would then be dialectical, not demonstrative, while you have made it demonstrative. Second: that logic, which is the standard of sciences according to you, does not allow that the premises that expound it be dialectical, for then it would not be science, but rather something that some people have agreed upon their claim, and we know that many people claim the validity of logic, but what benefit is there in this? Third: that it could be said: even if you are granted this division, the proof is erroneous, as will be explained later. And its error is evidence for the corruption of the basis of the division. And if the basis of the division is erroneous, then the proof is invalid, and the division is invalid. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that some necessary concomitants (اللوازم) require a middle term (وسط) in reality. The author begins by stating his position: no necessary concomitant requires a middle term in reality. He then addresses an objection: the opponent says there are two types of concomitance—one for what the thing is, and one for what it is not—and that only the latter requires a middle term. The opponent further claims that a concomitant that follows without a middle term is evident and known by mere conception. Ibn Taymiyya responds by pointing out that the opponent's argument is based on an assumed division of concomitants, which the disputant (the opponent in the debate) does not accept. The disputant argues that the concomitant follows the thing itself, and if a condition (like life for knowledge) is present, that condition is not a middle term but simply a necessary condition. Ibn Taymiyya then uses the example of God's attributes: all attributes are mutually concomitant (متلازمة) and none is a middle term for another. Similarly, for humans, attributes like humanity, rationality, and risibility are all mutually concomitant and cannot function as middle terms for each other because they are inseparable. The author then argues that if the opponent insists on the division and uses it as a premise, then he is guilty of begging the question (مصادرة على المطلوب). Furthermore, even if the opponent were granted the division, the argument would be dialectical, not demonstrative, because it relies on premises accepted only by some logicians. Since logic is supposed to be a demonstrative science, using dialectical premises undermines its scientific status. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya states that the proof itself is erroneous, and that error refutes the original division as well. All examples are drawn from Islamic theology (God's attributes) and human essential properties, staying within the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
اللوازم
وسط
لزوم
متلازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this part of the passage, why does the example of God's attributes disprove the need for a middle term?
A) Because God's attributes are unknowable.
B) Because the attributes are all mutually concomitant and follow from the essence, none being a middle term.
C) Because the attributes are separate from the essence.
D) Because there is no logical relation between attributes.

Key Takeaway
Necessary concomitants are entailed directly by the essence; the attempt to insert a middle term is based on an unproven division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the example of human attributes (humanity, rationality, risibility), explain why Ibn Taymiyya would say that none of these is a middle term for the other. What does this show about the logician's division?
?
Construct a short argument in syllogistic form that you think the logician might use to prove a middle term, and then identify where Ibn Taymiyya would object using the concept of begging the question.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
10 min
Begging the Question and the Dialectical Nature of the Logicians' Proof
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the fallacy of begging the question in the logicians' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of begging the question (مصادرة على المطلوب) in the logicians' argument and explain why their proof is dialectical, not demonstrative.

Core Idea
The logicians' proof for a middle term in concomitants assumes the very division they set out to prove.

Ibn Taymiyya now focuses on the logical flaw in the logician's argument. He charges them with begging the question: they take the disputed division of concomitants as a granted premise. Moreover, even if the division were accepted, their proof rests on dialectical premises, not demonstrative ones, undermining the scientific status of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the charge of مصادرة على المطلوب
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says the logician assumes the division without proof. The Arabic phrase is 'صادَرْتَ على المطلوب'.
2
Analyze the three aspects of error
Ibn Taymiyya lists three reasons why the logician's argument is flawed even if the division is granted: (1) it is dialectical, not demonstrative; (2) it uses disputed premises; (3) even if granted, the proof is erroneous.
3
Contrast demonstrative vs. dialectical
Understand the difference: demonstrative (برهاني) uses certain necessary premises; dialectical (جدلي) uses accepted but not certain premises.
4
See the consequence
If the proof is dialectical, then the claim that logic is the standard of sciences is undermined, because it relies on unproven assumptions.

المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : لا يفتقر شيء من اللوازم إلى وسط في نفس الأمر. فإن كونها ملزومة حقيقة معقولة، فقول القائل: «لما هي هي يقتضي أن اللزوم قسمان لزوم لما هي هي، ولزوم لغير ما هي هي.
فإن قيل : نعم عندي اللزوم نوعان فاللازم البين الذي لا يفتقر إلى وسط هو اللازم لما هي هي، واللازم الذي يفتقر إلى وسط هو اللازم لأجل الوسط، لا لما هي. قيل له الكلام في هذا الفرق فأنت تريد أن تقيم البرهان على أن ما كان لزومه بغير وسط كان بيناً لا يفتقر إلى دليل بل كان من الأوليات فمجرد تصور طرفي القضية كاف في العلم به والمنازع يقول لك : قد يكون اللازم بغير وسط كالعلة مع معلولها لا يُعلم إلا بدليل ووسط، فيكون وسطاً في العلم والذهن، لا وسطاً في الخارج. وأنت قد ادعيت أن كل ما كان مفتقراً إلى وسط في الذهن فإنه يفتقر إلى وسط في الوجود، وأن ما لا يفتقر إلى وسط في الوجود لا يفتقر إلى وسط في الذهن. وعلى هذا ادعيت إقامة البرهان.
ونحن نقول : مجرد التصور التام لما ادعيته يوجب العلم الضروري بفساده. لكن لا يفتقر ذلك إلى إقامة براهين. وهو أنك صادَرْتَ على المطلوب، فجعلت المطلوب مقدّمة في إثبات نفسه. فإنك لم يمكنك الاستدلال حتى أخذت مسلَّماً أن اللوازم تنقسم إلى البين الذي يلزم لما هي هي، وإلى اللازم بوسط الذي يفتقر إلى وسط في نفس الأمر. والمنازع لك يقول: لا نسلم افتقار شيء من اللوازم إلى وسط في نفس الأمر، بل جميع اللوازم يلزم الملزوم نفسه وإن كان بعض الملزومات شرطاً في البعض كما أن العلم مشروط بالحياة، والإرادة مشروطة بالعلم. فليس الشرط وسطاً ملزوماً للازم الثاني كما ادعيته، بل الذات مستلزمة للجميع، ومتى تحققت تحقق الجميع. ولا يتحقق شيء منها إلا مع الذات، وبعضها لا يتحقق إلا مع بعض بل قد تكون متلازمة كصفات الله تعالى، لا يتحقق شيء منها إلا مع الأخرى. فحياته لازمة لعلمه وقدرته ومشيئته، وكذلك قدرته ملازمة لعلمه وحياته. فهي كلها متلازمة. وهي أيضاً لازمة للذات، والذات لازمة لها وليس شيء من الصفات هو وسطاً للأخرى كما ادعيته من أن بعض اللوزام لازم للذات، وبعضها لازم اللازم، وبعضها لازم لازم اللازم بل جميعها لازمة للذات وهي أيضاً متلازمة.
وقد ذكرناه من أن بعض الصفات قد تكون شرطاً هو من جنس الصفات. وأما على المطلوب يكون خطأً جعله برهانياً. والذي ذكرناه من أن بعض الصفات قد تكون شرطاً هو من جنس الصفات. وأما على المطلوب خطأً جعله برهانياً، كالصفات اللازمة للموصوف مطلقاً، سواءً كان هو الخالق أو كان الملزوم مخلوقاً، كصفات الإنسان اللازمة كلها متلازمة فحيوانية الإنسان، وناطقيته، وضاحكيته متلازمة - لا يوجد واحد منها دون الآخر. وإن وُجدت حيوانية في غيره فتلك حيوانية غير حيوانيته. والفصول والخواص كلها متلازمة كالناطقية والضاحكية. وإذا كانت متلازمة وبعضها شرطاً في بعض امتنع أن يكون الشرط هو الوسط الذي به يثبت المشروط كما ادعوه لأن ذلك مستلزم أن يكون كل صفة لازمة وسطاً للأخرى، وأن يكون ليس شيء من الصفات اللازمة يلزم بنفسه، فيلزم الجمع بين النقيضين. فعلم أن كون إحدى الصفتين شرطاً في الأخرى لا يقتضي كونها وسطاً في الثبوت كما ادعوه. وإذا كان المنازع يمنع انقسام اللوازم إلا ما لا يلزم بنفسه وبوسط، وبرهانك مبني على أخذ هذا التقسيم مسلماً، كنت مُصادِراً على المطلوب، مثبتاً للشي بنفسه. فإن قلت : أنا أقيم هذا البرهان على من يسلم لي من المنطقيين أن من اللوازم ما يلزم بوسط وبغير وسط، قيل لك: فهذا أيضاً خطأ من وجوه. أحدها: أن القياس يكون حينئذٍ جَدَلياً، لابُرهانياً، وأنت جعلته بُرهانياً. الثاني: إن المنطق الذي هو ميزان العلوم عندكم لا يجوز أن تُجعل المقدمات المبينة له جَدَلية. فإنه حينئذ لا يكون علماً، بل يكون شيئاً اتفق بعض الناس على دعواه ونحن نعلم أن كثيراً من الناس يدعون صحة المنطق ولكن أي فائدة في هذا؟ الثالث أن يقال: ولو سلم لك هذا التقسيم فالبرهان خطأ، كما سيأتي بيانه. وخطأه دليل على فساد أصل التقسيم. وإذا كان أصل التقسيم خطأ كان البرهان باطلاً، وكان التقسيم باطلاً. وهو المطلوب.
The fourth station - the ninth aspect: Nothing among the necessary concomitants requires a middle term in reality. For their being concomitant is an intelligible reality, so the saying 'for what it is' necessitates that concomitance is two types: concomitance for what it is, and concomitance for what it is not. If it is said: Yes, according to me concomitance is two types: the evident concomitant that does not require a middle term is the concomitant for what it is, and the concomitant that requires a middle term is the concomitant for the sake of the middle term, not for what it is. It is said to him: The discussion is about this distinction, for you want to establish a proof that what has its concomitance without a middle term was evident, not requiring evidence, but rather is among primary principles, so mere conception of the two extremes of the proposition suffices for knowledge of it. But the disputant says to you: There may be a concomitant without a middle term, like the cause with its effect, not known except through evidence and a middle term, so it is a middle term in knowledge and the mind, not a middle term in existence. And you claim that everything that requires a middle term in the mind requires a middle term in existence, and that what does not require a middle term in existence does not require a middle term in the mind. On this basis you claim to have established a proof. And we say: Mere complete conception of what you claim necessitates necessary knowledge of its falsity. But that does not require establishing proofs. And that is that you have begged the question, so you made the desired conclusion a premise in proving itself. For you could not infer until you assumed as granted that the necessary concomitants are divided into the evident that follows for what it is, and the one with a middle term that requires a middle term in reality. And the disputant says to you: We do not accept that anything of the necessary concomitants requires a middle term in reality, but rather all necessary concomitants follow the thing itself, even if some of the things that are concomitant are conditions for others, just as knowledge is conditioned by life, and will is conditioned by knowledge. So the condition is not a middle term that is a consequence for the second concomitant as you claimed, but rather the essence is necessary for all, and when it is realized, all are realized. And nothing of them is realized except with the essence, and some are not realized except with others, but rather they may be mutually concomitant like the attributes of Allah, may He be exalted, none of them is realized except with the others. So His life is necessary for His knowledge, power, and will, and likewise His power is concomitant with His knowledge and life. So they are all mutually concomitant. And they are also necessary for the essence, and the essence is necessary for them, and no attribute is a middle term for another as you claimed—that some consequences are necessary for the essence, some are consequences of a consequence, and some are consequences of a consequence of a consequence—but rather all of them are necessary for the essence, and they are also mutually concomitant. And what we mentioned—that some attributes may be a condition—is of the same kind as attributes. As for being a proof, it is an error to consider it demonstrative. And what we mentioned—that some attributes may be a condition—is of the same kind as attributes. As for being a proof, it is an error to consider it demonstrative, like the attributes necessary for the described absolutely, whether He is the Creator or the thing that is concomitant is created, like the necessary attributes of a human are all mutually concomitant: humanity, rationality, and risibility are mutually concomitant—none of them exists without the other. And if humanity exists in another, that humanity is not his humanity. And the differentiae and properties are all mutually concomitant like rationality and risibility. And if they are mutually concomitant and some are conditions for others, it is impossible for the condition to be the middle term by which the conditioned is established as they claimed, because that would necessitate that every necessary attribute is a middle term for another, and that no attribute is necessary by itself, thus necessitating the combination of two contradictories. So it is known that one of the two attributes being a condition for the other does not require it to be a middle term in establishment as they claimed. And if the disputant denies the division of necessary concomitants except into what is necessary by itself and what is by a middle term, and your proof is based on taking this division as granted, you have begged the question, establishing the thing by itself. If you say: I establish this proof against those among the logicians who accept from me that some necessary concomitants follow by a middle term and some without a middle term, it is said to you: This is also erroneous from several aspects. One: that the syllogism would then be dialectical, not demonstrative, while you have made it demonstrative. Second: that logic, which is the standard of sciences according to you, does not allow that the premises that expound it be dialectical, for then it would not be science, but rather something that some people have agreed upon their claim, and we know that many people claim the validity of logic, but what benefit is there in this? Third: that it could be said: even if you are granted this division, the proof is erroneous, as will be explained later. And its error is evidence for the corruption of the basis of the division. And if the basis of the division is erroneous, then the proof is invalid, and the division is invalid. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that some necessary concomitants (اللوازم) require a middle term (وسط) in reality. The author begins by stating his position: no necessary concomitant requires a middle term in reality. He then addresses an objection: the opponent says there are two types of concomitance—one for what the thing is, and one for what it is not—and that only the latter requires a middle term. The opponent further claims that a concomitant that follows without a middle term is evident and known by mere conception. Ibn Taymiyya responds by pointing out that the opponent's argument is based on an assumed division of concomitants, which the disputant (the opponent in the debate) does not accept. The disputant argues that the concomitant follows the thing itself, and if a condition (like life for knowledge) is present, that condition is not a middle term but simply a necessary condition. Ibn Taymiyya then uses the example of God's attributes: all attributes are mutually concomitant (متلازمة) and none is a middle term for another. Similarly, for humans, attributes like humanity, rationality, and risibility are all mutually concomitant and cannot function as middle terms for each other because they are inseparable. The author then argues that if the opponent insists on the division and uses it as a premise, then he is guilty of begging the question (مصادرة على المطلوب). Furthermore, even if the opponent were granted the division, the argument would be dialectical, not demonstrative, because it relies on premises accepted only by some logicians. Since logic is supposed to be a demonstrative science, using dialectical premises undermines its scientific status. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya states that the proof itself is erroneous, and that error refutes the original division as well. All examples are drawn from Islamic theology (God's attributes) and human essential properties, staying within the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
مصادرة على المطلوب
جدلي
برهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ibn Taymiyya lists three reasons why the logician's argument is erroneous after granting the division. Which of the following is one of those reasons?
A) The syllogism is in the second figure.
B) The syllogism is dialectical, not demonstrative.
C) The premises are synthetic.
D) The conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
A demonstrative proof cannot rely on premises that are themselves disputed; otherwise, it loses its scientific certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify where in the passage Ibn Taymiyya says the syllogism becomes جدلي (dialectical) rather than برهاني (demonstrative). Explain why that is a problem for the logician.
?
Think of a theological argument about divine attributes that might commit the fallacy of begging the question. How would Ibn Taymiyya expose it?

Ibn Taymiyya's Sixth Refutation: Circularity in the Logicians' Condition
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' attempt to avoid regress by requiring a condition, showing it leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the vicious circularity (dawr) in the logicians' condition that knowledge of the cause requires the presence of the effect's conception.

Core Idea
A cause that depends on its effect for causality is a vicious circle by universal agreement.

The logicians tried to avoid an infinite regress by adding a condition: knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of the effect only if the 'conception of the effect' is present. Ibn Taymiyya exposes a deeper problem: this condition makes the cause dependent on the effect, creating a logical circle. In this lesson, you will dissect his argument and learn to recognise circularity in causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' condition
Find the statement of the condition in the Arabic: 'Knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect except with another condition... the presence of the conception of the effect.'
The condition is meant to block the regress.
2
Understand what the condition presupposes
The condition itself speaks of 'the effect' – this effect is the very thing that the cause is supposed to produce. So the condition already involves the effect.
3
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues: if the cause, in its being a cause, requires the effect (or its conception), then it is not an independent cause. The effect would have to precede the causality of the cause, while causality of the cause precedes the effect – a contradiction.
This is the core of the circularity.
4
Confirm the fallacy is vicious circularity
A vicious circle occurs when A depends on B and B depends on A. Here, the cause's causality depends on the effect's conception, and the effect depends on the cause's causality. This is impossible by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أن قوله :: العلم بماهية العلة لا يقتضي العلم بالمعلول إلا بشرط آخر، وهو كون الموصوف علة للازمه حضور تصور المعلول فيقال: المعلول عندكم هنا هو اللازم الأول الذي كان الموصوف علة [٤١١][له] في ذاته ، لم يفتقر في كونه علة له إلى المعلول. فإن العلة لو افتقرت في كونها علة إلى المعلول لم تكن وحدها علة وللزم الدور، وللزم تقدم المعلول على علية علته، وعلية علته متقدمة على ذاته. وهذا دور ممتنع باتفاق العقلاء.
It is his statement: 'Knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect except with another condition, which is that the described thing is a cause for its necessary concomitant [with] the presence of the conception of the effect.' It is said: The effect for you here is the first necessary concomitant for which the described thing is a cause in its essence; it did not need, in its being a cause for it, the effect. For if the cause, in its being a cause, needed the effect, it would not be alone a cause, and circularity would follow, and the priority of the effect over the causality of its cause would follow, and the causality of its cause is prior to its essence. This is an impossible circularity by consensus of the rational people.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's sixth argument, which directly addresses the logicians' response to the regress problem. The logicians had introduced a condition: knowledge of the cause does not give knowledge of the effect unless there is also a 'presence of the conception of the effect' (hudur tasawwur al-ma'lul). This condition is meant to block the regress. Ibn Taymiyya points out the circularity: the effect here is the first necessary concomitant that the described thing (the cause) is a cause for in its essence. But if the cause, in its being a cause, requires the effect (or its conception), then the cause would not be an independent cause; it would depend on the effect. This leads to a vicious circle (dawr) where the effect must precede the causality of the cause, yet the causality of the cause must precede the effect. Such circularity is impossible by universal agreement. In Islamic theology, this parallels the argument that if God's act of creation depended on the existence of creation, God would not be the absolute creator. Similarly, in logic, a cause must be prior to its effect in causality.

Key Terms
دور
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does Ibn Taymiyya identify in the logicians' condition that knowledge of the cause requires the presence of the effect's conception?
A) Infinite regress.
B) Circular reasoning (dawr).
C) Ambiguity.
D) False analogy.

Key Takeaway
A condition that makes a cause dependent on its own effect for causality creates a vicious circularity that is impossible by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, construct a brief argument showing that the condition 'knowledge of the cause requires the presence of the effect's conception' implies a vicious circle. Start by stating the condition, then show how it makes the cause dependent on the effect, and conclude that circularity follows.

Ibn Taymiyya's Reductio: The Absurdity of Knowing All Concomitants from One Thing
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim about immediate necessary concomitants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio argument against the claim that conceiving a thing entails knowing all its immediate necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
If knowing one thing required knowing all its necessary concomitants, then knowing any creature would mean knowing God and all creation.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a core claim of the logicians: that immediate necessary concomitants are known without proof. He shows that if this claim were universally applied, it leads to an infinite regress and the absurd conclusion that knowing any single thing requires knowing everything. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya is refuting: that conceiving a thing entails conceiving its immediate necessary concomitant (lāzim bi-ghayr wasaṭ) without need for proof.
The logicians held that immediate concomitants are known directly from the essence.
2
Extend the claim to a chain
If conceiving A entails conceiving its immediate concomitant B, and B itself has an immediate concomitant C, then conceiving A would require conceiving C as well, and so on ad infinitum.
This leads to an infinite regress of required conceptions.
3
Apply to the Creator-creature relation
Consider any creature: it necessarily entails the Creator (since it is contingent). The Creator necessarily entails His knowledge, will, and all His actions. Thus, knowing one creature would require knowing God and all of creation.
This is the specific absurdity Ibn Taymiyya highlights.
4
Identify the absurd conclusion
The conclusion — that knowing any creature implies knowing everything — is obviously false. Therefore, the original claim must be false.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الخامس
قوله يستلزم أن يقال : لو كان ما ذكرته صحيحاً للزم أن من عقل شيئاً عقل لوازم الشيء شيئاً تصور جميعها. فإنه إذا عقل الملزوم وجب أن يعقل لازمه الذي بغير وسط. ثم عقل ذلك اللازم يوجب عقل الآخر، وهلم جراً. فيلزم أن يكون قد عقل اللوازم جميعها . وهذا كما أنه يبطل حجته فهو يبطل أصل قولهم : إن اللازم بغير وسط لا يفتقر إلى دليل في حق أحد .
فإنه لو كان كذلك لكان من تصور شيئاً تصور جميع لوازمه. ويلزم أن من عرف ما يعلمه الله، فيعلم كل شيء، لأن علمه سبحانه من لوازم ذاته . بل يلزم من علم شيئاً من مخلوقاته علم كل شيء، فإن المخلوق مستلزم للخالق، والخالق مستلزم لعلمه بكل شيء بل وهو مستلزم لمشيئته ومشيئته مستلزمة لجميع ما خلقه، فإنه ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن . فلو كان من علم الملزوم لا بد أن يعلم لازمه لزم أن من علم شيئاً فقد علم كل شيء
وفساد هذا بين، مع أن فساد هذه الدعوى أظهر من أن يحتاج إلى دليل، لكن إذا عرفت شناعة لوازمها كان أدل على قبحها وفسادها.
The fifth path: His statement entails that it should be said: If what you mentioned were correct, then it would follow that whoever conceives a thing conceives all its necessary concomitants. For if he conceives the thing that has a necessary concomitant, he must conceive its immediate necessary concomitant. Then conceiving that necessary concomitant requires conceiving another, and so on. So it would follow that he would have conceived all necessary concomitants. And just as this invalidates his argument, it invalidates the foundation of their statement: that the immediate necessary concomitant does not require a proof for anyone. For if it were so, then whoever conceives a thing would conceive all its necessary concomitants. And it would follow that whoever knows what God knows would know everything, because His knowledge is among the necessary concomitants of His essence. Rather, it would follow that whoever knows a thing from His creatures would know everything, because the creature necessarily entails the Creator, and the Creator necessarily entails His knowledge of everything, and He necessarily entails His will, and His will necessarily entails all that He has created—for what He wills is, and what He does not will is not. So if whoever knows the thing that has a necessary concomitant must know its necessary concomitant, then it would follow that whoever knows a thing knows everything. The corruption of this is clear, and the corruption of this claim is more evident than to require a proof, but when the repulsiveness of its implications is known, it is more indicative of its ugliness and corruption.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' position that immediate necessary concomitants are known without proof. He argues that if their claim were true, then conceiving the essence of a thing would require conceiving all its necessary concomitants in an infinite chain, which is impossible. Moreover, he shows that this leads to the absurd conclusion that knowing any creature would necessitate knowing the Creator and all of creation, because the creature entails the Creator, who entails all His attributes and actions. Therefore, the claim is false and its implications are clearly corrupt.

Key Terms
لازم
ملزوم
بغير وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya identify as the absurd consequence of the logicians' claim about immediate necessary concomitants?
A) That one would need an infinite number of proofs for each thing.
B) That knowing any creature would require knowing the Creator and all creation.
C) That immediate concomitants cannot be known at all.
D) That logic is useless for obtaining knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's reductio shows that the claim of immediate knowledge of all necessary concomitants leads to absurdity, thus undermining the logical doctrine.
