Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Identity of Mental and External Middle Terms
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the identity of mental and external middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the identity of the mental and external middle terms and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Mental middle term requires two relata; external middle term requires only one — they cannot be identical.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of Aristotelian logic by challenging a key claim: that the middle term used in mental reasoning is the same as the external cause that produces an effect. He shows that if we examine the conditions for knowledge of necessary concomitance (luzūm), we find a mismatch that invalidates the identity claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The logicians claim that the mental middle term (al-wasaṭ al-dhihnī) is identical to the external middle term (al-wasaṭ al-khārijī). Locate this claim in the Arabic.
The first sentence of the passage states this claim is false.
2
Find the condition for knowledge of necessary concomitance
Ibn Taymiyya notes that according to the logicians, knowledge of a necessary concomitance (luzūm) requires conceiving both the cause (malzūm) and the effect (lāzim). This is a mental requirement.
The passage says: 'knowledge of necessary concomitance is dependent on the conception of the effect and the cause.'
3
Compare with external necessary concomitance
In external reality, the effect depends only on the cause, not on our conception of both. The necessary concomitance in the external world requires only the cause.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'external necessary concomitance only necessitates one, not two.'
4
Recognize the mismatch
Since the mental requirement involves two relata (cause and effect) and the external requirement involves only one (the cause), the mental middle term cannot be identical to the external middle term.
This is the core logical inconsistency Ibn Taymiyya exposes.
5
Conclude the invalidity
Therefore, the claim that the mental middle term is the external is false. Ibn Taymiyya ends with an invalidation of that statement.
The passage explicitly says 'your statement is invalidated.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، باطل
إبطال القول وأنتم جعلتم ما كان يفتقر لزومه إلى وسط افتقر العلم به إلى وسط، وما لزم بلا وسط عُلم بلا وسط . فإذا جعلتم العلم بلزومه موقوفاً على تصور المعلول والعلة والملزوم واللازم جميعاً وقلتم لا يُعلم اللزوم إلا بعد هذين التصورين، واللزوم الخارجي موقوف على العلة وحدها لا على الاثنين بطل قولكم : «إن الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي». فإن هذا يتوقف العلم بلزومه على تصورين ولزومه في الخارج إنما لازم لواحد، لا لاثنين.
The fourth station, the ninth aspect: The mental middle term is the external is false. Invalidation of the statement: You made what required a middle term for its necessary concomitance require a middle term for its knowledge, and what is necessary without a middle term is known without a middle term. So if you made knowledge of a necessary concomitance dependent on the conception of the effect and the cause and the thing that has the necessary concomitant and the necessary concomitant all together, and you said that the necessary concomitance is not known except after these two conceptions, while the external necessary concomitance depends on the cause alone, not on both, then your statement 'the mental middle term is the external' is invalidated. For knowledge of its necessary concomitance depends on two conceptions, while its necessary concomitance in the external only necessitates one, not two.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the mental middle term (the concept used in reasoning) is identical to the external middle term (the actual cause in reality). Ibn Taymiyya points out that according to the logicians themselves, knowledge of necessary concomitance requires conceiving both the cause and the effect, whereas in external reality, the effect depends only on the cause. Therefore, the mental and external are not the same, refuting their claim.

Refuting the Relational Account of Causality
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the reply that causality is relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the claim that causality is a relational matter and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows causality cannot be relational, because either it is privative (self-defeating) or positive (cause alone suffices).

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a common reply from philosophers who argue that causality is a relational matter requiring both cause and effect. He breaks the reply down by considering causality as either privative or positive, and shows both options fail. You will learn how Ibn Taymiyya uses a dichotomy to dismantle the relational account of causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that Ibn Taymiyya labels as 'invalid speech'. It says: the essence alone is insufficient for causality because causality is relational, requiring both relata.
2
Extract the dichotomous classification
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya splits causality into two types: privative (ʿadamī) and positive (wujūdī). He will test the reply against both.
This is a classic argument technique: exhaust the possibilities.
3
Analyze the privative case
If causality is privative (i.e., not a real attribute), then the reply that it requires two relata is meaningless because there is no real relation. The reply is 'invalid' by its own standard.
Privative matters do not have real relations.
4
Analyze the positive case
If causality is a positive attribute, then the cause alone must necessitate the effect. Otherwise, it would not be a full cause but only part of a cause (which would require another component). Since the effect comes after the cause, the cause alone suffices.
Ibn Taymiyya argues that necessity is inherent in the cause itself.
5
Reconstruct the overall argument
Combine both cases: the reply fails in both scenarios, therefore the claim that causality is relational is false. The cause alone is sufficient for causality and effect.
This is a valid disjunctive argument (modus tollendo ponens).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق السابع
قولك في الجواب : إن الماهية وحدها لا تكفي في حصول العلية، لأن العلية أمر إضافي، والأمور الإضافية لا يكفي في حصولها الشيء الواحد، بل لا بد من حصول كلا المضافين كلام باطل، سواء كانت العلية أمراً عدمياً أو وجودياً فإنها إن كانت عدمية والعليـــة بطل هذا الكلام. وإن كان أمراً وجودياً فمعلوم أن العلة وحدها هي الموجبة للمعلول، وإلا لم تكن علة له بل جُزء علة . والمعلول لا يكون إلا بعد العلة . فإذا كانت وحدها موجبة، وهو يوجد إلا بوجود العلة، عُلم أنها وحدها توجب المعلول والعلية.
The seventh path: Your statement in the answer: 'The essence alone is not sufficient for the attainment of causality, because causality is a relational matter, and relational matters are not attained by a single thing, but both relata must be attained' is invalid speech, whether causality is a privative or positive matter. For if it is privative, then this speech is invalid. And if it is a positive matter, then it is known that the cause alone necessitates the effect, otherwise it would not be a cause but a part of a cause. And the effect only comes after the cause. So if it alone necessitates, and it exists only with the existence of the cause, then it is known that it alone necessitates the effect and causality.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a common reply that causality is an 'amr iḍāfī' (relational matter) requiring both relata, so the cause alone is not sufficient. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this reply fails whether causality is privative or positive. If positive, the cause alone necessitates the effect; if privative, the reply is self-defeating. He concludes that the cause alone suffices to produce the effect, and causality is not dependent on the effect's existence.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya’s Refinement: Mental vs. External Relational Matters
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that while relational matters require two conceptions in the mind, in external reality a single cause suffices, using God as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between mental and external relational matters and explain why the logicians' claim fails in external reality.

Core Idea
Relational matters need two concepts in the mind, but in external reality a single cause can suffice.

The logicians assert that 'relational matters (al-umur al-idafiyya) are not attained by a single thing'. Ibn Taymiyya accepts this for mental conception but rejects it for external existence. This crucial distinction rescues theology from the error of requiring multiple eternal entities, using God's essence as the ultimate example of a single cause that necessitates all relations.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
The logicians claim: 'Relational matters are not attained by a single thing.' This means that for a relation to exist, two relata are required. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asking: do you mean in the mind or in external reality?
The ambiguity is the key: the claim is true in one domain, false in another.
2
Identify the ambiguity
Ibn Taymiyya forces a disambiguation: 'by a single thing' could mean in mental conception or in external existence. The logicians equivocate between these two.
Equivocation (ishtirak lafzi) is a common fallacy; here Ibn Taymiyya exposes it.
3
Analyse the mental domain
In the mind, the claim is true. Causality, for example, is not conceived except by conceiving both cause and effect. A mental conception of a relation requires two concepts.
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says: 'this is correct'.
4
Analyse the external domain
In external reality, the claim is false. A single entity, such as God's essence, can alone necessitate all relational attributes (e.g., being Creator, Lord, Agent). No second distinct entity is needed for these relations to exist externally.
Agreement of Muslims and philosophers is cited: God's essence alone suffices.
5
Evaluate the critique
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians by showing their claim holds only in one sense. The external sense is 'necessarily false by the agreement of rational people'. His distinction is a powerful tool for clarifying theological discussions.
This is a classic critical refinement: preserving the useful part of a doctrine while rejecting its overreach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثامن
قولك : الأمور الإضافية لا يكفي في حصولها الشيء الواحد) تعني به في الذهن، أو في الخارج؟ فإن هذا الموضع فيه التباس أن عنيت أنها لا تتصور إلا بتصور شيئين ، ولا ريب فهذا صحيح، أن العلية لا تتصور إلا بتصور العلة والمعلول، فحصولها في التصور الذهني مشروط بحصول تصور المضافين في الذهن وإن عنيت أنها لا توجد في الخارج إلا بالشيئين فهذا باطل بالضرورة واتفاق العقلاء فإن ذات الرب وحدها مستلزمة لكل ما يتصف به من الأمور الثبوتية والإضافية. وهذا متفق عليه بين المسلمين والفلاسفة، وسائر الناس . فإن كونه خالقاً للعالم، ورباً، وفاعلاً ، هو الذي يسمونه «علة» و «مؤثراً». والناس متنازعون في «الخلق» هل هو أمر عدمي، وهو نفس
The eighth path: Your statement: 'Relational matters are not attained by a single thing'—do you mean in the mind or in the external? For this place has confusion. If you mean that they are not conceived except by conceiving two things, then no doubt this is correct, that causality is not conceived except by conceiving the cause and the effect, so its attainment in mental conception is conditioned by the attainment of the conception of the two relata. But if you mean that they do not exist in the external except with two things, then this is necessarily false by the agreement of rational people, for the essence of the Lord alone necessitates all that He is described with from positive and relational matters. And this is agreed upon between Muslims and philosophers and all people. For His being the Creator of the world, Lord, and Agent is what they call 'cause' and 'efficient'. And people differ concerning 'creation' whether it is a privative matter or the same...
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification of the logicians' claim about relational matters. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between mental and external existence. In the mind, causality requires the conception of both cause and effect. However, in external reality, a single cause (like God) suffices to produce all effects and relations. He cites the unanimous agreement of Muslims and philosophers that God's essence alone necessitates all His attributes and actions, including creation. Thus, the logicians' claim that relational matters require two things externally is false.

تصور ذهني
خارج

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's refinement teaches that principles governing mental conception need not apply to external reality, a vital tool for avoiding theological errors.

Refuting the Claim That Well-Known Propositions Are Not Certain
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' classification of mashhurat and defends rational ethical knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that well-known ethical propositions are certain and evaluate his refutation of al-Amidi's accident objection.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya defends rational ethical knowledge against Ash'ari skepticism by redefining mashhurat and critiquing the accident argument.

In this passage from his critique of the logicians, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a key claim: that well-known propositions (mashhurat) are not certain. He argues that they are actually certain ethical truths known by reason, and he refutes the philosophical arguments against rational moral values. This lesson will walk you through his arguments and their implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find what the logicians say about mashhurat. They claim these propositions are not certain (yaqiniyyat) but are accepted by convention.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition
Ibn Taymiyya redefines mashhurat as scientific propositions such as 'justice is good', which he considers certain and known by reason.
He lists examples: justice, knowledge, truthfulness, etc.
3
Follow the historical claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the denial of rational moral values is an innovation that arose in the time of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari during debates on destiny.
He lists many scholars from various madhhabs who affirm rational goodness and badness.
4
Analyze the refutation of the accident argument
Al-Amidi argued that goodness and badness are accidents and cannot be known because an accident cannot subsist in another accident. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this, saying that attributes of accidents (like intense blackness) are predicated of the substance, not requiring accident-in-accident.
He gives examples: 'this is intense blackness', 'this is fast motion'.
5
Assess the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that mashhurat are certain ethical truths, and the deniers have no valid proof. This upholds rational ethical knowledge.

The second type: It is said that by 'well-known propositions' they mean all scientific propositions, such as that justice is good and injustice is bad, knowledge is good and ignorance is bad, truthfulness is good and lying is bad, benevolence is good, and similar matters about which people disagree whether their goodness and badness are known by reason or not. Those who affirm and those who deny rational goodness and badness. And most sects affirm rational goodness and badness, but they do not affirm it as the deniers of destiny from the Mu'tazila and others affirm it. Rather, those who say that things are good and bad by reason from Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, from the early and later generations, like those who say it from the four schools and others, they affirm destiny and the attributes and similar things in which the Mu'tazila oppose Ahl al-Sunna, and they say along with this the affirmation of rational goodness and badness. This is the saying of the Hanafis, and they also transmitted it from Abu Hanifa himself. And it is the saying of many of the Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis; such as Abu al-Hasan al-Tamimi, and Abu al-Khattab, and others from the Imams of Ahmad's followers; and like Abu Ali ibn Abi Hurayra, and Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Shashi, and others from the Shafi'is; and likewise from the followers of Malik; and likewise the people of hadith, like Abu Nasr al-Sijzi, and Abu al-Qasim Sa'd ibn Ali al-Zanjani, and others. Rather, these mentioned that the denial of that is among the innovations that occurred in Islam in the time of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari when he debated the Mu'tazila about destiny by the way of Jahm ibn Safwan and his like from those who occurred in the time of jabr, so he needed this denial. They said: Otherwise, the absolute denial of rational goodness and badness was not said by any of the ancestors of the nation nor its imams. Rather, what is taken from the speech of the imams and ancestors in explaining legal rulings, and clarifying Allah's wisdom in His creation and command, and clarifying what in what Allah commanded of goodness known by reason and what in His prohibitions of badness known by reason, contradicts the saying of the deniers. And the deniers have no proof for the denial at all. Abu al-Hasan al-Amidi examined the proofs they mentioned, and showed that most of them are corrupt. And he mentioned a proof weaker than others, which is that goodness and badness are accidents, and an accident does not subsist in an accident. For affirming this does not require the subsistence of an accident in an accident, just as accidents are described with attributes, and all of that subsists in the described substance, so we say 'this is intense blackness' and 'this is fast and slow motion'. And they admit that the action being a quality of perfection or deficiency, or suitable to the agent or repugnant to it, may be known by reason. And these are attributes of the action, and they subsist in the described. And some people think that goodness and badness are necessary attributes of the described, and that the meaning of 'good' is an essential attribute of it, but it is not so; rather, a thing may be good in one state and bad in another, just as it may be beneficial and loved in one state and harmful and hated in another. And goodness and nature of badness...
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that 'well-known propositions' (mashhurat) are not certain (yaqiniyyat). He begins by defining what the logicians mean by mashhurat: they are universally accepted propositions like 'justice is good' and 'injustice is bad'. Ibn Taymiyya points out that there is disagreement whether these are known by reason or not. He then states that most sects affirm rational goodness and badness, including many from Ahl al-Sunna, and provides a long list of scholars from various madhhabs (Hanafis, Malikis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and hadith scholars) who affirm this. He then makes a historical claim: the denial of rational goodness and badness is an innovation (bid'a) that arose in the time of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari when he debated the Mu'tazila about destiny. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that no early scholar or imam denied it absolutely. Next, he refutes the arguments of the deniers, focusing on the proof mentioned by al-Amidi that goodness and badness are accidents and an accident cannot subsist in another accident. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is invalid because accidents can be described with attributes (e.g., intense blackness, fast motion) without postulating an accident subsisting in another accident; rather, the attribute is predicated of the substance. He also clarifies that goodness and badness are not necessary attributes but can vary depending on circumstances, just as benefit and harm can vary. The passage is a defense of rational ethical knowledge against the Ash'ari position.

Refutation by Internal Contradiction: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Ibn Sina on Innate Moral Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Ibn Sina's claim of innate moral ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a refutation by internal contradiction (ilzam al-khalaf) and apply it to a classical Islamic debate.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya defeats Ibn Sina by using Ibn Sina's own words against him.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on Ibn Sina's claim that the soul's powers cannot perceive moral truths without cultural training. Rather than attacking from outside, Ibn Taymiyya shows that Ibn Sina himself, in another part of his work, affirms that intellectual and moral pleasures are recognized by the rational soul. This is a powerful logical move: refutation by internal contradiction. You will learn to recognize this technique and see how it works in Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find Ibn Sina's claim in the passage: a person created with complete intellect but no culture would not judge moral matters. This implies that mashhurat are not perceived by the soul's powers.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's initial denial
Ibn Taymiyya denies this, arguing that the intellect itself knows that justice and truth benefit the soul and that injustice harms it, even without external instruction.
3
Analyze the anticipated objection
The objection: people sometimes take pleasure in evil acts. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between bodily pleasure and intellectual/moral displeasure: the body may enjoy, but the heart and intellect are pained by injustice.
4
Find the internal contradiction
Ibn Taymiyya quotes Ibn Sina's own words from the Ishārāt, where Ibn Sina says that non-sensory pleasures (like dominance, dignity) can outweigh sensory ones. This shows that the soul does perceive moral values.
This directly contradicts Ibn Sina's claim that moral perception requires culture.
5
Assess the force of the refutation
The contradiction is effective because Ibn Sina cannot deny his own words. The refutation shows that his own premises lead to the opposite conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع : قوله : «لو توهّم الإنسان بنفسه أنه خُلق دفعة تام العقل ولم يسمع أدباً ولم يُطع انفعالاً نفسانياً ولا خُلُقاً لم يقض في أمثاله بشيء». هذا ممنوع . بل إذا كان تام العقل علم أن العلم والعدل والصدق ينفعه وتصلح به نفسه وتلتذ، وأن الكذب والظلم يضره ويُفْسِد نفسه ويؤلمها، ولو قُدّر أنه لا يُعْلِمُ به أحد غير علم العقل بأنه إذا ظَلَم أبغضه الناس وعادوه وغير علمه بأن الله يعاقبه. فإن قيل : الإنسان يلتذ بما يراه قبيحاً كما قد يلتذ بما يأخذه ظلماً فيأكله ويشربه قيل : وإن التذْ بَدَنُه فإن قلبه وعقله لا يلتذ بذلك، بل يلتذ إذا عَدَل. وإن قدر أنه يلتذ به لذة حاضرة فإنه يتألم لقُبح عاقبته عنده . وإذا لم يتألم فلغيبة عقله عن إدراك المؤلم، كما قد يحصل للسكران وغيره من أمور تؤلمه ولا يتألم بها لغيبة عقله عن إدراكها . وهذا مما قد ذكره ابن سينا نفسه، فقال في نمط البهجة والسعادة: إنه قد يسبق إلى الأوهام العامية أن اللَّذَّاتِ القوية المستعلية هي الحسّية، وأن بذلك ما عداها لَذَات ضعيفة وكلها خيالات غير حقيقية وقد يمكن أن يُنَبَّه من جملتهم مَنْ له تمييز ما فيقال له : أليس ألذ ما يصفونه من التذاذ هذا القبيل هو المنكوحات والمطعومات وأمور تجري مجراها؟ وأنتم تعلمون أن الإنسان بالغلبة المتمكن من غلبة ما - ولو في أمر خسيس كالشَّطْرَنج والنَّرْد - قد يعرض له منكوح ومطعوم فيرفضه لما يعتاضه من لذة الغلبة الوهمية. وقد يعرض منكوح ومطعوم للطالب العفة والرياسة مع صحة جسمه في صحبة حَشَمه فينفض اليد منهما مراعاة للحشمة، فيكون مراعاةُ الحِشمة آثَرُ والدُّ لا محالة هناك من المنكوح والمطعوم . وإذا عرض للكرام من الناس الالتذاذ بإنعام يصيبون موضِعَه أَثَرُوه على الالتذاذ بالإيثار.
The seventh type: His statement: 'If a man supposes himself to have been created suddenly with complete intellect, having heard no discipline nor obeyed any psychical passion nor character, he would not judge anything about his likes.' This is denied. Rather, if he has complete intellect, he knows that knowledge, justice, and truth benefit him, improve his soul, and give pleasure, and that lying and injustice harm him, corrupt his soul, and pain it, even if it were supposed that no one informs him except the intellect's knowledge that if he does injustice, people will hate and oppose him, and his knowledge that God punishes him. If it is said: Man takes pleasure in what he sees as evil, just as he may enjoy taking something unjustly and eating and drinking it, it is said: Even if his body takes pleasure, his heart and intellect do not take pleasure in that; rather, he takes pleasure when he is just. And if it is supposed that he takes immediate pleasure, he feels pain at the evil consequence in his view. And if he does not feel pain, it is due to the absence of his intellect from perceiving the painful, just as occurs with the drunkard and others: things cause him pain but he does not feel pain due to his intellect being absent from perceiving them. And this is something that Ibn Sina himself mentioned, for he said in the chapter on joy and happiness: 'It often precedes in common imaginations that the strong, prevailing pleasures are the sensory ones, and that besides them, pleasures are weak and all are unreal fantasies. And it may be possible to alert among them one with some discernment, and it is said to him: Is not the most pleasurable they describe of this kind of pleasure the things of marriage, food, and the like? And you know that a man, through the dominance of being able to dominate something—even in a base matter like chess and backgammon—may be presented with a wife and food but refuses them for the imaginary pleasure of dominance he substitutes. And a man who seeks chastity and leadership, with a healthy body in the company of his servants, may be presented with a wife and food but disposes of them out of regard for dignity, so that regarding dignity is more effective than the wife and food. And when noble people are presented with the pleasure of benefiting, they prefer it over the pleasure of giving preference.'
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of Ibn Sina's claim that well-known propositions are not perceived by the soul's powers. Ibn Taymiyya quotes Ibn Sina's hypothetical: if a person were created fully rational with no cultural training, he would not judge matters. Ibn Taymiyya denies this; he argues that such a person would still know that knowledge, justice, and truth are beneficial and pleasurable, and that injustice and lying are harmful, even without external instruction, because the intellect itself provides this knowledge. He then addresses an objection: people sometimes take pleasure in evil acts. He distinguishes between bodily pleasure and intellectual/moral displeasure; the heart and intellect are pained by injustice. If one feels no pain, it is due to a dulled intellect, like a drunkard. Finally, he cites Ibn Sina's own words from the chapter on joy and happiness (from the Ishārāt) where Ibn Sina argues that non-sensory pleasures (like dominance or dignity) can outweigh sensory ones. Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show that Ibn Sina himself admits that intellectual and moral pleasures are recognized by the rational soul, contradicting his earlier denial. This is a classic example of refutation by internal contradiction.

Key Terms
المشهورات
قوى النفس
الالتذاذ
الحشمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does a person with complete intellect know that justice is beneficial even without culture?
A) Because he has been taught by society
B) Because his intellect itself perceives it
C) Because his body feels pleasure
D) Because he fears punishment

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Ibn Sina by showing an internal contradiction: Ibn Sina's own admission of non-sensory pleasures contradicts his denial of innate moral knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a few sentences, explain how Ibn Taymiyya's citation of Ibn Sina's own words constitutes a refutation by internal contradiction. Identify the two conflicting propositions.
?
Task 2: Find another example in Islamic thought where a scholar refutes an opponent by pointing out an inconsistency in their own statements (e.g., al-Ghazālī refuting the philosophers). Briefly describe the contradiction.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
8 min
Syllogisms Cannot Know Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogisms cannot yield knowledge of particular events, using prophecies as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of syllogistic reasoning's limitation to universals and distinguish it from prophetic knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
Syllogisms yield only universals; prophetic knowledge is about unrepeatable particulars.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the claim that logical syllogisms can produce knowledge of particular realities. He uses concrete prophecies from authentic hadith to show that only revelation, not reason, can inform us about specific events. This lesson walks through his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Ibn Taymiyya targets the notion that syllogisms (qiyas) and demonstrative proofs (burhan) can yield knowledge of particular matters. He asserts that they only produce universal propositions.
The logicians' position is that burhan yields certain knowledge of any subject, including particulars.
2
Examine the first prophetic example: the Turks
The Prophet described the Turks with specific physical attributes—small eyes, flat noses, red cheeks—and predicted they would be fought before the Hour. This description was given centuries before the Turks appeared.
Ibn Taymiyya asks: Could a syllogism have generated such a detailed prediction?
3
Examine the second example: the fire of Hijaz
The Prophet foretold a fire from the land of Hijaz that would illuminate the necks of camels in Busra. This fire occurred in 655 AH and was witnessed by many.
The fire's specific effects (burning stone, not cooking meat) are concrete details beyond syllogistic deduction.
4
Understand the logical limitation
Syllogisms operate on universal premises and yield universal conclusions. They cannot produce knowledge of a specific individual, nation, or event unless those are subsumed under a universal rule—but the rule itself does not give the particular instance without additional premises.
Ibn Taymiyya's point is that prophetic knowledge involves unrepeatable, concrete realities that transcend any universal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن معرفته بقياسهم - لا البرهاني ولا غيره. فإن أقيستهم لا تفيد إلا أموراً كلية، وهذا أمور خاصة . وكذلك أخبر عما كان وعما سيكون بعده من الحوادث المعينة، حتى أخبر عن فتنة الترك التتر بما ثبت في الصحيحين عنه من غير وجه أنه قال: «لا تقوم الساعة حتى تُقاتِلوا التركَ صِغارَ الأعين ذُلْفَ الأنوف حُمر الخدود ينتعلون الشَّعْرَ كأن وجوههم المَجَاةُ المُطرَقَةُ» فهل يتصور أن قياسهم وبرهانهم يدل على آدمي معين أو أمة معينة، فضلاً عن أن يوصف بهذه الصفات قبل ظهورهم بنحو سبع مئة سنة ؟ وكذلك قوله الثابت في الصحيح: لا تقوم الساعة حتى تخرج نارٌ من أرض الحجاز تُضيء لها أعناق الإبل بيُضرى وهذه النار قد خرجت قبل مجيء أكثر الكفار من أرض الحجاز إلى بغداد سنة خمس وخمسين وست مئة . وقد تواتر عن أهل بصرى أنهم رأوا ببصرى أعناق الإبل من ضوء تلك النار . وخبرُ هذه النار مشهور متواتر بعد أن خرجت بجبال الحجاز، وكانت تحرق الحجر ولا تُنْضِج اللحم، وفزع لها الناس فزعاً شديداً. فهل يدل قياس برهاني أو غير برهاني على هذا الأمر المعيَّن ويخبر به المُخْبِر قبل حدوثه بأكثر من ست مئة وخمسين سنة؟ فإن هذا أخبر به النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم في آخر أيام النبوة، وأبو هريرة إنما أسلم عام خيبر سنة سبع من الهجرة، وصحب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أقل من أربع سنين . فأخبارُه كلها متأخرة . وكذلك سائر ما أخبر به من الأمور الماضية والمستقبلة والأمور الحاضرة مما يعلمون هم أنه يمتنع أن يُعرف ذلك بالقياس البرهاني وغيره. فإن ذاك إنما يدل على أمر مطلق كلي، لا على شيء معين .
It can be known by their syllogism—neither demonstrative nor other. For their syllogisms only yield universal matters, and these are particular matters. Likewise, he informed about what was and what will be after him of specific events, until he informed about the fitna of the Turkish Tatars by what is established in the Sahihayn from him through multiple chains that he said: 'The Hour will not be established until you fight the Turks—small eyes, flat noses, red cheeks, they will wear hair [sandals], their faces like hammered shields.' So can it be imagined that their syllogism and demonstration indicate a specific individual or a specific nation, let alone that they be described by these attributes about seven hundred years before their appearance? Likewise, his statement established in the Sahih: 'The Hour will not be established until a fire comes out from the land of Hijaz, illuminating the necks of camels in Busra.' And this fire had come out before the coming of most of the disbelievers from the land of Hijaz to Baghdad in the year 655 H. And it was widely transmitted from the people of Busra that they saw in Busra the necks of camels from the light of that fire. And the report of this fire became famous and widely transmitted after it came out in the mountains of Hijaz, and it was burning stone but not cooking meat, and people were terribly frightened by it. So does a demonstrative or non-demonstrative syllogism indicate this specific event and inform the informant before its occurrence by more than six hundred and fifty years? For this was informed by the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family, in the last days of prophethood, and Abu Hurayra only embraced Islam in the year of Khaybar, the seventh year of Hijra, and accompanied the Prophet for less than four years. So all his reports are later. And likewise all that he informed of past and future matters and present matters, which they know are impossible to be known by demonstrative or other syllogism. For that only indicates an absolute universal, not something specific.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that syllogisms (especially demonstrative ones) can produce knowledge of particular realities. Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogisms only yield universal conclusions, whereas prophetic knowledge concerns specific events—like the description of the Turks and the fire of Hijaz. He uses two clear examples from authentic hadith: the prophecy about fighting the Turks (with specific physical descriptions) and the prophecy about a fire in Hijaz that would illuminate the necks of camels in Busra. These events occurred centuries later, and no syllogism could have predicted them. He further notes that Abu Hurayra's reports are later but still depend on revelation, not reason. The core argument is that logical reasoning (whether burhani or otherwise) only gives universal, abstract knowledge, not the concrete particulars that only revelation can provide. This is a classic Ibn Taymiyyan critique against the primacy of Greek logic in epistemology.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Refers to logical syllogism as used by Greek logicians, which Ibn Taymiyya argues yields only universal propositions. [Note: In mainstream logic, qiyas is a valid inference; Ibn Taymiyya restricts its applicability to particulars.]
برهان (burhan): Demonstrative proof producing certain knowledge, but here Ibn Taymiyya denies it can reach particular existents. [Note: Standard logicians consider burhan the highest form of proof; Ibn Taymiyya critiques its scope.]
أمور كلية (umur kulliyya): Universal matters, the only type of knowledge that syllogisms can produce according to Ibn Taymiyya. [Note: This is standard usage in logic, but Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes its limitation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya claim that syllogisms cannot produce knowledge of the prophecy about the Turks?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because syllogisms only yield universal knowledge, not particular descriptions.
C) Because syllogisms are only valid in mathematics.
D) Because the Turks are not a logical subject.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogisms are limited to universal knowledge and cannot produce knowledge of particular events, which only revelation can provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the hadith about the fire of Hijaz, explain in two sentences why Ibn Taymiyya says this event cannot be known by syllogism.
?
Find another prophetic example (e.g., the prediction of the defeat of the Persians and Romans in Surah al-Rum) and explain why it is a particular that syllogism cannot produce.
